<body><font face="Courier"><span style="font-size:13pt"><font color="#000000">LETTER THE FIRST<br>
<br>
Madam,<br>
<br>
I sit down to give you an undeniable proof of my considering your<br>
desires as indispensable orders. Ungracious then as the task may be, I<br>
shall recall to view those scandalous stages of my life, out of which I<br>
emerged, at length, to the enjoyment of every blessing in the power of<br>
love, health and fortune to bestow; whilst yet in the flower of youth,<br>
and not too late to employ the leisure afforded me by great ease and<br>
affluence, to cultivate an understanding, naturally not a despicable<br>
one, and which had, even amidst the whirl of loose pleasures I had been<br>
tossed in, exerted more observation on the characters and manners of<br>
the world than what is common to those of my unhappy profession, who,<br>
looking on all though or reflection as their capital enemy, keep it at<br>
as great a distance as they can, or destroy it without mercy.<br>
<br>
Hating, as I mortally do, all long unnecessary prefaces, I shall give<br>
you good quarter in this, and use no farther apology, than to prepare<br>
you for seeing the loose part of my life, written with the same liberty<br>
that I led it.<br>
<br>
Truth! stark, naked truth, is the word; and I will not so much as take<br>
the pains to bestow the strip of a gauze wrapper on it, but paint<br>
situations such as they actually rose to me in nature, careless of<br>
violating those laws of decency that were never made for such<br>
unreserved intimacies as ours; and you have too much sense, too much<br>
knowledge of the originals, to sniff prudishly and out of character at<br>
the pictures of them. The greatest men, those of the first and most<br>
leading taste, will not scruple adorning their private closets with<br>
nudities, though, in compliance with vulgar prejudices, they may not<br>
think them decent decorations of the staircase, or salon.<br>
<br>
This, and enough, premised, I go souse into my personal history. My<br>
maiden name was Frances Hill. I was born at a small village near<br>
Liverpool, in Lancashire, of parents extremely poor, and, I piously<br>
believe, extremely honest.<br>
<br>
My father, who had received a maim on his limbs, that disabled him from<br>
following the more laborious branches of country drudgery, got, by<br>
making nets, a scanty subsistence, which was not much enlarged by my<br>
mother&rsquo;s keeping a little day-school for the girls in her neighborhood.<br>
They had had several children; but none lived to any age except myself,<br>
who had received from nature a constitution perfectly healthy.<br>
<br>
My education, till past fourteen, was no better than very vulgar:<br>
reading, or rather spelling, an illegible scrawl, and a little ordinary<br>
plain work, composed the whole system of it; and then all my foundation<br>
in virtue was no other than a total ignorance of vice, and the shy<br>
timidity general to our sex, in the tender age of life, when objects<br>
alarm or frighten more by their novelty than anything else. But then,<br>
this is a fear too often cured at the expense of innocence, when Miss,<br>
by degrees, begins no longer to look on a man as a creature of prey<br>
that will eat her.<br>
<br>
My poor mother had divided her time so entirely between her scholars<br>
and her little domestic cares, that she had spared very little to my<br>
instruction, having, from her own innocence from all ill, no hint or<br>
thought of guarding me against any.<br>
<br>
I was now entering on my fifteenth year, when the worst of ills befell<br>
me in the loss of my fond, tender parents, who were both carried off by<br>
the small-pox, within a few days of each other; my father dying first,<br>
and thereby by hastening the death of my mother: so that I was now left<br>
an unhappy friendless orphan (for my father&rsquo;s coming to settle there,<br>
was accidental, he being originally a Kentisrman). That cruel distemper<br>
which had proved so fatal to them, had indeed seized me, but with such<br>
mild and favourable symptoms, that I was presently out of danger, and<br>
what then I did not know the value of, was entirely unmarked I skip<br>
over here an account of the natural grief and affliction which I felt<br>
on this melancholy occasion. A little time, and the giddiness of that<br>
age, dissipated too soon my reflections on that irreparable loss; but<br>
nothing contributed more to reconcile me to it, than the notions that<br>
were immediately put into my head, of going to London, and looking out<br>
for a service, in which I was promised all assistance and advice from<br>
one Esther Davis, a young woman that had beer down to see her friends,<br>
and who, after the stay of a few days, was returned to her place.<br>
<br>
As I had now nobody left alive in the village, who had concern enough<br>
about what should become of me, to start any objections to this scheme,<br>
and the woman who took care of me after my parents&rsquo; death, rather<br>
encouraged me to pursue it, I soon came to a resolution of making this<br>
launch into the wide world, by repairing to London, in order to seek my<br>
fortune, a phrase which, by the bye, has ruined more adventurers of<br>
both sexes, from the country, than ever it made or advanced.<br>
<br>
Nor did Esther Davis a little comfort and inspirit me to venture with<br>
her, by piquing my childish curiosity with the fine sights that were to<br>
be seen in London: the Tombs, the Lions, the King, the Royal Family,<br>
the fine Plays and Operas, and, in short, all the diversions which fell<br>
within her sphere of life to come at; the detail of all which perfectly<br>
turned the little head of me.<br>
<br>
Nor can I remember, without laughing, the innocent admiration, not<br>
without a spice of envy, with which we poor girls, whose church-going<br>
clothes did not rise above dowlas shifts and stuff gowns, beplaced with<br>
silver: all which we imagined grew in London, and entered for a great<br>
deal into my determination of trying to come in for my share of them.<br>
<br>
The idea however of having the company of a towns-woman with her, was<br>
the trivial, and all the motives that engaged Esther to take charge of<br>
me during my journey to town, where she told me, after the manner and<br>
style, &ldquo;as how several maids out of the country had made themselves and<br>
all their kind for ever: that by preserving their virtue, some had<br>
taken so with their masters, that they had married them, and kept them<br>
coaches, and lived vastly grand and happy; and some, may-hap, came to<br>
be Duchesses; luck was all, and why not I, as well as another?&rdquo;; with<br>
other almanacs to this purpose, which set me a tip-toe to begin this<br>
promising journey, and to leave a place which, though my native one,<br>
contained no relations that I had reason to regret, and was grown<br>
insupportable to me, from the change of the tenderest usage into a cold<br>
air of charity, with which I was entertained, even at the only friend&rsquo;s<br>
house that I had the least expectation of care and protection from. She<br>
was, however, so just to me, as to manage the turning into money the<br>
little matters that remained to me after the debts and burial charges<br>
were allowed for, and, at my departure, put my whole fortune into my<br>
hands; which consisted of a very slender wardrobe, packed up in a very<br>
portable box, and eight guineas, with seventeen shillings in silver,<br>
stowed in a spring-pouch, which was a greater treasure than I ever had<br>
seen together, and which I could not conceive there was a possibility<br>
of running out; and indeed, I was so entirely taken up with the joy of<br>
seeing myself mistress of such an immence sum, that I gave very little<br>
attention to a world of good advice which was given me with it.<br>
<br>
Places, then, being taken for Esther and me in the Chester waggon, I<br>
pass over a very immaterial scene of leave-taking, at which I droped a<br>
few tears betwixt grief and joy; and, for the same reasons of<br>
insignificance, skip over all that happened to me on the road, such as<br>
the waggoner&rsquo;s looking liquorish on me, the schemes laid for me by some<br>
of the passengers, which were defeated by the valiance of my guardian<br>
Esther; who, to do her justice, took a motherly care of me, at the same<br>
time that she taxed me for the protection by making me bear all<br>
travelling charges, which I defrayed with the unmost cheerfulness, and<br>
thought myself much obliged to her into the bargain.<br>
<br>
She took indeed great care that we were not overrated, or imposed on,<br>
as well as of managing as frugally as possible; expensiveness was not<br>
her vice.<br>
<br>
It was pretty late in a summer evening when we reached the town, in our<br>
slow conveyance, though drawn by six at length. As we passed through<br>
the greatest streets that led to our inn, the noise, of the coaches,<br>
the hurry, the crowds of foot passengers, in short, the new scenery of<br>
the shops and houses, at once pleased and amazed me.<br>
<br>
But guess at my mortification and surprise when we came to the inn, and<br>
our things were landed and delivered to us, when my fellow traveller<br>
and protectress, Esther Davis, who had used me with the utmost<br>
tenderness during the journey, and prepared me by no preceedings signs<br>
for the stunning blow I was to receive, when I say, my only dependence<br>
and friend, in this strange place, all of a sudden assumed a strange<br>
and cool air towards me, as if she dreaded my becoming a burden to her.<br>
<br>
Instead, then, of proffering me the continuance of her assistance and<br>
good offices, which I relied upon, and never more wanted, she thought<br>
herself, it seems, abundantly acquitted of her engagements to me, by<br>
having brought me safe to my journey&rsquo;s end, and seeing nothing in her<br>
procedure towards me but what natural and in order, began to embrace me<br>
by the way of taking leave, whilst I was so confounded, so struck, that<br>
I had not spirit or sense enough so much as to mention my hopes or<br>
expectations from her experience, and knowledge of the place she had<br>
brought me to.<br>
<br>
Whilst I stood thus stupid and mute, which she doubtless attributed to<br>
nothing more than a concern at parting, this idea procured me perhaps a<br>
slight alleviation of it, in the following harangue: &ldquo;That now we were<br>
got safe to London, and that she was obliged to go to her place, she<br>
advised me by all means to get into one as soon as possible; that I<br>
need not fear getting one; there were more places than parish-churches;<br>
that she advised me to go to an intelligence office; that if she heard<br>
of any thing stirring, she would find me out and let me know; that in<br>
the meantime, I should take a private lodging, and acquaint her where<br>
to send to me; that she wished me good luck, and hoped I should always<br>
have the grace to keep myself honest, and not bringing a disgrace on my<br>
parentage.&rdquo; With this; she took her leave of me, and left me, as it<br>
were, on my own hands, full as lightly as I had been put into hers.<br>
<br>
Left thus alone, absolutely destitute and friendless I began then to<br>
feel most bitterly the severity of this separation, the scene of which<br>
had passed in a little room in the inn; and no sooner was her back<br>
turned, but the affliction I felt at my helpless strange circumstances,<br>
burst out into a flood of tears, which infinitely relieved the<br>
oppression of my heart; though I still remained stupified, and most<br>
perfectly perplexed how to dispose of myself.<br>
<br>
One of the drawers coming in, added yet more to my uncertainty, by<br>
asking me, in a short way, if I called for anything? to which I replied<br>
innocently: &ldquo;No.&rdquo; But I wished him to tell me where I might get a<br>
lodging for that night. He said he would go and speak to his mistress,<br>
who accordingly came, and told me drily, without entering in the least<br>
into the distress she saw me in, that I might have a bed for a<br>
shilling, and that, as she supposed I had some friends in town (there I<br>
fetched a deep sigh in vain!), I might provide for myself in the<br>
morning.<br>
<br>
It is incredible what trifling consolations the human mind will seize<br>
in its greatest afflictions. The assurance of nothing more than a bed<br>
to lie on that night, calmed my agonies; and being ashamed to acquaint<br>
the mistress of the inn that I had no friends to apply to in town, I<br>
proposed to myself to proceed, the very next morning, to an<br>
intelligence office, to which I was furnished with written directions<br>
on the back of a ballad, Esther had given me. There I counted on<br>
getting information of any place that such a country girl as I might be<br>
fit for, and where I could get into any sort of being, before my little<br>
stock should be consumed; and as to a character, Esther had often<br>
repeated to me, that I might depend on her managing me one; nor,<br>
however affected I was at her leaving me thus, did I entirely cease to<br>
rely on her, as I began to think, good-naturedly, that her procedure<br>
was all in course, and that is was only my ignorance of life that had<br>
made me take it in the light I at first did.<br>
<br>
Accordingly, the next morning I dressed myself as clean and as neat as<br>
my rustic wardrobe would permit me; and having left my box, with<br>
special recommendation, with the landlady, I ventured out by myself,<br>
and without any more difficulty than can be supposed of a young country<br>
girl, barely fifteen, and to whom every sign or shop was a gazing trap,<br>
I got to the wished for intelligence office.<br>
<br>
It was kept by an elderly woman, who sat at the receipt of custom, with<br>
a book before her in great form and order, and several scrolls made<br>
out, of directions for places.<br>
<br>
I made up then to this important personage, without lifting up my eyes<br>
or observing any of the people round me, who were attending there on<br>
the same errand as myself, and dropping her curtsies nine deep, just<br>
made a shift to stammer out my business to her.<br>
<br>
Madam heard me out, with all the gravity and brow of a petty minister<br>
of State, and seeing at one glance over my figure what I was, made me<br>
no answer, but to ask me the preliminary shilling, on receipt of which<br>
she told me places for women too slight built for hard work: but that<br>
she would look over her book, and see what was to be done for me,<br>
desiring me to stay a little, till she had dispatched some other<br>
customers.<br>
<br>
On this I drew back a little, most heartily mortified at a declaration<br>
which carried with it a killing uncertainly, that my circumstances<br>
could not well endure.<br>
<br>
Presently, assuming more courage, and seeking some diversion from my<br>
uneasy thoughts, I ventured to lift up my head a little, and sent my<br>
eyes on a course round the room, where they met full tilt with those of<br>
a lady (for such my extreme innocence pronounced her) sitting in a<br>
corner of the room, dressed in a velvet mantle (in the midst of<br>
summer), with her bonnet off; squat, fat, red-faced, and at least<br>
fifty.<br>
<br>
She looked as if she would devour me with her eyes, staring at me from<br>
head to foot, without the least regard to the confusion and blushes her<br>
eyeing me so fixedly put me to, and which were to her, no doubt, the<br>
strongest recommendation and marks of my being fit for her purpose.<br>
After a little time, in which my air, person and whole figure had<br>
undergone a strict examination, which I had, on my part, tried to<br>
render favourable to me, by primming, drawing up my neck, and setting<br>
my best looks, she advanced and spoke to me with the greatest<br>
demureness:<br>
<br>
&ldquo;Sweet-heart, do you want a place?<br>
<br>
&ldquo;Yes, and please you,&rdquo; (with a curtsey down to the ground).<br>
<br>
Upon this she acquainted me she was actually come to the office<br>
herself, to look out for a servant; that she believed I might do, with<br>
a little of her instruction; that she could take my very looks for a<br>
sufficient character; that London was a very wicked, vile, place; that<br>
she hoped I would be tractable, and keep out of bad company; in short,<br>
she said all to me that an old experienced practitioner in town could<br>
think of, and which was much more than was necessary to take in an<br>
artless inexperienced country maid, who was even afraid of becoming a<br>
wanderer about the streets, and therefore gladly jumped at the first<br>
offer of a shelter, especially from so grave and matron-like a lady,<br>
for such my flattering fancy assured me this new mistress of mine was,<br>
I being actually hired under the nose of the good woman that kept the<br>
office, whose shrewed smiles and shrugs I could not help observing, and<br>
innocently interpreted them as marks of being pleased at my getting<br>
into place so soon: but, as I afterwards came to know, these Beldams<br>
understood one another very well, and this was a market where Mrs.<br>
Brown, my mistress, frequently attended, on the watch for any fresh<br>
goods that might offer there, for the use of her customers, and her own<br>
profit.<br>
<br>
Madam was, however, so well pleased with her bargain that fearing I<br>
presume, lest better advice or some accident might occasion my slipping<br>
through her fingers, she would officiously take me in a coach to my<br>
inn, where, calling herself for my box, it was, I being present,<br>
delivered without the least scruple or explanation as to where I was<br>
going.<br>
<br>
This being over, she bid the coachman drive to a shop in St. Paul&rsquo;s<br>
Churchyard, where she bought a pair of gloves, which she gave me, and<br>
thence renewed her directions to the coachman to drive to her house in<br>
&mdash;&mdash;&mdash; street, who accordingly landed us at the door, after I had been<br>
cheered up and entertained by the way with the most plausible flams,<br>
without one syllable from which I could conclude anything but that I<br>
was, by the greatest luck, fallen into the hands of kindest mistress,<br>
not to say friend, that the vast world could afford; and accordingly I<br>
entered her doors with most complete confidence and exultation,<br>
promising, myself that, as soon as I could be a little settled, I would<br>
acquaint Esther Davis with my rare good fortune.<br>
<br>
You may be sure the good opinion of my place was not lessened by the<br>
appearance of a very handsome back parlor, into which I was led and<br>
which seemed to me magnificently furnished, who had never seen better<br>
rooms than the ordinary ones in inns upon the road. There were two gilt<br>
pier-glasses, and a buffet, on which a few pieces of plate, set out to<br>
the most shew, dazzled, and altogether persuaded me that I must be got<br>
into a very reputable family.<br>
<br>
Here my mistress first began her part, with telling me that I must have<br>
good spirits, and learn to be free with her; that she had not taken me<br>
to be a common servant, to do domestic drudgery, but to be a kind of<br>
companion to her; and that if I would be a good girl, she would do more<br>
than twenty mothers for me; to all which I answered only by the<br>
profoundest and the awkwardest curtsies, and a few monosyllables, such<br>
as &ldquo;&rsquo;yes! no! to be sure!&rdquo;<br>
<br>
Presently my mistress touched the bell, and in came a strapping<br>
maid-servant, who had let us in. &ldquo;Here, Martha,&rdquo; said Mrs. Brown, &ldquo;I<br>
have just hired this young woman to look after my linen; so step up and<br>
show her her chamber; and I charge you to use her with as much respect<br>
as you would myself, for I have taken a prodigious liking to her, and I<br>
do not know what I shall do for her.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
Martha, who was an arch-jade, and, being used to this decoy, had her<br>
cue perfect, made me a kind of half curtsy, and asked me to walk up<br>
with her; and accordingly showed me a neat room, two pair of stairs<br>
backwards, in which there was a handsome bed, where Martha told me I<br>
was to lie with a young gentlewoman, a cousin of my mistress, who she<br>
was sure would be vastly good to me. Then she ran out into such<br>
affected encomiums on her good mistress! her sweet mistress! and how<br>
happy I was to light upon her! and that I could not have bespoke a<br>
better; with other the like gross stuff, such as would itself have<br>
started suspicions in any but such an unpractised simpleton, who was<br>
perfectly new to life, and who took every word she said in the very<br>
sense she laid out for me to take it; but she readily saw what a<br>
penetration she had to deal with, and measured me very rightly in her<br>
manner of whistling to me, so as to make me pleased with my cage, and<br>
blind to the wires.<br>
<br>
In the midst of these false explanations of the nature of my future<br>
service, we were rung for down again, and I was reintroduced into the<br>
same parlour, where there was a table laid with three covers; and my<br>
mistress had now got with her one of her favourite girls, a notable<br>
manager of her house, and whose business it was to prepare and break<br>
such young fillies as I was to the mounting block; and she was<br>
accordingly, in that view, alloted me for a bed-fellow, and, to give<br>
her the more authority, she had the title of cousin conferred on her by<br>
the venerable president of this college.<br>
<br>
Here I underwent a second survey, which ended in the full approbation<br>
of Mrs. Ph&oelig;be Ayres, the name of my tutoress elect, to whose care and<br>
instruction I was affectionately recommended.<br>
<br>
Dinner was now set on table, and in pursuance of treating me as a<br>
companion, Mrs. Brown, with a tone to cut off all dispute, soon<br>
over-ruled my most humble and most confused protestations against<br>
sitting down with her Ladyship, which my very short breeding just<br>
suggested to me could not be right, or in the order of things.<br>
<br>
At table, the conversation was chiefly kept up by the two madams and<br>
carried on in double meaning expressions, interrupted every now and<br>
then by kind assurances to me, all tending to confirm and fix my<br>
satisfaction with my present condition: augment it they could not, so<br>
very a novice was I then.<br>
<br>
It was here agreed that I should keep myself up and out of sight for a<br>
few days, till such clothes could be procured for me as were fit for<br>
the character I was to appear in, of my mistress&rsquo;s companion, observing<br>
withal, that on the first impressions of my figure much might depend;<br>
and, as they rightly judged, the prospect of exchanging my country<br>
clothes for London finery, made the clause of confinement digest<br>
perfectly well with me. But the truth was, Mrs. Brown did not care that<br>
I should be seen or talked to by any, either of her customers, or her<br>
Does (as they called the girls provided for them), till she secured a<br>
good market for my maidenhead, which I had at least all the appearances<br>
of having brought into her Ladyship&rsquo;s service.<br>
<br>
To slip over minutes of no importance to the main of my story, I pass<br>
the interval to bed time, in which I was more and more pleased with the<br>
views that opened to me, of an easy service under these good people;<br>
and after supper being shewed up to bed, Miss Ph&oelig;be, who observed a<br>
kind of reluctance in me to strip and go to bed, in my shift, before<br>
her, now the maid was withdrawn, came up to me, and beginning with<br>
unpinning my handkerchief and gown, soon encouraged me to go on with<br>
undressing myself; and, blushing at now seeing myself naked to my<br>
shift, I hurried to get under the bed-clothes out of sight.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be laughed and was not long before she placed herself by my side.<br>
She was about five and twenty, by her most suspicious account, in<br>
which, according to all appearances, she must have sunk at least ten<br>
good years; allowance, too, being made for the havoc which a long<br>
course of hackneyship and hot waters must have made of her<br>
constitution, and which had already brought on, upon the spur, that<br>
stale stage in which those of her profession are reduced to think of<br>
showing company, instead of seeing it.<br>
<br>
No sooner then was this precious substitute of my mistress laid down,<br>
but she, who was never out of her way when any occasion of lewdness<br>
presented itself, turned to me, embraced and kissed me with great<br>
eagerness. This was new, this was odd; but imputing it to nothing but<br>
pure kindness, which, for ought I knew, it might be the London way to<br>
express in that manner, I was determined not to be behind-hand with<br>
her, and returned her the kiss and embrace, with all the fervour that<br>
perfect innocence knew.<br>
<br>
Encouraged by this, her hands became extremely free, and wandered over<br>
my whole body, with touches, squeezes, pressures, that rather warmed<br>
and surprised me with their novelty, than they either shocked or<br>
alarmed me.<br>
<br>
The flattering praises she intermingled with these invasions,<br>
contributed also not a little to bribe my passiveness; and, knowing no<br>
ill, I feared none, especially from one who had prevented all doubts of<br>
her womanhood, by conducting my hands to a pair of breasts that hung<br>
loosely down, in a size and volume that full sufficiently distinguished<br>
her sex, to me at least, who had never made any other comparison.<br>
<br>
I lay then all tame and passive as she could wish, whilst her freedom<br>
raised no other emotion but those of a strange, and, till then, unfelt<br>
pleasure. Every part of me was open and exposed to the licentious<br>
courses of her hands, which, like a lambent fire, ran over my whole<br>
body, and thawed all coldness as they went.<br>
<br>
My breasts, if it is not too bold a figure to call so two hard, firm,<br>
rising hillocks, that just began to shew themselves, or signify<br>
anything to the touch, employed and amused her hands awhile, till,<br>
slipping down lower, over a smooth track, she could just feel the soft<br>
silky down that had but a few months before put forth and garnished the<br>
mount-pleasant of those parts, and promised to spread a grateful<br>
shelter over the sweet seat of the most exquisite sensation, and which<br>
had been, till that instant, the seat of the most insensible innocence.<br>
Her fingers played and strove to twine in the young tendrils of that<br>
moss, which nature has contrived at once for use and ornament.<br>
<br>
But, not contented with these outer posts, she now attempts the main<br>
spot, and began to twitch, to insinuate, and at length to force an<br>
introduction of a finger into the quick itself, in such a manner, that<br>
had she not proceeded by insensible gradations that inflamed me beyond<br>
the power of modesty to oppose its resistance to their progress, I<br>
should have jumped out of bed and cried for help against such strange<br>
assaults.<br>
<br>
Instead of which, her lascivious touches had lighted up a new fire that<br>
wantoned through all my veins, but fixed with violence in that center<br>
appointed them by nature, where the first strange hands were now busied<br>
in feeling, squeezing, compressing the lips, then opening them again,<br>
with a finger between, till an &ldquo;Oh!&rdquo; expressed her hurting me, where<br>
the narrowness of the unbroken passage refused it entrance to any<br>
depth.<br>
<br>
In the meantime, the extension of my limbs, languid stretching, sighs,<br>
short heavings, all conspired to assure that experienced wanton that I<br>
was more pleased than offended at her proceedings, which she seasoned<br>
with repeated kisses and exclamations, such as &ldquo;Oh! what a charming<br>
creature thou art! What a happy man will he be that first makes a woman<br>
of you! Oh! that I were a man for your sake!&rdquo; with the like broken<br>
expressions, interrupted by kisses as fierce and salacious as ever I<br>
received from the other sex.<br>
<br>
For my part, I was transported, confused, and out of myself; feelings<br>
so new were too much for me. My heated and alarmed senses were in a<br>
tumult that robbed me of all liberty of thought; tears of pleasure<br>
gushed from my eyes, and somewhat assuaged the fire that raged all over<br>
me.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be, herself, the hackneyed, thorough-bred Ph&oelig;be, to whom all modes<br>
and devices of pleasure were known and familiar, found, it seems, in<br>
this exercise her those arbitrary tastes, for which there is no<br>
accounting. Not that she hated men, or did not even prefer them to her<br>
own sex; but when she met with such occasions as this was, a satiety of<br>
enjoyments in the common road, perhaps, too a great secret bias,<br>
inclined her to make the most of pleasure, wherever she could find it,<br>
without distinction of sexes. In this view, now well assured that she<br>
had, by her touches, sufficiently inflamed me for her purpose, she<br>
rolled down the bed clothes gently, and I saw myself stretched naked,<br>
my shift being turned up to my neck, whilst I had no power or sense to<br>
oppose it. Even my growing blushes expressed more desire than modesty,<br>
whilst the candle, left (to be sure not undesignedly) burning, threw a<br>
full light on my whole body.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;No!&rdquo; says Ph&oelig;be, &ldquo;you must not, my sweet girl, think to hide all these<br>
treasures from me. My sight must be feasted as my touch. I must devour<br>
with my eyes this springing bosom. Suffer me to kiss it. I have not<br>
seen it enough. Let me kiss it once more. What firm, smooth, white<br>
flesh is here! How delicately shaped! Then this delicious down! Oh! let<br>
me view the small, dear, tender cleft! This is too much, I cannot bear<br>
it! I must! I must!&rdquo; Here she took my hand, and in a transport carried<br>
it where you will easily guess. But what a difference in the state of<br>
the same thing! A spreading thicket of bushy curls marked the full<br>
grown, complete woman. Then the cavity to which she guided my hand<br>
easily received it; and as soon as she felt it within her, she moved<br>
herself to and fro, with so rapid a friction, that I presently withdrew<br>
it, wet and clammy, when instantly Ph&oelig;be grew more composed, after two<br>
or three sighs, and heart-fetched Oh&rsquo;s! and giving me a kiss that<br>
seemed to exhale her soul through her lips, she replaced the<br>
bed-clothes over us. What pleasure she had found I will not say; but<br>
this I know, that the first sparks of kindling nature, the first ideas<br>
of pollution, were caught by me that night; and that the acquaintance<br>
and communication with the bad of our sex, is often as fatal to<br>
innocence as all the seductions of the other. But to go on. When Ph&oelig;be<br>
was restored to that calm, which I was far from the enjoyment of<br>
myself, she artfully sounded me on all the points necessary to govern<br>
the designs of my virtuous mistress on me, and by my answers, drawn<br>
from pure undissembled nature, she had no reason but to promise herself<br>
all imaginable success, so far as it depended on my ignorance, easiness<br>
and warmth of constitution.<br>
<br>
After a sufficient length of dialogue, my bedfellow left me to my rest,<br>
and I fell asleep, through pure weariness, from the violent emotions I<br>
had been led into, when nature which had been too warmly stirred and<br>
fermented to subside without allaying by some means or other relieved<br>
me by one of those luscious dreams, the transports of which are scarce<br>
inferior to those of waking real action.<br>
<br>
In the morning I awoke about ten, perfectly gay and refreshed. Ph&oelig;be<br>
was up before me, and asked me in the kindest manner how I did, how I<br>
had rested, and if I was ready for breakfast? carefully, at the same<br>
time, avoiding to increase the confusion she saw I was in, at looking<br>
her in the face, by any hint of the night&rsquo;s bed scene. I told her if<br>
she pleased I would get up, and begin any work she would be pleased to<br>
set me about. She smiled; presently the maid brought in the tea<br>
equipage, and I just huddled my clothes on, when in waddled my<br>
mistress. I expected no less than to be told of, if not chid for, my<br>
late rising, when I was most agreeably disappointed by her compliments<br>
on my pure and fresh looks. I was &ldquo;a bud of beauty&rdquo; (this was her<br>
style), &ldquo;and how vastly all the fine men would admire me!&rdquo; to all which<br>
my answers did not, I can assure you, wrong my breeding; they were as<br>
simple and silly as they could wish, and, no doubt, flattered them<br>
infinitely more than had they proved me enlightened by education and a<br>
knowledge of the world.<br>
<br>
We breakfasted, and the tea things were scarce removed, when in were<br>
brought two bundles of linen and wearing apparel: in short, all the<br>
necessaries for rigging me out, as they termed it, completely.<br>
<br>
Imagine to yourself, Madam, how my little coquet heart fluttered with<br>
joy at the sight of a white lutestring, flowered with silver, scoured<br>
indeed, but passed on me for spick and span new, a Brussels lace cap,<br>
braited shoes, and the rest in proportion, all second-hand finery, and<br>
procured instantly for the occasion, by the diligence and industry of<br>
the good Mrs. Brown, who had already a chapman for me in the house,<br>
before whom my charms were to pass in review; for he had not only, in<br>
course, insisted on a previous sight of the premises, but also on<br>
immediate surrendering to him, in case of his agreeing for me;<br>
concluding very wisely, that such a place as I was in, was of the<br>
hottest to trust the keeping of such a perishable commodity in, as a<br>
maidenhead.<br>
<br>
The care of dressing and tricking me out for the market, was then left<br>
to Ph&oelig;be, who acquitted herself, if not well, at least perfectly to the<br>
satisfaction of everything but my impatience of seeing myself dressed.<br>
When it was over, and I viewed myself in the glass, I was no doubt, too<br>
natural, too artless, to hide my childish joy at the change: a change,<br>
in the real truth, for much the worse, since I must have much better<br>
become the neat easy simplicity of my rustic dress than the awkward,<br>
untoward, tawdry finery that I could not conceal my strangeness to.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be&rsquo;s compliments, however, in which her own share in dressing me was<br>
not forgot, did not a little confirm me in the first notions I had ever<br>
entertained concerning my person; which, be it said without vanity, was<br>
then tolerable to justify a taste for me, and of which it may not be<br>
out of place here to sketch you an unflattered picture.<br>
<br>
I was tall, yet not too tall for my age, which, as I before remarked,<br>
was barely turned of fifteen; my shape perfectly straight, thin<br>
waisted, and light and free without owing anything to stays; my hair<br>
was a glossy auburn, and as soft as silk, flowing down my neck in<br>
natural curls, and did not a little to set off the whiteness of a<br>
smooth skin; my face was rather too ruddy, though its features were<br>
delicate, and the shape was a roundish oval, except where a pit on my<br>
chin had far from a disagreeable effect; my eyes were as black as can<br>
be imagined, and rather languishing than sparkling, except on certain<br>
occasions, when I have been told they struck fire fast enough; my<br>
teeth, which I ever carefully preserved, were small, even and white; my<br>
bosom was finely raised, and one might then discern rather the promise<br>
than the actual growth of the round, firm breast, that in a little time<br>
made that promise good. In short, all the points of beauty that are<br>
most universally in request, I had, or at least my vanity forbid me to<br>
appeal from the decision of our sovereign judges the men, who all, that<br>
I ever knew at last, gave it thus highly in my favour; and I met with,<br>
even in my own sex, some that were above denying me that justice,<br>
whilst others praised me yet more unsuspectedly, by endeavouring to<br>
detract from me, in points of person and figure that I obviously<br>
excelled in. This is, I own, too strong of self praise; but I should be<br>
ungrateful to nature, and to a form to which I owe such singular<br>
blessings of pleasure and fortune, were I to suppress, through an<br>
affectation of modesty, the mention of such valuable gifts.<br>
<br>
Well then, dressed I was, and little did it then enter into my head<br>
that all this gay attire was no more than decking the victim out for<br>
sacrifice, whilst I innocently attributed all to mere friendship and<br>
kindness in the sweet good Mrs. Brown; who, I was forgetting to<br>
mention, had, under pretence of keeping my money safe, got from me,<br>
without the least hesitation, the driblet (so I now call it) which<br>
remained to me after the expenses of my journey.<br>
<br>
After some little time most agreebly spent before the glass, in scarce<br>
self-admiration, since my new dress had by much the greatest share in<br>
it, I was sent for down to the parlour, where the old lady saluted me,<br>
and wished me joy of my new clothes, which she was not ashamed to say,<br>
fitted me as if I had worn nothing but the finest all my life-time; but<br>
what was it she could not see me silly enough to swallow? At the same<br>
time, she presented me to another cousin of her own creation, an<br>
elderly gentleman, who got up, at my entry into the room, and on my<br>
dropping a curtsy to him, saluted me, and seemed a little affronted<br>
that I had only presented my cheek to him: a mistake, which, if one, he<br>
immediately corrected, by gluing his lips to mine, with an ardour which<br>
his figure had not at all disposed me to thank him for: his figure, I<br>
say, than which nothing could be more shocking or detestable: for ugly<br>
and disagreeable were terms too gentle to convey a just idea of it.<br>
<br>
Imagine to yourself, a man rather past threescore, short and ill-made,<br>
with a yellow cadaverous hue, great goggle eyes, that stared as if he<br>
was strangled; an out-mouth from two more properly tusks than teeth,<br>
livid lips, and breath like a Jake&rsquo;s: then he had a peculiar<br>
ghastliness in his grin, that made him perfectly frightful, if not<br>
dangerous to women with child; yet, made as he was thus in mock of man,<br>
he was so blind to his own staring deformities, as to think himself<br>
born to please, and that no woman could see him with impunity: in<br>
consequence of which idea, he had lavished great sums on such wretches<br>
as could gain upon themselves to pretend love to his person, whilst to<br>
those who had not art or patience to dissemble the horror it inspired,<br>
he behaved even brutally. Impotence, more than necessity, made him seek<br>
in variety, the provocative that was wanting to raise him to the pitch<br>
of enjoyment, which he too often saw himself baulked of, by the failure<br>
of his powers: and this always threw him into a fit of rage, which he<br>
wreaked, as far as he durst, on the innocent objects of his fit of<br>
momentary desire.<br>
<br>
This then was the master to which my conscientious benefactress, who<br>
had long been his purveyor in this way, had doomed me, and sent for me<br>
down purposely for his examination. Accordingly she made me stand up<br>
before him, turned me round, unpinned my handkerchief, remarked to him<br>
the rise and fall, the turn and whiteness of a bosom just beginning to<br>
fill; then made me walk, and took even a handle from the rusticity of<br>
my charms: in short, she omitted no point of jockeyship; to which he<br>
only answered by gracious nods of approbation, whilst he looked goats<br>
and monkeys at me: for I sometimes stole a corner glance at him, and<br>
encountering his fiery, eager stare, looked another way from pure<br>
horror and affright, which he, characteristically, attributed to<br>
nothing more than maiden modesty, or at least the affectation of it.<br>
<br>
However, I was soon dismissed, and reconducted to my room by Ph&oelig;be, who<br>
stuck close to me, not leaving me alone, and at leisure to make such<br>
reflections as might naturally rise to any one, not an idiot, on such a<br>
scene as I had just gone through; but to my shame be it confessed, that<br>
just was my invincible stupidity, or rather portentous innocence, that<br>
I did not yet open my eyes to Mrs. Brown&rsquo;s designs, and saw nothing in<br>
this titular cousin of hers but a shockingly hideous person, which did<br>
not at all concern me, unless that my gratitude for my benefactress<br>
made me extend my respect to all her cousinhood.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be, however, began to sift the state and pulses of my heart toward<br>
this monster, asking me how I should approve of such a fine gentelman<br>
for a husband. (Fine gentleman, I suppose she called him, from his<br>
being daubed with lace.) I answered her very naturally, that I had no<br>
thoughts of a husband, but that if I was to choose one, it should be<br>
among my own degree, sure! so much had my aversion to that wretch&rsquo;s<br>
hideous figure indisposed me to all &ldquo;fine gentlemen,&rdquo; and confounded my<br>
ideas, as if those of that rank had been necessarily cast in the same<br>
mould that he was. But Ph&oelig;be was not to be put off so, but went on with<br>
her endeavours to melt and soften me for the purposes of my reception<br>
into that hospitable house: and whilst she talked of the sex in<br>
general, she had no reason to despair of a compliance, which more than<br>
one reason showed her would be easily enough obtained of me; but then<br>
she had too much experience not to discover that my particular fixed<br>
aversion to that frightful cousin would be a block not so readily to be<br>
removed, as suited the consummation of their bargain, and sale of me.<br>
<br>
Mother Brown had in the meantime agreed the terms with this loquorice<br>
old goat, which I afterwards understood were to be fifty guineas<br>
peremptory, for the liberty of attempting me, and a hundred more at the<br>
complete gratification of his desires, in the triumph over my<br>
virginity: and as for me, I was to be left entirely at the discretion<br>
of his liking and generosity. This unrighteous contract being thus<br>
settled, he was so eager to be put in possession, that he insisted on<br>
being introduced to drink tea with me that afternoon, when we were to<br>
be left alone; nor would he hearken to the procuress&rsquo;s remonstrances,<br>
that I was not sufficiently prepared, and ripened for such an attack;<br>
that I was too green and untamed, having been scarce twenty-four hours<br>
in the house: it is the character of lust to be impatient, and his<br>
vanity arming him against any supposition of other than the common<br>
resistance of a maid on those occasions, made him reject all proposals<br>
of a delay, and my dreadful trial was thus fixed, unknown to me, for<br>
that very evening.<br>
<br>
At dinner, Mrs. Brown and Ph&oelig;be did nothing but run riot in praise of<br>
this wonderful cousin, and how happy that woman would be that he would<br>
favour with his addresses; in short my two gossips exhausted all their<br>
rhetoric to persuade me to accept them: &ldquo;that the gentleman was<br>
violently smitten with me at first sight; that he would make my fortune<br>
if I would be a good girl and not stand in my own light; that I should<br>
trust his honour; that I should be made for ever, and have a chariot to<br>
go abroad in,&rdquo; with all such stuff as was fit to turn the head of such<br>
a silly ignorant girl as I then was: but luckily here my aversion had<br>
taken already such deep root in me, my heart was so strongly defended<br>
from him by my senses, that wanting the art to mask my sentiments, I<br>
gave them no hopes of their employer succeeding, at least very easily,<br>
with me. The glass too marched pretty quick, with a view, I suppose, to<br>
make a friend of the warmth of my constitution, in the minutes of the<br>
imminent attack.<br>
<br>
Thus they kept me pretty long at table, and about six in the evening,<br>
after I had retired to my apartment, and the tea board was set, enters<br>
my venerable mistress, followed close by that satyr, who came in<br>
grinning in a way peculiar to him, and by his odious presence,<br>
confirmed me in all the sentiments of detestation which his first<br>
appearance had given birth to.<br>
<br>
He sat down fronting me, and all tea time kept ogling me in a manner<br>
that gave me the utmost pain and confusion, all the mark of which he<br>
still explained to be my bashfulness, and not being used to see<br>
company.<br>
<br>
Tea over, the commoding old lady pleady urgent business (which indeed<br>
was true) to go out, and earnestly desired me to entertain her cousin<br>
kindly till she came back, both for my own sake and her; and then, with<br>
a &ldquo;Pray, sir, be very good, be very tender to the sweet child,&rdquo; she<br>
went out of the room, leaving me staring, with my mouth open, and<br>
unprepared by the suddenness of her departure, to oppose it.<br>
<br>
We were now alone; and on that idea a sudden fit of trembling seized<br>
me. I was so afraid, without a precise notion of why, and what I had to<br>
fear, that I sat on the settee, by the fire side, motionless and<br>
petrified, without life or spirit, not knowing how to look or how to<br>
stir.<br>
<br>
But long I was not suffered to remain in this state of stupefaction:<br>
the monster squatted down by me on the settee, and without farther<br>
ceremony or preamble, flings his arms about my neck, and drawing me<br>
pretty forcibly towards him, obliged me to receive, in spite of my<br>
struggles to disengage from him, his pestilential kisses, which quite<br>
overcame me. Finding me then next to senseless, and unresisting, he<br>
tears off my neck handkerchief, and laid all open there, to his eyes<br>
and hands: still I endured all without flinching, till emboldened by my<br>
sufferance and silence, for I had not the power to speak or cry out, he<br>
attempted to lay me down on the settee, and I felt his hand on the<br>
lower part of my naked thighs, which were crossed, and which he<br>
endeavoured to unlock. Oh then! I was roused out of my passive<br>
endurance, and springing from him with an activity he was not prepared<br>
for, threw myself at his feet, and begged him, in the most moving tone,<br>
not to be rude, and that he would not hurt me. &ldquo;Hurt you, my dear?&rdquo;<br>
says the brute, &ldquo;I intend you no harm. Has not the old lady told you<br>
that I love you? that I shall do handsomely by you?&rdquo;<br>
<br>
&ldquo;She has indeed, sir,&rdquo; said I, &ldquo;but I cannot love you, indeed I cannot!<br>
pray let me alone! yes! I will love you dearly if you will let me alone<br>
and go away.&rdquo; But I was talking to the wind, for whether my tears, my<br>
attitude, or the disorder of my dress proved fresh incentives, or<br>
whether he was now under the dominion of desires he could not bridle,<br>
but snorting and foaming with lust and rage, he renews his attack,<br>
seizes me, and again attempts to extend and fix me on the settee: in<br>
which he succeeded so far as to lay me along, and even to toss my<br>
petticoats over my head, and lay my thighs bare, which I obstinately<br>
kept close, nor could he, though he attempted with his knee to force<br>
them open, effect it so as to stand fair for being master of the main<br>
avenue; he was unbuttoned, both waistcoat and breeches, yet I only felt<br>
the weight of his body upon me, whilst I lay struggling with<br>
indignation, and dying with terrors; but he stopped all of a sudden,<br>
and got off, panting, blowing, cursing, and repeating &ldquo;old and ugly!&rdquo;<br>
for so I had very naturally called him in the heat of my defence.<br>
<br>
The brute had, it seems, as I afterwards understood, brought on, by his<br>
eagerness and struggle, the ultimate period of his hot fit of lust,<br>
which his power was too short-lived to carry him through the full<br>
execution of; of which my thighs and linen received the effusion.<br>
<br>
When it was over he bid me, with a tone of displeasure, get up: &ldquo;that<br>
he would not do me the honour to think of me any more; that the old<br>
b&mdash;&mdash;h might look out for another cully; that he would not be fooled so<br>
by ever a country mock modesty in England; that he supposed I had left<br>
my maidenhead with some hobnail in the country, and was come to dispose<br>
of my skim-milk in town&rdquo; with a volley of the like abuse; which I<br>
listened to with more pleasure than ever fond woman did to<br>
protestations of love from her darling minion: for, incapable as I was<br>
of receiving any addition to my perfect hatred and aversion to him, I<br>
looked on this railing, as my security against his renewing his most<br>
odious caress.<br>
<br>
Yet, plain as Mrs. Brown&rsquo;s views were now come out, I had not the<br>
heart, or spirit to open my eyes to them: still I could not part with<br>
my dependence on that beldam, so much did I think myself hers, soul and<br>
body: or rather, I sought to deceive myself with the continuation of my<br>
good opinion of her, and choose to wait the worst at her hands, sooner<br>
than be turned out to starve in the streets, without a penny of money<br>
or a friend to apply to these fears were my folly.<br>
<br>
While this confusion of ideas was passing in my head, and I sat<br>
pensively by the fire, with my eyes brimming with tears, my neck still<br>
bare, and my cap fallen off in the struggle, so that my hair was in the<br>
disorder you may guess, the villain&rsquo;s lust began, I suppose, to be<br>
again in flow, at the sight of all that bloom of youth which presented<br>
itself to his view, a bloom yet unenjoyed, and of course not yet<br>
indifferent to him.<br>
<br>
After some pause, he asked me with a tone of voice mightily softer,<br>
whether I would make it up with him before the old lady returned, and<br>
all should be well; he would restore me to his affections, at the same<br>
time offering to kiss me and feel my breasts. But now my extreme<br>
aversion, my fears, my indignation, all acting upon me, gave me a<br>
spirit not natural to me, so that breaking loose from him, I ran to the<br>
bell and rang it, with such violence and effect as to bring up the maid<br>
to know what was the matter, or whether the gentleman wanted anything;<br>
and before he could proceed to greater extremities, she bounced into<br>
the room, and seeing me stretched on the floor, my hair all<br>
dishevelled, my nose gushing out blood, which did not a little<br>
tragedize the scene, and my odious persecutor still intent of pushing<br>
his brutal point, unmoved by all my cries and distress, she was herself<br>
confounded and did not know what to do.<br>
<br>
As much, however, as Martha might be prepared and hardened to<br>
transactions of this sort, all womanhood must have been out of her<br>
heart could she have seen this unmoved. Besides that, on the face of<br>
things, she imagined that matters had gone greater lengths than they<br>
really had, and that the courtesy of the house had been actually<br>
consummated on me, and flung: me into the condition I was in: in this<br>
notion she instantly took my part, and advised the gentleman to go down<br>
and leave me to recover myself, and &ldquo;that all would be soon over with<br>
me; that when Mrs. Brown and Ph&oelig;be, who were gone out, were returned,<br>
they would take order for everything to his satisfaction; that nothing<br>
would be lost by a little patience with the poor tender thing; that for<br>
her part she was frightened; she could not tell what to say to such<br>
doings; but that she would stay by me till my mistress came home.&rdquo; As<br>
the wench said all this in a resolute tone, and the monster himself<br>
began to perceive that things would not mend by his staying, he took<br>
his hat and went out of the room murmuring and pitting his brows like<br>
an old ape, so that I was delivered from the horrors of his detestable<br>
presence.<br>
<br>
As soon as he was gone, Martha very tenderly offered me her assistance<br>
in anything, and would have got me some hartshorn drops and put me to<br>
bed; which last I, at first, positively refused, in the fear that the<br>
monster might return and take me at that disadvantage. However, with<br>
much persuasion and assurances that I should not be molested that night<br>
she prevailed on me to lie down; and indeed I was so weakened by my<br>
struggles, so dejected by my fearful apprehension, so terror-struck,<br>
that I had not power to sit up, or hardly to give answers to the<br>
questions with which the curious Martha plied and perplexed me.<br>
<br>
Such too, and so cruel was my fate, that I dreaded the sight of Mrs.<br>
Brown, as if I had been the criminal, and she the person injured; a<br>
mistake which you will not think so strange, on distinguishing that<br>
neither virtue nor principles had the least share in the defence I had<br>
made, but only the particular aversion I had conceived against this<br>
first brutal and frightful invader of my tender innocence.<br>
<br>
I passed then the time till Mrs. Brown came home, under all the<br>
agitations of fear and despair that may easily be guessed.<br>
<br>
About eleven at night my two ladies came home, and having received<br>
rather a favourable account from Martha, who had run down to let them<br>
in, for Mr. Crofts (that was the name of my brute) was gone out of the<br>
house, after waiting till he had tired his patience for Mrs. Brown&rsquo;s<br>
return, they came thundering up stairs, and seeing me pale, my face<br>
bloody, and all the marks of the most thorough dejection, they employed<br>
themselves more to comfort and re-inspirit me than in making me the<br>
reproaches I was weak enough to fear, I who had so many juster and<br>
stronger to retort upon them.<br>
<br>
Mrs. Brown withdrawn, Ph&oelig;be came presently to bed to me, and what with<br>
the answers she drew from me, what with her own method of palpably<br>
satisfying herself, she soon discovered that I had been more frightened<br>
than hurt; upon which I suppose, being herself seized with sleep, and<br>
reserving her lectures and instructions till the next morning, she left<br>
me, properly speaking, to my unrest; for, later tossing and turning the<br>
greatest part of the night, and tormenting myself with the falsest<br>
notions and apprehensions of things, I fell, through mere fatigue into<br>
a kind of delirious doze, out of which I waked late in the morning, in<br>
a violent fever: a circumstance which was extremely critical to<br>
reprieve me, at least for a time, from the attacks of a wretch,<br>
infinitely more terrible to me than death itself.<br>
<br>
The interested care that was taken of me during my illness, in order to<br>
restore me to a condition of making good the bawd&rsquo;s engagements, or of<br>
enduring further trials, had, however, such an effect on my grateful<br>
disposition that I even thought myself obliged to my un-doers for their<br>
attention to promote my recovery; and, above all, for the keeping out<br>
of my sight of that brutal ravisher, the author of my disorder, on<br>
their finding I was too strongly moved at the bare mention of his name.<br>
<br>
Youth is soon raised, and a few days were sufficient to conquer the<br>
fury of my fever: but, what contributed most to my perfect recovery and<br>
to my reconciliation with life, was the timely news that Mr. Crofts,<br>
who was a merchant of considerable dealings, was arrested at the King&rsquo;s<br>
suit, for nearly forty thousand pounds, on account of his driving a<br>
certain contraband trade, and that his affairs were so desperate, that<br>
even were it in his inclination, it would not be in his power to renew<br>
his designs upon me: for he was instantly thrown into a prison, which<br>
it was not likely he would get out of in haste.<br>
<br>
Mrs. Brown, who had touched his fifty guineas, advanced to so little<br>
purpose, and lost all hopes of the remaining hundred, began to look<br>
upon my treatment of him with a more favourable eye; and as they had<br>
observed my temper to be perfectly tractable and conformable to their<br>
views, all the girls that composed her flock were suffered to visit me,<br>
and had their cue to dispose me, by their conversation, to a perfect<br>
resignation of myself to Mrs. Brown&rsquo;s direction.<br>
<br>
Accordingly they were let in upon me, and all that frolic and<br>
thoughtless gaiety in which those giddy creatures consume either<br>
leisure, made me envy a condition of which I only saw the fair side;<br>
insomuch, that the being one of them became even my ambition: a<br>
disposition which they all carefully cultivated; and I wanted now<br>
nothing but to restore my health, that I might be able to undergo the<br>
ceremony of the initiation.<br>
<br>
Conversation, example, in short all, contributed, in that house, to<br>
corrupt my native parity, which had taken no root in education; whilst<br>
now the inflammable principal of pleasure, so easily fired at my age,<br>
made strange work within me, and all the modesty I was brought up in<br>
the habit, not the instruction of, began to melt away like dew before<br>
the sun&rsquo;s heat; not to mention that I made a vice of necessity, from<br>
the constant fears I had of being turned out to starve.<br>
<br>
I was soon pretty well recovered, and at certain hours allowed to range<br>
all over the house, but cautiously kept from seeing any company till<br>
the arrival of Lord B&mdash;&mdash;, from Bath, to whom Mrs. Brown, in respect to<br>
his experienced generosity on such occasions, proposed to offer the<br>
perusal of that trinket of mine, which bears so great an imaginary<br>
value; and his lordship being expected in town in less than a<br>
fortnight, Mrs. Brown judged I would be entirely renewed in beauty and<br>
freshness by that time, and afforded her the chance of a better bargain<br>
than she had driven with Mr. Crofts.<br>
<br>
In the meantime, I was so thoroughly, as they call it, brought over, so<br>
tame to their whistle, that, had my cage door been set open, I had no<br>
idea that I ought to fly anywhere, sooner than stay where I was; nor<br>
had I the least sense of regretting my condition, but waited very<br>
quietly for whatever Mrs. Brown should order concerning me; who on her<br>
side, by herself and her agents, took more than the necessary<br>
precautions to lull and lay asleep all just reflections on my destiny.<br>
<br>
Preachments of morality over the left shoulder; a life of joy painted<br>
in the gayest colours; caresses, promises, indulgent treatment;<br>
nothing, in short, was wanting to domesticate me entirely and to<br>
prevent my going out anywhere to get better advice. Alas! I dreamed of<br>
no such thing.<br>
<br>
Hitherto I had been indebted only to the girls of the house for the<br>
corruption of my innocence: their luscious talk, in which modesty was<br>
far from respected, their description of their engagements with men,<br>
had given me a tolerable insight into the nature and mysteries of their<br>
profession, at the same time that they highly provoked an itch of<br>
florid warm-spirited blood through every vein: but above all, my bed<br>
fellow Ph&oelig;be, whose pupil I more immediately was, exerted her talents<br>
in giving me the first tinctures of pleasure: whilst nature, now warmed<br>
and wantoned with discoveries so interesting, piqued a curiosity which<br>
Ph&oelig;be artfully whetted, and leading me from question to question of her<br>
own suggestion, explained to me all the mysteries of Venus. But I could<br>
not long remain in such a house as that, without being an eye-witness<br>
of more than I could conceive from her descriptions.<br>
<br>
One day, about twelve at noon, being thoroughly recovered of my fever,<br>
I happened to be in Mrs. Brown&rsquo;s dark closet, where I had not been half<br>
an hour, resting upon the maid&rsquo;s bed, before I heard a rustling in the<br>
bed-chamber, separated from the closet only by two sash doors, before<br>
the glasses of which were drawn two yellow damask curtains, but not so<br>
close as to exclude the full view of the room from any person in the<br>
closet.<br>
<br>
I instantly crept softly and posted myself so, that seeing everything<br>
minutely, I could not myself be seen; and who should come in but the<br>
venerable mother Abbess herself! handed in by a tall, brawny young<br>
Horse-grenadiers, moulded in the Hercules style: in fine, the choice of<br>
the most experienced dame, in those affairs, in all London.<br>
<br>
Oh! how still and hush did I keep at my stand, lest any noise should<br>
baulk my curiosity, or bring Madam into the closet!<br>
<br>
But I had not much reason to fear either, for she was entirely taken up<br>
with her present great concern, that she had no sense of attention to<br>
spare to anything else.<br>
<br>
Droll was it to see that clumsy fat figure of her&rsquo;s flop down on the<br>
foot of the bed, opposite to the closet door so that I had a full front<br>
view of all her charms.<br>
<br>
Her paramour sat down by her: he seemed to be a man of very few words,<br>
and a great stomach; for proceeding instantly to essentials, he gave<br>
her some hearty smacks, and thrusting his hands into her breasts,<br>
disengaged them from her stays, in scorn of whose confinement they<br>
broke loose, and sagged down, navel-low at least. A more enormous pair<br>
did my eyes never behold, nor of a worse colour, flagging, soft, and<br>
most lovingly contiguous: yet such as they were, this great beef-eater<br>
seemed to paw them with a most unenviable lust, seeking in vain to<br>
confine or cover one of them with a hand scarce less than a shoulder of<br>
mutton. After toying with them thus some time, as if they had been<br>
worth it, he laid her down pretty briskly, and canting up her<br>
petticoats, made barely a mask of them to her broad red face, that<br>
blushed with nothing but brandy.<br>
<br>
As he stood on one side, unbuttoning his waistcoat and breeches, her<br>
fat brawny thighs hung down, and the whole greasy landscape lay fairly<br>
open to my view; a wide open mouthed gap, overshaded with a grizzly<br>
bush, seemed held out like a beggar&rsquo;s wallet for its provision.<br>
<br>
But I soon had my eyes called off by a more striking object that<br>
entirely engrossed them.<br>
<br>
Her sturdy stallion had now unbuttoned, and produced naked, stiff and<br>
erect, that wonderful machine, which I had never seen before, and<br>
which, for the interest my own seat of pleasure began to take furiously<br>
in it, I stared at with all the eyes I had: however, my senses were too<br>
much flurried, too much concentered in that now burning spot of mine,<br>
to observe anything more than in general the make and turn of that<br>
instrument; from which the instinct of nature, yet more than all I had<br>
heard of it, now strongly informed me, I was to expect that supreme<br>
pleasure which she had placed in the meeting of those parts so<br>
admirably fitted for each other.<br>
<br>
Long, however, the young spark did not remain before giving it two or<br>
three shakes, by way of brandishing it, he threw himself upon her, and<br>
his back being now towards me, I could only take his being ingulphed<br>
for granted, by the directions he moved in, and the impossibility of<br>
missing so staring a mark; and now the bed shook, the curtains rattled<br>
so that I could scarce hear the sighs and murmurs, the heaves and<br>
pantings that accompanied the action, from the beginning to the end;<br>
the sound and sight of which thrilled to the very soul of me, and made<br>
every vein of my body circulate liquid fires: the emotion grew so<br>
violent that it almost intercepted my respiration.<br>
<br>
Prepared then, and disposed as I was by the discourse of my companions,<br>
and Ph&oelig;be&rsquo;s minute detail of everything, no wonder that such a sight<br>
gave the last dying blow to my native innocence.<br>
<br>
Whilst they were in the heat of the action, guided by nature only, I<br>
stole my hand up my petticoats, and with fingers on fire, seized and<br>
yet more inflamed that center of all my senses: my heart palpitated, as<br>
if it would force its way through my bosom: I breathed with pain; I<br>
twisted my thighs, squeezed and compressed the lips of that virgin<br>
slit, and following mechanically the example of Ph&oelig;be&rsquo;s manual<br>
operation on it, as far as I could find admission, brought on at last<br>
the critical ecstasy, the melting flow, into which nature, spent with<br>
excess of pleasure, dissolves and dies away.<br>
<br>
After which, my senses recovered coolness enough to observe the rest of<br>
the transaction between this happy pair.<br>
<br>
The young fellow had just dismounted, when the old lady immediately<br>
sprung up, with all the vigour of youth, derived, no doubt, from her<br>
late refreshment; and making him sit down, began in her turn to kiss<br>
him, to pat and pinch his cheeks, and play with his hair: all which he<br>
received with an air of indifference and coolness that showed him to be<br>
much altered from what he was when he first went on to the breach.<br>
<br>
My pious governess, however, not being above calling in auxiliaries,<br>
unlocks a little case of cordials that stood near the bed, and made him<br>
pledge her in a very plentiful dram: after which, and a little amorous<br>
parley, Madam set herself down upon the same place, at the bed&rsquo;s foot;<br>
and the young fellow standing sidewise by her, she, with the greatest<br>
effrontery imaginable, unbuttons his breeches, and removing his shirt,<br>
draws out his affair, so shrunk and diminished, that I could not but<br>
remember the difference, now crest-fallen, or just faintly lifting its<br>
head: but our experience matron very soon, by chaffing it with her<br>
hands, brought it to swell to that size and erection I had before seen<br>
it up to.<br>
<br>
I admired then, upon a fresh account, and with a nicer survey, the<br>
texture of that capital part of man: the flaming red head as it stood<br>
uncapt, the whiteness of the shaft, and the shrub growth of curling<br>
hair that embrowned the foots of it, the roundish bag that dangled down<br>
from it, all exacted my eager attention, and renewed my flame. But, as<br>
the main affair was now at the point the industrious dame had laboured<br>
to bring it to, she was not in the humour to put off the payment of her<br>
pains, but laying herself down, drew him gently upon her, and thus they<br>
finished, in the same manner as before, the old last act.<br>
<br>
This over, they both went out lovingly together, the old lady having<br>
first made him a present, as near as I could observe, of three or four<br>
pieces; he being not only her particular favourite on account of his<br>
performances, but a retainer to the house; from whose sight she had<br>
taken great care hitherto to secret me, lest he might not have had<br>
patience to wait for my lord&rsquo;s arrival, but have insisted on being his<br>
taster, which the old lady was under too much subjection to him to dare<br>
dispute with him; for every girl of the house fell to him in course,<br>
and the old lady only now and then got her turn, in consideration of<br>
the maintenance he had, and which he could scarce be accused of not<br>
earning from her.<br>
<br>
As soon as I heard them go down-stairs, I stole up softly to my own<br>
room, out of which I had luckily not been missed; there I began to<br>
breathe more free, and to give a loose to those warm emotions which the<br>
sight of such an encounter had raised in me, I laid me down on the bed,<br>
stretched myself out, joining and ardently wishing, and requiring any<br>
means to divert or allay the rekindled rage and tumult of my desires,<br>
which all pointed strongly to their pole: man. I felt about the bed as<br>
if I sought for something that I grasped in my waking dream, and not<br>
finding it, could have cried for vexation; every part of me plowing<br>
with simulated fires. At length, I resorted to the only present remedy,<br>
that of vain attempts at digitation, where the smallness of the theatre<br>
did not yet afford room enough for action, and where the pain my<br>
fingers gave me, in striving for admission, though they procured me a<br>
slight satisfaction for the present, started an apprehension which I<br>
could not be easy till I had communicated to Ph&oelig;be and received her<br>
explanations upon it.<br>
<br>
The opportunity, however, did not offer till next morning, for Ph&oelig;be<br>
did not come to bed till long after I was gone to sleep. As soon then<br>
as we were both awake, it was but in course to bring our ly-a-bed chat<br>
to hand, on the subject of my uneasiness: to which a recital of the<br>
love scene I had thus, by chance, been spectatress of, served for a<br>
preface.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be could not hear it to the end without more than one interruption<br>
by peals of laughter, and my ingenuous way of relating matters did not<br>
a little heighten the joke to her.<br>
<br>
But, on her sounding me how the sight had affected me, without mincing<br>
or hiding the pleasurable emotions it had inspired me with, I told her<br>
at the same time that one remark had perplexed me, and that very<br>
considerably. &ldquo;Aye!&rdquo; says she, &ldquo;what was that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why,&rdquo; replied I,<br>
&ldquo;having very curiously and attentively compared the size of that<br>
enormous machine, which did not appear, at least to my fearful<br>
imagination, less than my wrist, and at least three of my hand-fuls<br>
long, to that of the tender small part of me which was framed to<br>
receive it, I could not conceive its being possible to afford it<br>
entrance without dying, perhaps in the greatest pain, since she well<br>
knew that even a finger thrust in there hurt me beyond bearing. As to<br>
my mistress&rsquo;s and yours, I can very plainly distinguish the different<br>
dimensions of them from mine, palpable to the touch, and visible to the<br>
eye; so that, in short, great as the promised pleasure may be, I am<br>
afraid of the pain of the experiment.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be at this redoubled her laugh, and whilst I expected a very serious<br>
solution of my doubts and apprehensions in this matter, only told me<br>
that &ldquo;she never heard of a mortal wound being given in those parts, by<br>
that terrible weapon, and that some she knew younger, and as delicately<br>
made as myself, had outlived the operation; that she believed, at the<br>
worst, I should take a great deal of liking; that true it was, there<br>
was a great diversity of sizes in those parts, owing to nature,<br>
child-bearing, frequent over-stretching with unmerciful machines, but<br>
that at a certain age and habit of body, even the most experienced in<br>
those affairs could not well distinguish between the maid and the<br>
woman, supposing too an absence of all artifice, in their natural<br>
situation: but that since chance had thrown in my way one sight of that<br>
sort, she would procure me another, that should feast my eyes more<br>
delicately, and go a great way in the cure of my fears from that<br>
imaginary disproportion&rdquo;.<br>
<br>
On this she asked me if I knew Polly Phillips? &ldquo;Undoubterly,&rdquo; says I,<br>
&ldquo;the fair girl which was so tender of me when I was sick, and has been,<br>
as you told me, but two months in the house.&rdquo; &ldquo;The same,&rdquo; says Ph&oelig;be.<br>
&ldquo;You must know then, she is kept by a young Genoes merchant, whom his<br>
uncle, who is immensely rich, and whose darling he is, on a pretex of<br>
settling some accounts, but in reality to humour his inclinations for<br>
travelling, and seeing the world. He met casually with this Polly once<br>
in company, and taking a likning to her, makes it worth her while to<br>
keep entirely to him. He comes to her here twice or thrice a week, and<br>
she receives him in the light closet up one pair of stairs, where he<br>
enjoys her in a taste, I suppose, peculiar to the heat, or perhaps the<br>
caprices of his own country, I say no more, but to-morrow being his<br>
day, you shall see what passes between them, from a place only known to<br>
your mistress and myself.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
You may be sure, in the ply I was now taking, I had no objection to the<br>
proposal, and was rather a tip-toe for its accomplishments.<br>
<br>
At five in the evening next day, Ph&oelig;be, punctual to her promise, came<br>
to me as I sat alone in my own room, and beckoned me to follow her.<br>
<br>
We went down the back stairs very softly, and opening the door of a<br>
dark closet, where there was some old furniture kept, and some cases of<br>
liquor, she drew me in after her, and fastened the door upon us, we had<br>
no light but what came through a long crevice in the partition between<br>
ours and the light closet, where the scene of action lay; so that<br>
sitting on those low cases, we could, with the greatest ease, as well<br>
as clearness, see all objects (ourselves unseen), only by applying our<br>
eyes close to the crevice, where the moulding of a panel had warped, or<br>
started a little on the other side.<br>
<br>
The young gentleman was the first person I saw, with his back directly<br>
towards me, looking at a print. Polly was not yet come: in less than a<br>
minute though, the door opened, and she came in; and at the noise the<br>
door made he turned about, and come to meet her, with an air of the<br>
greatest tenderness and satisfaction.<br>
<br>
After saluting her, he led her to a coach that fronted us, where they<br>
both sat down, and the young Genoes helped her to a glass of wine, with<br>
some Naples biscuits on a salver.<br>
<br>
Presently, when they had exchanged a few kisses, and questions in<br>
broken English on one side, he began to unbutton, and, in fine, stript<br>
unto his shirt.<br>
<br>
As if this had been the signal agreed on for pulling off all their<br>
clothes, a scheme which the heat of the season perfectly favoured,<br>
Polly began to draw her pins, and as she had no stays to unlace, she<br>
was in a trice, with her gallant&rsquo;s officious assistance, undressed to<br>
all but her shift.<br>
<br>
When he saw this, his breeches were immediately loosened, waist and<br>
knee bands, and slipped over his ankles, clean off; his shirt collar<br>
was unbottoned too: then, first giving Polly an encouraging kiss, he<br>
stole, as it were, the shift off the girl, who being, I suppose, broke<br>
and familiarized to this humour, blushed indeed, but less than I did at<br>
the apparition of her, now standing stark naked, just as she came ont<br>
of the hands of pure nature, with her black hair loose and a-float down<br>
her dazzling white neck and shoulders, whilst the deepened carnation of<br>
her cheeks went off gradually into the hue of glazed snow: for such<br>
were the blended tints polish of her skin.<br>
<br>
This girl could not be above eighteen: her face regular and sweet<br>
featured, her shape exquisite; nor could I help envying her two ripe<br>
enchanting breasts, finely plumped out in flesh, but withal so round,<br>
so firm, that they sustained themselves, in scorn of any stay: then<br>
their nipples, pointing different ways, marked their pleasing<br>
separation; beneath them lay the delicious tract of the belly, which<br>
terminated in a parting of rift scarce discerning, that modesty seemed<br>
to retire downward, and seek shelter between two plump fleshy thighs:<br>
the curling hair that overspread its delightful front, clothed it with<br>
the richest sable fur in the universe: in short, she was evidently a<br>
subject for the painters to court her, sitting to them for a pattern<br>
female beauty, in all the true pride and pomp of nakedness.<br>
<br>
The young Italian (still in his shirt) stood gazing and transported at<br>
the sight of beauties that might have fired a dying hermit; his eager<br>
eyes devoured her, as she shifted attitudes at his discretion: neither<br>
were his hands excluded their share of the high feast, but wandered, on<br>
the hunt of pleasure, over every part and inch of her body, so<br>
qualified to afford the most exquisite sense of it.<br>
<br>
In the mean time time, one could not help observing the swell of his<br>
shirt before, that bolstered out, and pointed out the condition of<br>
things behind the curtain: but he soon removed it, by slipping his<br>
shirt over his head; and now, as to nakedness, they had nothing to<br>
reproach one another.<br>
<br>
The young gentleman, by Ph&oelig;be&rsquo;s guess, was about two and twenty; tall<br>
and well limbed. His body was finely formed, and of a most vigorous<br>
make, square shouldered, and broad chested: his face was not remarkable<br>
any way, but for a nose inclining to the Roman, eyes large, black, and<br>
sparkling, and a ruddiness in his cheeks that was the more a grace; for<br>
his complexion was of the brownest, not of that dusky dun colour which<br>
excludes, the idea of freshness, but of that clear, olive gloss, which<br>
glowing with life, dazzles perhaps less than fairness, and yet pleases<br>
more, when it pleases at all. His hair being too short to tie fell no<br>
lower than his neck, in short easy curls; and he had a few sprigs about<br>
his paps, that garnished his chest in a style of strength and<br>
manliness. Then his grand movement, which seemed to rise out of a<br>
thicket of curling hair, that spread from the root all over his thighs<br>
and belly up to the navel, stood stiff and upright, but of a size to<br>
frighten me, by sympathy for the small tender part which was the object<br>
of its fury, and which now lay exposed to my fairest view; for he had,<br>
immediately on stoppings off his shirt, gently pushed her down on the<br>
couch, which stood conveniently to break her willing fall. Her thighs<br>
were spread out to their utmost extention, and discovered between them<br>
the mark of the sex, the red-centered cleft of flesh, whose lips<br>
vermillioning inwards, expressed a small ruby line in sweet miniature,<br>
such as Guide&rsquo;s touch or colouring: could never attain to the life or<br>
delicacy of.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be, at this, gave me a gentle jog, to prepare me for a whisper<br>
question: &ldquo;Whether I thought my little maiden-head was much less?&rdquo; But<br>
my attention was too much engrossed, too much inwrapped with all I saw,<br>
to be able to give her any answer.<br>
<br>
By this time the young gentelman had changed her posture from lying<br>
breadth to length-wise on the coach: but her thighs were still spread,<br>
and the mark lay fair for him, who now kneeling between them, displayed<br>
to us a side view of that fierce erect machine of his, which threatened<br>
no less than splitting the tender victim, who lay smiling at the<br>
