uplifted stroke, nor seemed to decline it. He looked upon his weapon<br>
himself with some pleasure, and guiding it with his hand to the<br>
inviting; slit, drew aside the lips, and lodged it (after some thrusts,<br>
which Polly seemed even to assist) about half way; but there it stuck,<br>
I suppose from its growing thickness: he draws it again, and just<br>
wetting it with spittle, re-enters, and with ease sheathed it now up to<br>
the hilt, at which Polly gave a deep sigh, which was quite another tone<br>
than one of pain; he thrusts, she heaves, at first gently, and in a<br>
regular cadence; but presently the transport began to be too violent to<br>
observe any order or measure; their motions were too rapid, their<br>
kisses too fierce&rsquo; and fervent for nature to support such fury long:<br>
both seemed to me out of themselves: their eyes darted fires: &ldquo;Oh! oh!<br>
I can&rsquo;t bear it. It is too much. I die. I am going,&rdquo; were Polly&rsquo;s<br>
expressions of extasy: his joys were more silent: but soon broken<br>
murmurs, sighs heart-fetched, and at length a dispatching thrust, as if<br>
he would have forced himself up her body, and then the motionless<br>
languor of all his limbs, all shewed that the die-away moment was come<br>
upon him; which she gave signs of joining with by, the wild throwing of<br>
her hands about, closing her eyes, and giving a deep sob, in which she<br>
seemed to expire in an agony of bliss.<br>
<br>
When he had finished his stroke, and got from off her, she lay still<br>
without the least motion, breathless, as it should seem, with pleasure.<br>
He replaced her again breadth-wise on the couch, unable to sit up, with<br>
her thighs open, between which I could observe a kind of white liquid,<br>
like froth, hanging about the outward lips of that recently opened<br>
wound, which now glowed with a deeper red. Presently she gets up, and<br>
throwing her arms round him, seemed far undelighted with the trial he<br>
had put her to, to judge, at least by the fondness with which she eyed,<br>
and hung upon him.<br>
<br>
For my part, I will not pretend to describe what I felt over me during<br>
this scene; but from that instant, adieu all fears of what man can do<br>
unto me! they were now changed into such ardent desires, such<br>
ungovernable longings, that I could have by the sleeve, and offered him<br>
the bauble, which I now imagined the loss of would be a gain I could<br>
not too soon procure myself.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be, who had more experience, and to whom such sights were not so<br>
new, could not however, be unmoved at so warm a scene; and drawing me<br>
away softly from the peeping hole, for fear of being overheard, guided<br>
me as the door as possible, all passive and obedient to her least<br>
signals.<br>
<br>
Here was no room either to sit or lie, but making me stand with my back<br>
towards the door, she lifted up my petticoats, and with her busy<br>
fingers fell to visit and explore that part of me, where I was<br>
perfectly sick and ready to die with desire; that the bare touch of her<br>
finger, in that critical place, had the effect of a fire to a train,<br>
and her hand instantly made her sensible to what a pitch I was wound<br>
up, and melted by the sight she had thus procured me. Satisfied then<br>
with her success, in allaying a heat that would have made me impatient<br>
of seeing the continuation of the transactions between our amourous<br>
couple, she brought me again to the crevice, so favourable to our<br>
curiosity.<br>
<br>
We had certainly been but a few instants away from it, and yet on our<br>
return we saw everything in good forwardness for recommencing the<br>
tender hostilities.<br>
<br>
The young foreigner was sitting down, fronting us, on the coach, with<br>
Polly upon one knee, who had her arms round his neck, whilst the<br>
extreme whiteness of her skin was not undelightfully contrasted by the<br>
smooth glossy brown of her lover&rsquo;s.<br>
<br>
But who could count the fierce, unnumbered kisses given and taken? In<br>
which I could often discover their mouths were double tongued, and<br>
seemed to favour the mutual insertion with the greatest gust and<br>
delight.<br>
<br>
In the meantime, his red-headed champion, that had so lately fled the<br>
pit, quelled and abashed, was now recovered to the top of his<br>
condition, perked and crested up between Polly&rsquo;s thighs, who was not<br>
wanting, on her part, to coax and keep it in good humour, stroking it,<br>
with her head down, and receiving even its velvet tip between the lips<br>
of not its proper mouth: whether it was to render it more glib and easy<br>
of entrance, I could not tell; but it had such an effect, that the<br>
young gentleman seemed by his eyes, that sparkled with more excited<br>
lustre, and his inflamed countenance, to receive increase of pleasure.<br>
He got up, and taking Polly in his arms, embraced her, and said<br>
something too softly for me to hear, leading her withal to the foot of<br>
the couch, and taking delight to slap her thighs and posteriors with<br>
that stiff sinew of his, which hit them with a spring that he gave it<br>
with his hand, and made them resound again, but her about as much as he<br>
meant to hurt her, for she seemed to have as frolic a taste as himself.<br>
<br>
But guess my surprise, when I saw the lazy young rogue lie down on his<br>
back, and gently pull down Polly upon him, who giving way to his<br>
humour, stradled, and with her hands conducted her blind favourite to<br>
the right place; and following her impulse, ran directly upon the<br>
flaming point of this weapon of pleasure, which she staked herself<br>
upon, up pierced, and infixed to the extremest hair breadth of it: thus<br>
she sat on him a few instants, enjoying and relishing her situation,<br>
whilst he toyed with her provoking breasts. Sometimes she would stoop<br>
to meet his kiss: but presently the sting of pleasure spurred them up<br>
to fiercer action; then began the storm of heaves, which, from the<br>
undermost combatant, were thrust at the same time, he crossing his<br>
hands over her, and drawing her home to him with a sweet violence: the<br>
inverted strokes of anvil over hammer soon brought on the critical<br>
period, in which all the signs of a close conspiring extasy informed us<br>
of the point they were at.<br>
<br>
For me, I could bear to see no more; I was so overcome, so inflamed at<br>
the second part of the same play, that, mad to an intolerable degree, I<br>
hugged, I clasped Ph&oelig;be, as if she had wherewithal to relieve me.<br>
Pleased however with, and pitying the taking she could feel me in, she<br>
drew towards the door, and opening it softly as she could, we both got<br>
off undiscovered, and reconducted me to my own room, where, unable to<br>
keep my legs, in the agitation I was in, I instantly threw myself down<br>
on the bed, where I lay transported, though ashamed at what I felt.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be lay down by me, and asked me archly, &ldquo;if, now that I had seen the<br>
enemy, and fully considered him, I was still afraid of him? or did I<br>
think I could come to a close engagement with him?&rdquo; To all which, not a<br>
word on my side; I sighed, and could scarcely breathe. She takes hold<br>
of my hand, and having rolled up her own petticoats, forced it half<br>
strivingly, towards those parts, where, now grown more knowing, I<br>
missed the main object of my wishes; and finding not even the shadow of<br>
what I wanted, where every thing was so flat, or so hollow, in the<br>
vexation I was in at it. I should have withdrawn my hand, but for fear<br>
of disobliging her. Abandoning it then entirely to her management, she<br>
made use of it as she thought proper, to procure herself rather the<br>
shadow than the substance of any pleasure. For my part, I now pined for<br>
more solid food, and promised tacitly to myself that I would not be put<br>
off much longer with this foolery of woman to woman, of Mrs. Brown did<br>
not soon provide me with the essential specific. In short, I had all<br>
the air of not being able to wait the arrival of my lord B&mdash;&mdash;, though he<br>
was now expected in a very fews days: nor did I wait for him, for love<br>
itself took charge of the disposal of me, in spite of interest, or<br>
gross lust.<br>
<br>
It was now two days after the closet scene, that I got up about six in<br>
the morning, and leaving my bedfellow fast asleep, stole down, with no<br>
other thought than of taking a little fresh air in a small garden,<br>
which our back parlour opened into, and from which my confinement<br>
debarred me, at the times company came to my house; but now sleep and<br>
silence reigned all over it.<br>
<br>
I opened the parlour door, and well surprised was I at seeing, by the<br>
side of a fire half-out, a young gentleman in the old lady&rsquo;s elbow<br>
chair, with his legs laid upon another, fast asleep, and left there by<br>
his thoughtless companions, who had drank him down, and then went off<br>
with every one but his mistress, whilst he stayed behind by the<br>
courtesy of the old matron, who would not disturb or turn him out in<br>
that condition at one in the morning; and beds, it is more than<br>
probable there were none to spare. On the table still remained the<br>
punch bowl and glasses, stewed about in their usual disorder after a<br>
drunken revel.<br>
<br>
But when I drew nearer, to view the sleeping estray, heavens! what a<br>
sight! No! term of years, no turn of fortune could ever eraze the<br>
lightninglike impression his form made on me. Yes! dearest object of my<br>
earliest passion, I command for ever the remembrance of thy first<br>
appearance to my ravished eyes, it calls thee up, present; and I see<br>
thee now.<br>
<br>
Figure to yourself, Madam, fair stripling between eighteen and<br>
nineteen, with his head reclined on one of the sides of the chair, his<br>
hair disordered curls, irregularly shading a face, on which all the<br>
roseate bloom of youth and all the manly graces conspired to fix my eye<br>
sand heart; even the languour and paleness of his face, in which the<br>
momentary triumph of the lily over the rose was owing to the excesses<br>
of the night, gave an inexpressible sweetness to the finest features<br>
imaginable: his eyes, closed in sleep, displayed the meeting edges of<br>
their lids beautifully bordered with long eye-lashes; over which no<br>
pencil could have described two more regular arches than those that<br>
graced his forehead, which was high, perfectly white and smooth; then a<br>
pair of vermilion lips, pouting and swelling to the touch, as if a bee<br>
had freshly stung them, seemed to challenge me to get the gloves off<br>
this lovely sleeper, had not the modesty and respect, which in both<br>
sexes are inseparable from a true passion, checked my impulses.<br>
<br>
But on seeing his shirt collar unbottoned, and bosom whiter than a<br>
drift of snow, the pleasure of considering it could not bribe me to<br>
lengthen it, at the hazard of a health that began to be my life&rsquo;s<br>
concern. Love, that made me timid, taught me to be tender too: with a<br>
trembling hand I took hold of one of his, and waking him as gently as<br>
possible, he started, and looking, at first a little wildly, said with<br>
a voice that sent its harmonious sound to my heart: &ldquo;Pray, child,<br>
what-a-clock is it?&rdquo; I told him, and added that he might catch cold if<br>
he slept longer with his breast open in the cool of the morning air. On<br>
this he thanked me with a sweetness perfectly agreeing with that of his<br>
features and eyes; the last now broad open, and eagerly surveying me,<br>
carried the surightly fires they sparkled with directly to my heart.<br>
<br>
It seems, that having drank too freely before he came upon the rake<br>
with some of his young companions, he had put himself out of a<br>
condition to go through all the weapons with them, and crown the night<br>
with a getting a mistress; so that seeing me in a loose undress, he did<br>
not doubt but I was one of the misses of the house, sent in to repair<br>
his loss of time; but though he seized that notion, and a very obvious<br>
one it was, without hesitation, yet, whether my figure made a more than<br>
ordinary impression on him, or whether it was his natural politeness,<br>
he addressed me in a manner far from rude, though still on the foot of<br>
one of the house pliers come to amuse him; and giving me the first kiss<br>
that I ever relished from man in my life, asked me if I could favour<br>
him with my company, assuring me that he would make it worth my while:<br>
but had not even new-born love, that true refiner of lust, opposed so<br>
sudden a surrender, the fear of being surprised by the house was a<br>
sufficient bar to my compliance.<br>
<br>
I told him then, in a tone set by love itself, that for reasons I had<br>
not time to explain to him. I could not stay with him, and might even<br>
ever see him again, with a sigh at these words, which broke from the<br>
bottom of my heart. My conqueror, who, as he afterwards told me, had<br>
been struck with my appearance, and liked me as much as he could think<br>
of liking any one in my supposed way of life, asked me briskly at once,<br>
if I would be kept by him, and that he would take a lodging for me<br>
directly, and relieve me from any engagements he presumed I might be<br>
under to the house.<br>
<br>
Rash, sudden, undigested, even dangerous as this offer might be from a<br>
perfect stranger, and that stranger a giddy boy, the prodigious love I<br>
was struck with for him, had put a charm into every objection: I not<br>
resisting, and blinded me to every objection; I could, at that instant,<br>
have died for him: think if I could resist an invitation to live with<br>
him! Thus my heart, beating strong to the proposal, dictated my answer,<br>
after scarce a minute&rsquo;s pause, that I would accept of his offer, and<br>
make my escape to him in what way he pleased, and that I would be<br>
entirely at his disposal, let it be good or bad. I have often since<br>
wondered that so great an easiness did not disgust him, or make me too<br>
cheap in his eyes, but my fate had so appointed it, that in his fears<br>
of the hazzard of the town, he had been some time looking out for a<br>
girl to take into keeping, and my person happening to hit his fancy, it<br>
was by one of those miracles reserved to love, that we struck the<br>
bargain in the instant, which we sealed by an exchange of kisses, that<br>
the hopes of a more uninterrupted enjoyment engaged him to content<br>
himself with.<br>
<br>
Never, however, did dear youth carry in his head more wherewith to<br>
justify the turning of a girl&rsquo;s head, and making her set all<br>
consequences at defiance, for the sake of following a gallant.<br>
<br>
For, besides all the perfections of manly beauty which were assembled<br>
in his form, he had an air of neatness and gentility, certain smartness<br>
in the carriage and port of his head, that yet more distinguished him;<br>
his eyes were sprightly and full of meaning; his looks had in them<br>
something at once sweet and commanding; his complexion out-bloomed the<br>
lovely coloured rose, whilst its inimitable tender vivid glow clearly<br>
saved it from the reproach of wanting life, of raw and dough-like,<br>
which is commonly made of those so extremely fair as he was.<br>
<br>
Our little plan was, that I should get out about seven the next morning<br>
(which I could readily promise, as I knew where to get the key of the<br>
street door) and he would wait at the end of the street with a coach to<br>
convey me safe off; after which, we would send, and clear any debt<br>
incurred by my stay at Mrs. Brown&rsquo;s, who, he only judged, in gross,<br>
might not care to part with one, he thought, so fit to draw custom to<br>
the house.<br>
<br>
I then just hinted to him not to mention in the house his having seen<br>
such a person as me, for reasons I would explain to him more at<br>
leisure. And then, for fear of miscarrying, by being seen together, I<br>
tore myself from him with a bleeding heart, and stole up softly to my<br>
room, where I found Ph&oelig;be still fast asleep, and hurrying off my few<br>
clothes, lay down by her, with a mixture of joy and anxiety, that may<br>
be easier conceived than expressed.<br>
<br>
The risks of Mrs. Brown&rsquo;s discovering my purpose, of disappointments,<br>
misery, ruin, all vanished before this new-kindled flame. The seeing,<br>
the touching, the being, if but for a night, with this idol of my fond<br>
virgin heart, appeared to me a happiness above the purchase of my<br>
liberty or life. He might use me ill, let him: he was the master,<br>
happy, too happy, even to receive death at so dear a hand.<br>
<br>
To this purpose were the reflections of the whole day, of which every<br>
minute seemed to me a little eternity. How often did I visit the clock!<br>
nay, was tempted to advance the tedious hand, as if that would have<br>
advanced the time with it! Had those of the house had the least<br>
observations on me, they must have remarked something extraordinary<br>
from the discomposure I could not help betraying; especially when at<br>
dinner mention was made of the charmingest youth having been there, and<br>
stayed breakfast. &ldquo;Oh! he was such a beauty!... I should have died for<br>
him!... they would pull caps for him!...&rdquo; and the like fooleries;<br>
which, however, was throwing oil on a fire I was sorely put to it to<br>
smother the blaze of.<br>
<br>
The fluctuations of my mind, the whole day, produced one good effect:<br>
which was, that, through mere fatigue, I slept tolerably well till five<br>
in the morning, when I got up, and having dressed myself, waited, under<br>
the double tortures of fear and impatience, for the appointed hour. It<br>
came at last, the dear, critical, dangerous hour came; and now,<br>
supported only by the courage love lent me, I ventured, a tip-toe, down<br>
stairs, leaving my box behind, for fear of being surprized with it in<br>
going out.<br>
<br>
I got to the street door, the key whereof was always laid on the chair<br>
by our bed side, in trust with Ph&oelig;be, who having not the least<br>
suspicion of my entertaining any design to go from them (nor, indeed,<br>
had I, but the day before), made no reserve or concealment of it from<br>
me. I opened the door with great ease; love, that emboldened, protected<br>
me too: and now, got safe into the street, I saw my new guardian angel<br>
waiting at a coach door, ready open. How I got to him I know not: I<br>
suppose I flew; but I was in the coach in a trice, and he by the side<br>
of me, with his arms clasped round me, and giving me the kiss of<br>
welcome. The coachman had his orders, and drove to them.<br>
<br>
My eyes were instantly filled with tears, but tears of the most<br>
delicious delight; to find myself in the arms of that beauteous youth,<br>
was a rapture that my little hear swam in; past or future were equally<br>
out of the question with me; the present was as much as all my powers<br>
of life were sufficient to bear the transport of, without fainting. Nor<br>
were the most tender embraces, the most soothing expressions wanting on<br>
his side, to assure me of his love, and of never giving me cause to<br>
repent the bold step I had taken, in throwing myself thus entirely upon<br>
his honour and generosity. But, alas! this was no merit in me, for I<br>
was drove to it by a passion too impetuous for me to resist, and, I did<br>
what I did, because I could not help it.<br>
<br>
In an instant, for time was now annihilated with me, we were landed at<br>
a public house in Chelsea, hospitably commodious for the reception of<br>
duet parties of pleasure, where a breakfast of chocolate was prepared<br>
for us.<br>
<br>
An old jolly stager, who kept it, and understood life perfectly well,<br>
breakfasted with us, and leering archly at me, gave us both joy, and<br>
said, &ldquo;we were well paired, i&rsquo; faith! that a great many gentlemen and<br>
ladies used his house, but he had never seen a handsomer couple... he<br>
was sure I was a fresh piece... I looked so country, so innocent! well<br>
my spouse was a lucky man!...&rdquo; all which, common landlord&rsquo;s cant, not<br>
only pleased and soothed me, but helped to diver my confusion at being<br>
with my new sovereign, whom, the minute approached, I began to fear to<br>
be alone with: a timidity which true love had a greater share in than<br>
even maiden bashful-ness.<br>
<br>
I wished, I doated, I could have died for him; and yet, I know not how,<br>
or why I dreaded the point which had been the object of my fiercest<br>
wishes; my pulses beat fears, amidst a flush of the warmest desires.<br>
This struggle of the passions, however, this conflict betwixt modesty<br>
and lovesick longings, made me burst again into tears; which he took,<br>
as he had done before, only for the remains of concern and emotion at<br>
the suddenness of my change of condition, in committing myself to his<br>
care; and, in consequence of that idea, did and said all that he<br>
thought would most comfort and re-inspirit me.<br>
<br>
After breakfast, Charles (the dear familiar name I must take the<br>
liberty henceforward to distinguish my Adonis by), with a smile full of<br>
meaning, took me gently by the hand, and said: &ldquo;Come, my dear, I will<br>
show you a room that commands a fine prospect over some gardens&rdquo;; and<br>
without waiting for an answer, in which he relieved me extremely, he<br>
led me up into a chamber, airy and lightsome, where all seeing of<br>
prospects was out of the question, except that of a bed, which had all<br>
the air of recommending the room to him.<br>
<br>
Charles had just slipped the bolt of the door, and running, caught me<br>
in his arms, and lifting me from the ground, with his lips glued to<br>
mine, bore me trembling, panting, dying with soft fears and tender<br>
wishes, to the bed; where his impatience would not suffer him to<br>
undress me, more than just unpinning my handkerchief and gowns, and<br>
unlacing my stays.<br>
<br>
My bosom was now bare, and rising in the warmest throbs, presented to<br>
his sight and feeling the firm hard swell of a pair of young breast,<br>
such as may be imagined of a girl not sixteen, fresh out of the<br>
country, and never before handled: but even their pride, whiteness,<br>
fashion, pleasing resistance to the touch, could not bribe his restless<br>
hands from roving; but, giving them the loose, my petticoats and shift<br>
were soon taken up, and their stronger center of attraction laid open<br>
to their tender invasion. My fears, however, made me mechanically close<br>
my thighs; but the very touch of his hand insinuated between them,<br>
disclosed them and opened a way for the main attack.<br>
<br>
In the mean time, I lay fairly exposed to the examination of his eyes<br>
and hands, quiet and unresisting; which confirmed him the opinion he<br>
proceeded so cavalierly upon, that I was no novice in these matters,<br>
since he had taken me out of a common bawdy house, nor had I said one<br>
thing to prepossess him of my virginity; and if I had, he would sooner<br>
have believed that I took him for a cully that would swallow such an<br>
improbability, than that I was still mistress of that darling treasure,<br>
that hidden mine, so eagerly sought after by the men, and which they<br>
never dig for, but to destroy.<br>
<br>
Being now too high wound up to bear a delay, he unbuttoned, and drawing<br>
out the engine of love assaults, drove it currently, as at a ready made<br>
breach... Then! then! for the first time, did I feel that stiff<br>
horn-hard gristle, battering against the tender part; but imagine to<br>
yourself his surprise, when he found, after several vigorous pushes,<br>
which hurt me extremely, that he made not the least impression.<br>
<br>
I complained, but tenderly complained: &ldquo;I could not bear it... indeed<br>
he hurt me!...&rdquo; Still he thought no more, than that being so young, the<br>
largeness of his machine (for few men could dispute size with him) made<br>
all the difficulty; and that possibly I had not been enjoyed by any so<br>
advantageously made in that part as himself: for still, that my virgin<br>
flower was yet un-cropped, never entered into his head, and he would<br>
have thought it idling with time and words, to have questioned me upon<br>
it.<br>
<br>
He tried again, still no admittance, still no penetration; but he had<br>
hurt me yet more, while my extreme love made me bear extreme pain,<br>
almost without a groan. At length, after repeated fruitless trials, he<br>
lay down panting by me, kissed my falling tears, and asked me tenderly<br>

better from other than I did from him?&rdquo; I answered, with a simplicity<br>
framed to persuade, that he was the first man that ever served me so.<br>
Truth is powerful, and it is not always that we do not believe what we<br>
eagerly wish.<br>
<br>
Charles, already disposed by the evidence, of his senses to think my<br>
pretences to virginity not entirely apocryphal, smothers me with<br>
kisses, begs me, in the name of love, to have a little patience, and<br>
that he wilt be as tender of hurting me as he would be of himself..<br>
<br>
Alas! it was enough I knew his pleasure to submit joyfully to him,<br>
whatever pain I foresaw it would cost, me.<br>
<br>
He now resumes his attempts in more form: first, he put one of the<br>
pillows under me, to give the blank of his aim a more favourable<br>
elevation, and another Under my head, in ease of it; then spreading my<br>
thighs, and placing himself standing betwen them, made them rest upon<br>
his; applying then the point of his machine to the slit, into which he<br>
sought entrance, it was so small, he could scarce assure himself of its<br>
being rightly pointed. He looks, he feels, and satisfies himself: there<br>
driving on with fury, its prodigious stiffness, thus impacted,<br>
wedgelike, breaks the union of those parts, and gained him just the<br>
insertion of the tip of it, lip deep; which being sensible of, he<br>
improved his advantage, and following well his stroke, in a straight<br>
line, forcibly deepens his penetration; but put me to such intolerable<br>
pain, from the separation of the sides of that soft passage by a hard<br>
thick body, I could have screamed out; but, as I was unwilling to alarm<br>
the house, I held in my breath, and crammed my petticoat, which was;<br>
turned up over my face, into my mouth, and bit it through in the agony.<br>
At length, the tender texture of that tract giving way to such fierce<br>
tearing and rending, he pierced something further into me: and now,<br>
outrageous and no longer his own master, but borne headlong away by the<br>
fury and over-mettle of that member, now exerting itself with a kind of<br>
native rage, he breaks in, carries all before him, and one violent<br>
merciless lunge, sent it, imbrued, and reeking with virgin blood, up to<br>
the very hilt in me... Then! then all my resolution deserted me: I<br>
screamed out, and fainted away with the sharpness of the pain; and, as<br>
he told me afterwards, on his drawing out, when emission was over with<br>
him, my thighs were instantly all in a stream of blood, that flowed<br>
from the wounded torn passage.<br>
<br>
When I recovered my senses, I found myself undressed and a-bed, in the<br>
arms of the sweet relenting murderer of my virginity, who hung mourning<br>
tenderly over me, and holding in his hand a cordial, which, coming from<br>
the still dear author of so much pain, I could not refuse; my eyes,<br>
however, moistened with tears, and languishingly turned upon him,<br>
seemed to reproach him with his cruelty, and ask him, if such were the<br>
rewards of love. But Charles, to whom I was now infinitely endeared by<br>
his complete triumph over a maidenhead, where he so little expected to<br>
find one, in tenderness to that pain which he had put me to, in<br>
procuring himself the height of pleasure, smothered his exultation, and<br>
employed himself with so much sweetness, so much warmth, to sooth, to<br>
caress, and comfort me in my soft complainings, which breathed, indeed,<br>
more love than resentment, that I presently drowned all sense of pain<br>
in the pleasure of seeing him, of thinking that I belonged to him: he<br>
who was now the absolute disposer of my happiness, and, in one word, my<br>
fate.<br>
<br>
The sore was, however, too tender, the wound too bleeding fresh, for<br>
Charles&rsquo;s good-nature to put my patience presently to another trial;<br>
but as I could not stir, or walk a-cross the room, he ordered the<br>
dinner to be brought to the bed side, where it could not be otherwise<br>
than my getting down the wing of a fowl, and two or three glasses of<br>
wine, since it was my adored youth who both served, and urged them on<br>
me, with that sweet irresistible authority with which love had invested<br>
him over me.<br>
<br>
After dinner, and everything but the wine was taken away, Charles very<br>
impudently asks a leave, he might read the grant of in my eyes, to come<br>
to bed to me, and accordingly falls to undressing; which I could not<br>
see the progress of without strange emotions of fear and pleasure.<br>
<br>
He is now in bed with me the first time, and in broad day; but when<br>
thrusting up his own shirt and my shift, he laid his naked glowing body<br>
to mine... oh insupportable delight! oh! superhuman rapture! what pain<br>
could stand before a pleasure so transporting? I felt no more the smart<br>
of my wounds below; but, curling round him like the tendril of a vine,<br>
as if I feared any part of him should be untouched or unpressed by me,<br>
I returned his strenuous embraces and kisses with a fervour and gust<br>
only known to true love, and which mere lust never rise to.<br>
<br>
Yes, even at this time, that all the tyranny of the passions is fully<br>
over, and that my veins roll no longer but a cold tranquil stream, the<br>
remembrance of those passages that most affected me in my youth, still<br>
cheers and refreshes me; let me proceed then. My beauteous youth was<br>
now glued to me in all the folds and twists that we could make our<br>
bodies meet in; when, no longer able to rein in the fierceness of<br>
refreshed desires, he gives his steed the head, and gently insinuating<br>
his thighs between mine, stopping my mouth with kisses of humid fire,<br>
makes a fresh eruption, and renewing his thrusts, pierces, tears, and<br>
forces his way up the torn tender folds, that yielded him admission<br>
with a smart little less severe that when the breach was first made I<br>
stifled, however, my cries, and bore him with the passive fortitude of<br>
an heroine; soon his thrusts, more and more furious, cheeks flushed<br>
with a deeper scarlet, his eyes turned up in the fervent fit, some<br>
dying sighs, and an agonizing shudder, announced the approaches of that<br>
ecstatic pleasure, I was yet in too much pain to come in for my share<br>
of.<br>
<br>
Nor was it till after a few enjoyments had numbed and blunted the sense<br>
of the smart, and given me to feel the titillating inspersion of<br>
balsamic sweets, drew from me the delicious return, and brought down<br>
all my passion, that I arrived at excess of pleasure through excess of<br>
pain. But, when successive engagements had broke and inured me, I began<br>
to enter into the true unalloyed relish of that pleasure of pleasures,<br>
when the warm gush darts through all the ravished inwards; what floods<br>
of bliss! what melting transports! what agonies of delight! too fierce,<br>
too mighty for nature to sustain?... well has she therefore, no doubt<br>
provided the relief of a delicious momentary dissolution, the<br>
approaches of which are intimated by a dear delirium, a sweet thrill,<br>
on the point of emitting those liquid sweets, in which enjoyment itself<br>
is drowned, when one gives the languishing stretch out, and die at the<br>
discharge.<br>
<br>
How often, when the rage and tumult of my senses had subsided, after<br>
the melting flow, have I, in a tender meditation, asked myself cooly<br>
the question, if it was in nature for any of its creatures to be so<br>
happy as I was? Or, what were all fears of the consequence, put in the<br>
scale of one night&rsquo;s enjoyment, of any thing so transcendently the<br>
taste of my eyes and heart, as that delicious, fond, matchless youth.<br>
<br>
Thus we spent the whole afternoon, till supper time in a continued<br>
circle of love delights, kissing, turtle-billing, toying, and all the<br>
rest of the feast. At length, supper was served in, before which<br>
Charles had, for I do not know what reason, slipped his clothes on; and<br>
sitting down by the bed side, we made table and tablecloth of the bed<br>
and sheets, whilst he suffered nobody to attend or serve but himself.<br>
He ate with a very good appetite, and seemed charmed to see me eat. For<br>
my part, I was so transported with the comparison of the delights I now<br>
swam in, with the insipidity of all my past scenes of life, that I<br>
thought them sufficiently cheap, at even the price of my ruin, or the<br>
risk of their not lasting. The present possession was all my little<br>
head could find room for.<br>
<br>
We lay together that night, when, after playing repeated prizes of<br>
pleasure, nature, overspent and satisfied, gave us up to the arms of<br>
sleep: those of my dear youth encircled me, the consciousness of which<br>
made even that sleep more delicious.<br>
<br>
Late in the morning I waked, first; and observing my lover slept<br>
profoundly, softly disengaged myself from his arms, scarcely daring to<br>
breathe, for fear of shortening his repose; my cap, my hair, my shift,<br>
were all in disorder, from the rufflings I had undergone; and I took<br>
this opportunity to adjust and set them as well as I could: whilst,<br>
every now and then, looking at the sleeping youth, with inconceivable<br>
fondness and delight, and reflecting on all the pain he had put me to,<br>
tacitly owned that the pleasure had overpaid me for my sufferings.<br>
<br>
It was then broad day. I was sitting up in the bed, the clothes of<br>
which were all tossed, or rolled off, by the unquietness of our<br>
motions, from the sultry heat of the weather; nor could I refuse myself<br>
a pleasure that solicited me so irresistibly, as this fair occasion of<br>
feasting my sight with all those treasures of youthful beauty I had<br>
enjoyed, and which lay now almost entirely naked, his shirt being<br>
trussed up in a perfect wisp, which the warmth of the season and room<br>
made me easy about the consequence of. I hung over him enamoured<br>
indeed! and devoured all his naked charms with only two eyes, when I<br>
could have wished them at least an hundred for the fuller enjoyment of<br>
the gaze.<br>
<br>
Oh! could I paint his figure as I see it now, still present to my<br>
transported imagination! a whole length of an all perfect manly beauty<br>
in full view. Think of a face without a fault, glowing with all the<br>
opening bloom and verdant freshness of an age, in which beauty is of<br>
either sex, and which the first down over his upper lip scarce began to<br>
distinguish.<br>
<br>
The parting of the double ruby pout of his lips seemed to exhale an air<br>
sweeter and purer than what it drew in: ah! what violence did it not<br>
cost me to refrain the so tempted kiss!<br>
<br>
Then a neck exquisitely turned, graved behind and on the sides with<br>
fais hair, playing freely in natural ringlets, connected his head to a<br>
body of the most perfect form, and of the most vigorous contexture, in<br>
which all the strength of manhood was concealed, and softened to<br>
appearance by the delicacy of his complexion, the smoothness of his<br>
skin, and the plumpness of his flesh.<br>
<br>
The platform of his snow white bosom, that was laid out in a manly<br>
proportion, presented, on the vermilion summit of each pap, the idea of<br>
a rose about to blow.<br>
<br>
Nor did his shirt hinder me from observing the symmetry of his limbs,<br>
that exactness of shape, in the fall of it towards the loins, where the<br>
waist ends and the rounding swell of the hips commences; where the<br>
skin, sleek, smooth, and dazzling white, burnishes on; the stretch-over<br>
firm, plump, ripe flesh, that crimped&rsquo; and ran into dimples at the<br>
least pressure, or that the touch could not rest upon, but slid over on<br>
the surface of the most polished ivory.<br>
<br>
His thighs, finely fashioned, and with a florid glossy roundness,<br>
gradually tapering away to the knees, seemed pillars worthy to support<br>
that beauteous frame at the bottom of which I could not, without some<br>
remains of terror, some tender emotions too, fix my eyes on that<br>
terrible machine, which had, not long before, with such fury broke<br>
into, torn, and almost ruined those soft, tender parts of mine, that<br>
had not yet done smarting with the effects of its rage; but behold it<br>
now! crest fallen, reclining its half-caped vermilion head over one of<br>
his thighs, quiet, pliant, and to all appearances incapable of the<br>
mischiefs and cruelty it had committed. Then the beautiful growth of<br>
the hair, in short and soft curls round its roots, its whiteness,<br>
branched veins, the supple softness of the shaft, as it lay<br>
foreshortened, rolled and shrunk up into a squat thickness, languid,<br>
and borne up from between his thighs, by its globular appendage, that<br>
wondrous treasure bag of nature&rsquo;s sweets, which revelled round, and<br>
pursed up in the only wrinkles that are known to please, perfected the<br>
prospect, and altogether formed the most interesting moving picture in<br>
nature, and surely infinitely superior to those nudities furnished by<br>
the painters, statuaries, or any art, which are purchased at immense<br>
prices; whilst the sight of them in actual life is scarce sovereignly<br>
tasted by any but the few whom nature has endowed with a fire of<br>
imagination, warmly pointed by a truth of judgment to the spring-head,<br>
the originals of beauty, of nature&rsquo;s unequalled composition, above all<br>
the imitations of art, or the reach of wealth to pay their price.<br>
<br>
But every thing must have an end. A motion made by this angelic youth,<br>
in the listlessness of goingoff sleep, replaced his shirt and the bed<br>
clothes in a posture that shut up that treasury from longer view.<br>
<br>
I lay down then, and carrying my hands to that part of me in which the<br>
objects just seen had begun to raise a mutiny, that prevailed over the<br>
smart of them, my fingers now opened themselves an easy passage; but<br>
long I had not time to consider the wide difference there, between the<br>
maid and the now finished woman, before Charles waked, and turning<br>
towards me, kindly enquired how I had rested? and, scarce giving me<br>
time to answer, imprinted on my lips one of his burning rapture kisses,<br>
which darted a flame to my heart, that from thence radiated to every<br>
part of me; and presently, as if he had proudly meant revenge for the<br>
survey I had smuggled of all his naked beauties, he spurns off the bed<br>
clothes, and trussing up my shift as high as it would go, took his turn<br>
to feast his eyes on all the gifts nature had bestowed on my person;<br>
his busy hands, too, ranged intemperately over every part of me. The<br>
delicious austerity and hardness of my yet unripe budding breasts, the<br>
whiteness and firmness of my flesh, the freshness and regularity of my<br>
features, the harmony of my limbs, all seemed to confirm him in his<br>
satisfaction with his bargain; but when curious to explore the havock<br>
he had made in the centre of his over fierce attack, he not only<br>
directed his hands there, but with a pillow put under, placed me<br>
favourably for his wanton purpose of inspection. Then, who can express<br>
the fire his eyes glistened, his hands glowed with! whilst sighs of<br>
pleasure, and tender broken exclamations, were all the praises he could<br>
utter. By this time his machine, stiffly risen at me, gave me to see it<br>
in its highest state and bravery. He feels it himself, seems pleased at<br>
its condition, and, smiling loves and graces, seizes one of my hands,<br>
and carries it, with gentle compulsion, to this pride of nature, and<br>
its richest master piece.<br>
<br>
I, struggling faintly, could not help feeling what I could not grasp, a<br>
column of the whitest ivory, beautifully streaked with blue veins, and<br>
carrying, fully un-capt, a head of the liveliest vermilion: no horn<br>
could be harder or stiffer; yet no velvet more smooth or delicious to<br>
the touch. Presently he guided my hand lower, to that part in which<br>
nature, and pleasure keep their stores in concert, so aptly fastened<br>
and hung on to the root of their first instrument and minister, that<br>
not improperly he might be styled their purse-bearer too: there he made<br>
me feel distinctly, through their soft cover, the contents, a pair of<br>
roundish balls, that seemed to play within, and elude all pressure, but<br>
the tenderest, from without.<br>
<br>
But now this visit of my soft, warm hand, in those so sensible parts,<br>
had put every thing into such ungovernable fury, disdaining all further<br>
preluding, and taking advantage of my commodious posture, he made the<br>
storm fall where I scarce patiently expected, and where he was sure to<br>
lay it: presently, then, I felt the stiff intersection betwen the<br>
yielding, divided lips of the wound, now open for life; where the<br>
narrowness no longer put me to intolerable pain, and afforded my lover<br>
no more difficulty than what heightened his pleasure, in the strict<br>
embrace of that tender, warm sheath, round the instrument it was so<br>
delicately adjusted to, and which now cased home, so gorged me with<br>
pleasure, that it perfectly suffocated me and took away my breath; then<br>
the killing thrusts! the unnumbered kisses! every one of which was a<br>
joy inexpressible; and that joy lost in a crowd of yet greater blisses!<br>
But this was a disorder too violent in nature to last long: the<br>
vessels, so stirred and intensely heated, soon boiled over, and for<br>
that time put out the fire; meanwhile all this dalliance and disport<br>
had so far consumed the morning, that it became a kind of necessity to<br>
lay breakfast and dinner into one.<br>
<br>
In our calmer intervals Charles gave the following account of himself,<br>
every tittle of which was true. He was the only son of a father, who,<br>
having a small post in the revenue, rather overlived his income, and<br>
had given this young gentleman a very slender education: no profession<br>
had he bred him up to, but designed to provide for him in the army, by<br>
purchasing him an ensign&rsquo;s commission, that is to say, provided he<br>
could raise the money, or procure it by interest, either of which<br>
clauses was rather to be wished than hoped for by him. On no better a<br>
plan, however, had his improvident father suffered this youth, a youth<br>
of great promise, to run up to the age of manhood, or near it at least,<br>
in next to idleness; and had, besides, taken no sort of pains to give<br>
him even the common premonitions against the vices of the town, and the<br>
dangers of all sorts which wait the unexperienced and unwary in it. He<br>
lived at home, and at discretion with his father, who himself kept a<br>
mistress; and for the rest, provided Charles did not ask him for money,<br>
he was indolently kind to him: he might lie out when he pleased, any<br>
excuse would serve, and even his reprimands were so slight, that they<br>
carried with them rather an air of connivance at the fault, than any<br>
serious control or constraint. But, to supply his calls for money,<br>
Charles, whose mother was dead, had, by her side, a grandmother, who<br>
doated upon him. She had a considerable annuity to live on, and very<br>
regularly parted with every shilling she could spare, to this darling<br>
of her&rsquo;s, to the no little heart-burn of his father; who was vexed, not<br>
that she, by this means, fed his son&rsquo;s extravagance, but that she<br>
preferred Charles to himself; and we shall too soon see what a fatal<br>
turn such a mercenary jealousy could operate on the breast of a father.<br>
<br>
Charles was, however, by the means of his grandmother&rsquo;s lavish<br>
fondness, very sufficiently enabled to keep a mistress, so easily<br>
contented as my love made me; and my good fortune, for such I must ever<br>
call it, threw me in his way, in the manner above related, just as he<br>
was on the look-out for one.<br>
<br>
As to temper, the even sweetness of it made him seem born for domestic<br>
happiness: tender, naturally polite, and gentle-manner&rsquo;d; it could<br>
never be his fault, if ever jars, or animosities ruffled a calm he was<br>
so qualified every way to maintain or restore. Without those great or<br>
shining qualities that constitute a genius, or are fit to make a noise<br>
in the world, he had all those humble ones that compose the softer<br>
social merit: plain common sense, set off with every grace of modesty<br>
and good nature, made him, if not admired, what is much happier:<br>
universally beloved and esteemed. But, as nothing but the beauties of<br>
his person had at first attracted my regard and fixed my passion,<br>
neither was I then a judge of the internal merit, which I had<br>
afterwards full occasion to discover, and which, perhaps, in that<br>
season of giddiness and levity, would have touched my heart very<br>
little, had it been lodged in a person less the delight of my eyes, and<br>
idol of my senses. But to return to our situation.<br>
<br>
After dinner, which we ate a-bed in most voluptuous disorder, Charles<br>
got up, and taking a passionate leave of me for a few hours, went to<br>
town, where concerting matters with a young sharp lawyer, they went<br>
together to my late venerable mistress&rsquo;s, from whence I had, but the<br>
day before, made my elopement, and with whom he was determined to<br>
settle accounts, in a manner that should cut off all after reckonings<br>
from that quarter.<br>
<br>
Accordingly they went; but by the way, the Templar, his friend, on<br>
thinking over Charles&rsquo;s information, saw reason to give their visit<br>
another turn, and, instead of offering satisfaction, to demand it.<br>
<br>
On being let in, the girls of the house flocked round Charles, whom<br>
they knew, and from the earlyness of my escape, and their perfect<br>
ignorance of his ever having so much as seen me, not having the least<br>
suspicion of his being accessory to my flight, they were, in their way,<br>
making up to him; and as to his companion, they took him probably for a<br>
fresh cully. But the Templar soon checked their forwardness, by<br>
enquiring for the old lady, with whom he said, with a grave-like<br>
countenance, that he had some business to settle.<br>
<br>
Madam was immediately sent for down, and the ladies being desired to<br>
clear the room, the lawyer asked her, severely, if she did know, or had<br>
not decoyed, under pretence of hiring as a servant, a young girl, just<br>
come out of the country, called Frances or Fanny Hill, describing me<br>
withal as particularly as he could from Charlie&rsquo;s description.<br>
<br>
It is peculiar to vice to tremble at the enquiries of justice; and Mrs.<br>
Brown, whose conscience was not entirely clear upon my account, as<br>
knowing as she was of the town as hackneyed as she was in bluffing<br>
through all the dangers of her vocation, could not help being alarmed<br>
at the questions, especially when he went on to talk of a Justice of<br>
peace, Newgate, the Old Bailey, indictments for keeping a disorderly<br>
house, pillory, carting, and the whole process of that nature. She,<br>
who, it is likely, imagined I had lodged an information against her<br>
house, looked extremely blank, and began to make a thousand<br>
protestations and excuses. However, to abridge, they brought away<br>
triumphantly my box of things, which, had she not ben under an awe, she<br>
might have disputed with them; and not only that, but a clearance and<br>
discharge of any demands on the house, at the expense of no more than a<br>
bowl of arrack-punch, the treat of which, together with the choice of<br>
the house conveniences, was offered and not accepted. Charles all the<br>
time acted the chance companion of the lawyer, who had brought him<br>
there, as he knew the house, and appeared in no wise interested in the<br>
issue; but he had the collateral pleasure of hearing all that I told<br>
him verified, as far as the bawd&rsquo;s fears would give her leave to enter<br>
into my history, which, if one may guess by the composition she so<br>
readily came into, were not small.<br>
<br>
Ph&oelig;be, my kind tutoress Ph&oelig;be, was at the time gone out, perhaps in<br>
search of me, or their cooked-up story had not, it is probable, passed<br>
smoothly.<br>
<br>
This negociation had, however, taken up some time, which would have<br>
appeared much longer to me, left as I was, in a strange house, if the<br>
landlady, a motherly sort of a woman, to whom Charles had liberally<br>
recommended me, had not come up and borne me company. We drank tea, and<br>
her chat helped to pass away the time very agreeably, since he was our<br>
theme; but as the evening deepened, and the hour set for his return was<br>
elapsed, I could not dispel the gloom of impatience, and tender fears<br>
which gathered upon me, and which our timid sex are apt to feel in<br>
proportion to their love.<br>
<br>
Long, however, I did not suffer: the sight of him over-paid me; and the<br>
soft reproach I had prepared for him, expired before it reached my<br>
lips.<br>
<br>
I was still a-bed, yet unable to use my legs otherwise than awkwardly,<br>
and Charles flew to me, catches me in his arms, raised and extending<br>
mine to meet his dear embrace, and gives me an account, interrupted by<br>
many a sweet parenthesis of kisses, of the success of his measures.<br>
<br>
I could not help laughing at the fright of the old woman had been put<br>
into, which my ignorance, and indeed my want of innocence, had far from<br>
prepared me from bespeaking. She had, it seems, apprehended that I fled<br>
the shelter to some relation I had recollected in town, on my dislike<br>
of their ways and proceedings towards me, and that this application<br>
came from thence; for, as Charles had rightly judged, not one neighbour<br>
had, at that still hour, seen the circumstance of my escape into the<br>
coach, or, at least, noticed him; neither had any in the house, the<br>
least hint of suspicion of my having spoken to him, much less of my<br>
having clapt up such a sudden bargain with a perfect stranger, thus the<br>
greatest improbability is not always what we should most mistrust.<br>
<br>
We supped with all the gaiety of two young giddy creatures at the top<br>
of their desires; and as I had given up to Charles the whole charge of<br>
my future happiness, I thought of nothing beyond the exquisite pleasure<br>
of possessing him.<br>
<br>
He came to bed in due time; and this second night, the pain being<br>
pretty well over, I tasted, in full draught, all the transports of<br>
perfect enjoyment: I swam, I bathed in bliss, till both fell asleep,<br>
through the natural consequences of satisfied desires, and appeased<br>
flames; nor did we wake but to renewed raptures.<br>
<br>
Thus, making the most of love, and life did we stay in this lodging in<br>
Chelsea about ten days; in which time Charles took care to give his<br>
excursions from home a favourable gloss, and to keep his footing with<br>
his fond indulgent grand-mother, from whom he drew constant and<br>
sufficient supplies for the charge I was to him, and which was very<br>
trifling, in comparison with his former less regular course of<br>
pleasure.<br>
<br>
Charles removed me then to a private ready furnished lodging in D....<br>
street, St. James&rsquo;s, where he paid half a guinea a week for two rooms<br>
and a closet on the second floor, which he had been some time looking<br>
out for, and was more convenient for the frequency of his visits, than<br>
where he had at first placed me, in a house, which I cannot say but I<br>
left with regret, as it was infinitely endeared to me by the first<br>
possession of my Charles, and the circumstance of losing, there, that<br>
jewel, which can never be twice lost. The landlord, however, had no<br>
reason to complain of any thing, but of a procedure in Charles too<br>
liberal not to make him regret the loss of us.<br>
<br>
Arrived at our new lodging, I remember I thought them extremely fine,<br>
though ordinary enough, even at that price; but, had it been a dungeon<br>
that Charles had brought me to, his presence would have made a little<br>
Versailles.<br>
<br>
The landlady, Mrs. Jones, waited on us to our apartment, and with great<br>
volubility of tongue, explained to us all its conveniences: &ldquo;that her<br>
own maid should wait on us... that the best of quality had lodged at<br>
her house... that her first floor was let to a foreign secretary of an<br>
embassy, and his lady... that I looked like a very good natured<br>
lady...&rdquo; At the word lady, I blushed out of flattered vanity: this was<br>
strong for a girl of my condition; for though Charles had the<br>
precaution of dressing me in a less tawdry flaunting style than were<br>
the clothes I escaped to him in, and of passing me for his wife, that<br>
she had secretly married, and kept private (the old story) on account<br>
of his friends, I dare swear this appeared extremely apocryphal to a<br>
woman who knew the town so well as she did; but that was the least of<br>
her concern: it was impossible to be less scruple-ridden than she was;<br>
and the advantage of letting her rooms being her sole object, the truth<br>
itself would have far from scandalized her, or broke her bargain.<br>
<br>
A sketch of her picture, and personal history, will dispose you to<br>
account for the part she is to act in my concern.<br>
<br>
She was about forty six years old, tall, meagre, red-haired, with one<br>
of those trivial ordinary faces you meet with every where, and go about<br>
unheeded and un-mentioned. In her youth she had been kept by a<br>
gentleman, who, dying, left her forty pounds a year during her life, in<br>
consideration of a daughter he had by her: which daughter, at the age<br>
of seventeen, she sold, for not a very considerable sum neither, to a<br>
gentleman who was going on envoy abroad, and took his purchase with<br>
him, where he used her with the utmost tenderness, and it is thought,<br>
was secretly married to her: but had constantly made a point of her not<br>
keeping up the least correspondence with a mother base enough to make a<br>
market of her own flesh and blood. However, as she had not nature, nor,<br>
indeed, any passion but that of money, this gave her no further<br>
uneasiness, then, as she thereby lost a handle of squeezing presents,<br>
or other after advantages, out of the bargain. Indifferent then, by<br>
nature of constitution, to every other pleasure but that of increasing<br>
the lump, by any means whatever, she commenced a kind of private<br>
procuress, for which she was not amiss fitted, by her grave decent<br>
appearance, and sometimes did a job in the match-making way; in short,<br>
there was, nothing that appeared to her under the shape of gain, that<br>
she would not have undertaken. She knew most of the ways of the town,<br>
having not only herself been upon, but kept up constant intelligences<br>
in promoting a harmony between the two sexes, in private pawn-broking,<br>
and other profitable secrets. She rented the house she lived in, and<br>
made the most of it, by letting it out in lodgings; though she was<br>
worth, at least, near three or four thousand pounds, she would not<br>
allow herself even the necessaries, of life, and pinned her subsistence<br>
entirely on what she could squeeze out of her lodgers.<br>
<br>
When she saw such a young pair come under her roof, her immediate<br>
notions, doubtless, were how she should make the most money of us, by<br>
every means that money might be made, and which, she rightly judged,<br>
our situations and inexperience would soon beget her occasions of.<br>
<br>
In this hopeful sanctuary, and under the clutches of this harpy, did we<br>
pitch our residence. It will not be might material to you, or very<br>
pleasant to me, to enter into a detail of all the petty cut-throat ways<br>
and means with which she used to fleece us; all which Charles<br>
indolently chose to bear with, rather than take the trouble of<br>
removing, the difference of expense being scarce attended to by a young<br>
gentleman who had no ideas of stint, or even economy, and a raw country<br>
girl who knew nothing of the matter.<br>
<br>
Here, however, under the wings of my sovereignly beloved, did the most<br>
delicious hours of my life flow on; my Charles I had, and, in him,<br>
every thing my fond heart could wish or desire. He carried me to plays,<br>
operas, masquerades, and every diversion of the town; all which pleased<br>
me, indeed, but pleased me infinitely the more for his being with me,<br>
and explaining every thing to me, and enjoying perhaps, the natural<br>
impressions of surprise and admiration, which such sights, at the<br>
first, never fail to excite in a country girl, new to the delights of<br>
them; but to me, they sensibly proved the power and dominion of the<br>
sole passion of my heart over me, a passion in which soul and body were<br>
concentered, and left me no room for any other relish of life but love.<br>
<br>
As to the men I saw at those places, or at any other, they suffered so<br>
much in the comparison my eyes made of them with my all-perfect Adonis,<br>
that I had not the infidelity even of one wandering thought to reproach<br>
myself with upon his account. He was the universe to me, and all that<br>
was not him, was nothing to me.<br>
<br>
My love, in fine, was so excessive, that is arrived at annihilating<br>
every suggestion or kindling spark of jealousy; for, one idea only,<br>
tending that way, gave me such exquisite torment, that my self-love,<br>
and dread of worse than death, made me for ever renounce and defy it:<br>
nor had I, indeed, occasion; for, were I to enter here on the recital<br>
of several instances wherein Charles sacrificed to me women of much<br>
greater importance than I dare hint (which, considering his form, was<br>
no such wonder), I might, indeed, give you full proof of his unshaken<br>
constancy to me; but would not you accuse me of warming up against a<br>
feast, which my vanity ought long ago to have been satisfied with?<br>
<br>
In our cessations from active pleasure, Charles framed himself one, in<br>
instructing me, as far as his own lights reached, in a great many<br>
points of life, that I was, in consequence of my no-education,<br>
perfectly ignorant of: nor did I suffer one word to fall in vain from<br>
the mouth of my lovely teacher: I hung on every syllable he uttered,<br>
and received, as oracles, all he said; whilst kisses were all the<br>
interruption I could not refuse myself the pleasure of admitting, from<br>
lips that breathed more than Arabian sweetness, I was in a little time<br>
enabled, by the progress I had made, to prove the deep regard I had<br>
paid to all that he had said to me: repeating it to him almost word for<br>
word; and to shew that I was not entirely the parrot, but that I<br>
reflected upon, that I entered into it, I joined my own comments, and<br>
asked him questions of explanation.<br>
<br>
My country accent, and the rusticity of my gait, manners, and<br>
deportment, began now sensibly to wear off: so quick was my<br>
observation, and so efficacious my desire of growing every day worthier<br>
of his heart.<br>
<br>
As to money, though, he brought me constantly all he received, it was<br>
with difficulty he even got me to give it room in my bureau; and what<br>
clothes I had, he could prevail on me to accept of on no other foot,<br>
than that of pleasing him by the greater neatness in my dress, beyond<br>
which I had no ambition. I could have made a pleasure of the greatest<br>
toil, and worked my fingers to the bone, with joy, to have supported<br>
him: guess, then, if I could harbour any idea of being burthensome to<br>
him, and this disinterested turn in me was so unaffected, so much the<br>
dictate of my heart, that Charles could not but feel it: and if he did<br>
not love me as much as I did him (which was the constant and only<br>
matter of sweet contention between us), he managed so, at least, as to<br>
give me the satisfaction of believing it impossible for man to be more<br>
tender, more true, more faithful than he was.<br>
<br>
Our landlady, Mrs. Jones, came frequently up to my apartment, from<br>
whence I never stirred on any pretext without Charles; nor was it long<br>
before she wormed out, without much art, the secret of our having<br>
cheated the church of a ceremony, and, in course, of the terms we lived<br>
together upon; a circumstance which far from displeased her,<br>
considering the designs she had upon me, and which, alas! she will have<br>
too soon, room to carry into execution. But in the meantime, her own<br>
experience of life let her see, that any attempt, however indirect or<br>
disguised, to divert or break, at least presently, so strong a cement<br>
of hearts as ours was, could only end in losing two lodgers, of whom<br>
she had made very competent advantages, if either of us came to smoke<br>
her commission, for a commission she had from one of her customers,<br>
either to debauch, or get me away from my keeper at any rate.<br>
<br>
But the barbarity of my fate soon saved her the task of disuniting us.<br>
I had now been eleven months with this life of my life, which had<br>
passed in one continued rapid stream of delight: but nothing so violent<br>
was ever made to last. I was about three months gone with a child by<br>
him, a circumstances would have added to his tenderness, had he ever<br>
left me room to believe it could receive an addition, when the mortal,<br>
the unexpected blow of separation fell upon us. I shall gallop<br>
post-over the particulars, which I shudder yet to think of, and cannot;<br>
to this instant, reconcile myself how, or by what means I could<br>
out-live it.<br>
<br>
Two live-long days had I lingered through without hearing from him, I<br>
who breathed, who existed but in him, and had never yet seen<br>
twenty-four hours pass without seeing or hearing from him. The third<br>
day my impatience was so strong, my alarms had been so severe, that I<br>
perfectly sickened with them; and being unable to support the shock<br>
longer, I sunk upon the bed, and ringing for Mrs. Jones, who had far<br>
from comforted me under my anxieties, she came up, and I had scarce<br>
breath and spirit enough to find words to beg of her, if she would save<br>
my life, to fall upon some means of finding out, instantly, what was<br>
become of its only prop and comfort. She pitied me in a way that rather<br>
sharpened my affliction than suspended it, and went out upon this<br>
commission.<br>
<br>
For she had but to go to Charles&rsquo;s house, who lived but an easy<br>
distance, in one of the streets that run into Covent Garden. There she<br>
went into a public house, and from thence sent for a mid servant, whose<br>
name I had given her, as the properest to inform her.<br>
<br>
The maid readily came, and as readily, when Mrs. Jones enquired of her<br>
what had become of Mr. Charles, or whether he was gone out of town,<br>
acquainted her with the disposal of her master&rsquo;s son, which, the very<br>
day after, was no secret to the servants. Such sure measures had he<br>
taken, for the most cruel punishment of his child for having more<br>
interest with his grandmother than he had, though he made use of a<br>
pretence, plausible enough, to get rid of him in this secret abrupt<br>
manner, for fear her fondness should have interposed a bar to his<br>
leaving England, and proceeding on a voyage he had concerted for him;<br>
which pretext was, that it was indispensably necessary to secure a<br>
considerable inheritance that devolved to him by the death of a rich<br>
merchant (his own brother) at one of the factories in the South Seas,<br>
of which he had lately received advice, together with a copy of the<br>
will.<br>
<br>
In consequence of which resolution, to send away his son, he had,<br>
unknown to him, made the necessary preparations for fitting him out,<br>
struck a bargain with the captain of a ship, whose punctual execution<br>
of his orders he had secured, by his interest with his principal owners<br>
and patron; and, in short, concerted his measures so secretly, and<br>
effectually, that whilst the son thought he was going down to the<br>
river, that would take him a few hours, he was stopt on board of a<br>
ship, debarred from writing, and more strictly watched than a State<br>
criminal.<br>
<br>
Thus was the idol of my soul torn from me, and forced on a long voyage,<br>
without taking leave of one friend, or receiving one line of comfort,<br>
except a dry explanation and instructions, from his father, how to<br>
proceed when he should arrive at his destined port, enclosing, withal,<br>
some letters of recommendation to a factor there: all these particulars<br>
I did not learn minutely till some time after.<br>
<br>
The maid, at the same time, added, that she was sure this usage of her<br>
sweet young master would be the death of his grand-mamma, as indeed it<br>
proved true; for the old lady, on hearing it, did not survive the news<br>
a whole month, and as her fortune consisted in an annuity, out of which<br>
she had laid up no reserves, she left nothing worth mentioning to her<br>
so fatally envied darling, but absolutely refused to see his father<br>
before she died.<br>
<br>
When Mrs. Jones returned, and I observed her looks, they seemed so<br>
unconcerned, and even nearest to pleased, that I half flattered myself<br>
she was going to set my tortured heart at ease, by bringing me good<br>
news; but this, indeed, was a cruel delusion of hope: the barbarian,<br>
with all the coolness imaginable, stabs me to the heart, in telling me,<br>
succinctly, that he was sent away, at least, on a four years&rsquo; voyage<br>
(here she stretched maliciously), and that I could not expect, in<br>
reason, ever to see him again: and all this with such pregnant<br>
circumstances, that I could not escape giving them credit, as they<br>
were, indeed, too true!<br>
<br>
She had hardly finished her report before I fainted away, and after<br>
several successive fits, all the while wild and senseless, I miscarried<br>
of the dear pledge of my Charles&rsquo;s love; but the wretched never die<br>
when it is fittest they should die, and women are hard-lived! to a<br>
proverb.<br>
<br>
The cruel and interested care taken to recover me, saved an odious<br>
life: which, instead of the happiness and joys it had overflower in,<br>
all of a sudden presented no view before me of any thing but the depth<br>
of misery, horror, and the sharpest affliction.<br>
<br>
Thus I lay six weeks, in the struggles of youth and constitution,<br>
against the friendly efforts of death, which I constantly invoked to my<br>
relief and deliverance, but which proved too weak for my wish. I<br>
recovered at length, but into a state of stupefaction and despair, that<br>
threatened me with the loss of my senses, and a mad house.<br>
<br>
Time, however, that great comforter in ordinary, began to assuage the<br>
violence of my suffering, and to numb my feeling of them. My health<br>
returned to me, though I still retained an air of grief, dejection, and<br>
languor, which taking off from the ruddiness of my country complexion,<br>
rendered it rather more delicate and affecting.<br>
<br>
The landlady had all this while officiously provided, and seen that I<br>
wanted for nothing: and as soon as she saw me retrieved into a<br>
condition of answering her purpose, one day, after we had dined<br>
together, she congratulated me on my recovery, the merit of which she<br>
took entirely to herself, and all this by way of introduction to a most<br>
terrible, and scurvy epilogue: &ldquo;You are now,&rdquo; says she, &ldquo;Miss Fanny,<br>
tolerably well, and you are very welcome to stay in these lodgings as;<br>
long as you please! you see I have asked you for nothing this long<br>
time, but truly I have a call to make up a sum of money, which must be<br>
answered.&rdquo; And, with that, presents me with a bill of arrears for rent,<br>
diet, apothecaries&rsquo; charges, nurse, etc., sum total twenty-three<br>
pounds, seventeen and six-pence: towards discharging of which I had not<br>
in the world (which she well knew) more than seven guineas, left by<br>
chance, of my dear Charles&rsquo;s common stock, with me. At the same time,<br>
she desired me to tell her what course I would take for payment. I<br>
burst out into a flood of tears, and told her my condition: that I<br>
would sell what few clothes I had, and that, for the rest, would pay<br>
her as soon as possible. But my distress, being favourable to her view,<br>
only stiffened her the more.<br>
<br>
She told me, very cooly, that &ldquo;she was indeed sorry for my misfortunes,<br>
but that she must do herself justice, though it would go to the very<br>
heart of her to send such a tender young creature to prison....&rdquo; At the<br>
word &ldquo;prison!&rdquo; every drop of my blood chilled, and my fright acted so<br>
strongly upon me, that, turning as pale and faint as a criminal at the<br>
first sight of his place of execution, I was on the point of swooning.<br>
My landlady, who wanted only to terrify me to a certain point, and not<br>
to throw me into a state of body inconsistent with her designs upon it,<br>
began to sooth me again, and told me, in a tone composed to more pity<br>
and gentleness, that &ldquo;it would be my own fault, if she was forced to<br>
proceed to such extremities; but she believed there was a friend to be<br>
found in the world, who would make up matters to both our<br>
satisfactions, and that she would bring him to drink tea with us that<br>
very afternoon, when she hoped we would come to a right understanding<br>
in our affairs.&rdquo; To all this, not a word of answer; I sat mute,<br>
confounded, terrified.<br>
<br>
Mrs. Jones, however, judging rightly that it was time to strike while<br>
the impressions were so strong upon me, left me to myself and to all<br>
the terrors of an imagination, wounded to death by the idea of going to<br>
prison, and, from a principle of self-preservation, snatching at every<br>
glimpse of redemption from it.<br>
<br>
In this situation I sat near half an hour, swallowed up in grief and<br>
despair, when my landlady came in, and observing a death-like dejection<br>
in my countenance, still in pursuance of her plan, put on a false pity,<br>
and bidding me be of good heart: &ldquo;Things,&rdquo; she said, &ldquo;would be but my<br>
own friend&rdquo;; and closed with telling me &ldquo;she had brought a very<br>
honourable gentleman to drink tea with me, who would give me the best<br>
advice how to get rid of all my troubles.&rdquo; Upon which, without waiting<br>
for a reply, she goes out, and returns with this very honourable<br>
gentleman, whose very honourable procuress she had been, on this, as<br>
well as other occasions.<br>
<br>
The gentleman, on his entering the room, made me a very civil bow,<br>
which I had scarce strength, or presence of mind enough to return a<br>
curtsey to; when the landlady, taking upon her to do all the honours of<br>
the first interview (for I had never, that I remember, seen the<br>
gentleman before), sets a chair for him, another for herself. All this<br>
while not a word on either side; a stupid stare was all the face I<br>
could put on this strange visit.<br>
<br>
The tea was made, and the landlady, unwilling, I suppose, to lose any<br>
time, observing my silence and shyness before this entire stranger:<br>
&ldquo;Come, Miss Fanny,&rdquo; says she, in a coarse familiar style, and tone of<br>
authority, &ldquo;hold up your head, child, and do not let sorrow spoil that<br>
pretty face of yours. What! sorrows are only for a time; come, be free,<br>
here is a worthy gentleman who has heard of your misfortunes, and is<br>
willing to serve you; you must be better acquainted with him, do not<br>
you now stand upon your punctilios, and this and that, but make your<br>
market while you may.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
At this so delicate, and eloquent harangue, the gentleman, who saw I<br>
looked frighted and amazed, and, indeed, incapable of answering, took<br>
her up for breaking things in so abrupt a manner, as rather to shock<br>
than incline me to an acceptance of the good he intended me then,<br>
addressing himself to me, told me &ldquo;he was perfectly acquainted with my<br>
whole story, and every circumstance of my distress which he owned was a<br>
cruel plunge for one of my youth and beauty to fall into.... that he<br>
had long taken a liking to my person, for which he appealed to Mrs.<br>
Jones, there present; but finding me so deeply engaged to another, he<br>
had lost all hopes of succeeding, till he had heard the sudden reverse<br>
of fortune that had happened to me, on which he had given particular<br>
orders to my landlady to see that I should want for nothing; and that,<br>
had he not been forced abroad to the Hague, on affairs he could not<br>
refuse himself to, he would himself have attended me during my<br>
sickness;... that on his return, which was the day before, he had, on<br>
learning my recovery, desired my landlady&rsquo;s good offices to introduce<br>
him to me, and was as angry, at least, as I was shocked, at the manner<br>
in which she had conducted herself towards obtaining him that<br>
happiness; but, that to show me how much he disdained her procedure,<br>
and how far he was from taking any ungenerous advantage of my<br>
situation, and from exacting any security for my gratitude, he would<br>
before my face, that instant, discharge my debt entirely to my<br>
landlady, and give me her receipt in full; after which I should be at<br>
liberty either to reject or grant his suit, as he was much above<br>
putting any force upon my inclinations.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
Whilst he was exposing his sentiments to me, I ventured just to look up<br>
to him, and observed his figure, which was that of a very well-looking<br>
gentleman, well made, of about forty, dressed in a suit of plain<br>
clothes, with a large diamond ring on one of his fingers, the lustre of<br>
which played in my eyes as he waved his hand in talking, and raised my<br>
notions of his importance. In short, he might pass for what is commonly<br>
called a comely black man, with an air of distinction natural to his<br>
birth and condition.<br>
<br>
To all his speeches, however, I answered only in tears that flower<br>
plentifully to my relief, and choking up my voice, excused me from<br>
speaking, very luckily, for I should not have known what to say.<br>
<br>
The sight, however, moved him, as he afterwards told me, irresistibly,<br>
and by way of giving me some reason to be less powerfully afflicted, he<br>
drew out his purse, and calling for pen and ink, which the landlady was<br>
prepared for, paid her every farthing of her demand, independent of a<br>
liberal gratification which was to follow unknown to me, and taking a<br>
receipt in full, very tenderly forced me to secure it, by guiding my<br>
hand, which he had thrust it into, so as to make me passively put it<br>
into my pocket.<br>
<br>
Still I continued in a state of stupidity, or melancholic despair, as<br>
my spirits could not yet recover from the violent shocks that they had<br>
received; and the accommodating landlady had actually left the room,<br>
and me alone with this strange gentleman, before I had observed it, and<br>
then I observed it without alarm, for I was now lifeless, and<br>
indifferent to every thing.<br>
<br>
The gentleman, however, no novice in affairs of this sort, drew near<br>
me; and, under the pretence of comforting me, first with his<br>
handkerchief dried my tears as they ran down my cheeks: presently he<br>
ventured to kiss me on my part, neither resistance nor compliance. I<br>
sat stock still; and now looking on myself as bought by the payment<br>
that had been transacted before me.<br>
<br>
I did not care what became of my wretched body: and wanting life,<br>
spirits, or courage to oppose the least struggle, even that of the<br>
modesty of my sex, I suffered, tamely, whatever the gentleman pleased;<br>
who proceeding insensibly from freedom to freedom, insinuating his hand<br>
between my handkerchief and bosom, which he handled at discretion:<br>
finding thus no repulse, and that every thing favoured, beyond<br>
expectation, the completion of his desires, he took me in his arms, and<br>
bore me, without life or motion, to the bed, on which laying me gently<br>
downed, and having me at what advantage he pleased, I did not so much<br>
as know what he was about, till recovering from a trance of lifeless<br>
insensibility, I found him buried in me, whilst I lay passive and<br>
innocent of the least sensations of pleasure: a death-cold corpse could<br>
scarce have less life or sense in it. As soon as he had thus pacified a<br>
passion which had too little respected the condition I was in, he got<br>
off, and after recomposing the disorder of my clothes, employed himself<br>
with the utmost tenderness to calm the transports of remorse and<br>
madness at myself, with which I was seized, too late, I confess, for<br>
having suffered on that bed, the embraces of an utter stranger I tore<br>
my hair, wrung my hands, and beat my breast like a mad woman. But when<br>
my new master, for in that light I then viewed him, applied himself to<br>
appease me, as my whole rage was levelled at myself, no part of which I<br>
thought myself permitted to aim at him, I begged of him with more<br>
submission than anger, to leave me alone, that I might, at least, enjoy<br>
my affliction in quiet. This he positively refused, for fear, as he<br>
pretended, I should do myself a mischief. Violent passions seldom last<br>
long, and those of women least of any. A dead still calm succeeded this<br>
storm, which ended in a profuse shower of tears.<br>
<br>
Had any one, but a few instants before, told me that I should have ever<br>
known any man but Charles, I would have spit in his face or had I been<br>
offered infinitely a greater sum of money than that I saw paid for me,<br>
I had spurned the proposal in cold blood. But our virtues and our vices<br>
depend too much on our circumstances; unexpectedly beset as I was,<br>
betrayed by a mind weakened by a long severe affliction, and stunned<br>
with the terrors of a goal, my defeat will appear the more excusable,<br>
since I certainly was not present at, or a party in any sense to it.<br>
However, as the first enjoyment is decisive, and he was now over the<br>
