bar, I thought I had no longer a right to refuse the caresses of one<br>
that had got that advantage over me, no matter how obtained; conforming<br>
myself then to this maxim, I considered myself as so much in his power,<br>
that I endured his kisses and embraces without affecting struggles or<br>
anger; not that he, as yet, gave me any pleasure, or prevailed over the<br>
aversion of my soul, to give myself up to any sensation of that sort;<br>
what I suffered, I suffered out of a kind of gratitude, and as a matter<br>
of course what had passed.<br>
<br>
He was, however, so regardful as not to attempt the renewal of those<br>
extremities which had thrown me, just before, into such violent<br>
agitations; but, now secure of possession, contented himself with<br>
bringing me to temper by degrees, and waiting at the hand of time for<br>
those fruits of generosity and courtship, which he since often<br>
reproached himself with having gathered much too green, when, yielding<br>
to the inability to resist him, and overborne by desires, he had<br>
wreaked his passion on a mere lifeless, spiritless body, dead to all<br>
purpose of joy, since taking none, it ought to be supposed incapable of<br>
giving any. This is, however, certain; my heart never thoroughly<br>
forgave him the manner in which I had fallen to him, although, in point<br>
of interest, I had fallen to him, I had reason to be pleased that he<br>
found, in my person, wherewithal to keep him from leaving me as easily<br>
as he had had me.<br>
<br>
The evening was, in the mean time, so far advanced, that the maid came<br>
in to lay the cloth for supper, when I understood, with joy, that my<br>
landlady, whose sight was present poison to me, was not to be with us.<br>
<br>
Presently a neat and elegant supper was introduced, and a bottle of<br>
Burgundy, with the other necessaries, were set on a dumb-waiter.<br>
<br>
The maid quitting the room, the gentleman insisted, with a tender<br>
warmth, that I should sit up in the elbow chair by the fire, and see<br>
him eat, if I could not be prevailed on to eat myself. I obeyed with a<br>
heart full or affliction, at the comparison it made between those<br>
delicious _t&ecirc;te-&agrave;-t&ecirc;tes_ with my very dear youth, and this forced<br>
situation, this new awkward scene, imposed and obtruded on me a cruel<br>
necessity.<br>
<br>
At supper, after a great many arguments used to comfort and reconcile<br>
me to my fate, he told me that his name was H..., brother to the Earl<br>
of L.... and that having, by the suggestions of my landlady, been led<br>
to see me, he had found me perfectly to his taste, and given her a<br>
commission to procure me at any rate, and that at length he had<br>
succeeded, as much to his satisfaction as he passionately wished it<br>
might be to mine adding, withal, some flattering assurances, that I<br>
should have no cause to repent my knowledge of him.<br>
<br>
I had now got down at least half a partridge, and three or four glasses<br>
of wine, which he compelled me to drink by way of restoring nature, but<br>
whether there was any thing extraordinary put into the wine, or whether<br>
there wanted no more to revive the natural warmth of my constitution,<br>
and give fire to the old train, I began no longer to look with that<br>
constraint, not to say disguise, on Mr. H...., which I had hitherto<br>
done but, withal, there was not the least grain of love mixed with this<br>
softening of my sentiments: any other man would have been just the same<br>
to me as Mr. H..., that stood in the same circumstances, and had done<br>
for me, and with me, what he had done.<br>
<br>
There are not, on earth at least, eternal griefs; mine were, if not at<br>
an end, at least suspended: my heart, which had been so long overloaded<br>
with anguish and vexation, began to dilate and open to the last gleam<br>
of diversion or amusement. I wept a little, and my tears relieved me; I<br>
sighed, and my sighs seemed to lighten me of a load that oppressed me;<br>
my countenance grew, if not cheerful, at least more composed and free.<br>
<br>
Mr. H..., who had watched, perhaps brought on this change, knew too<br>
well not to seize it: he thrust the table imperceptibly from between<br>
us, and bringing his chair to face me, he soon began, after preparing<br>
me by all the endearments of assurance and protestations, to lay hold<br>
of my hands, to kiss me, and once more to make free with my bosom,<br>
which, being at full liberty from the disorder of a loose dishabile,<br>
now panted and throbbed, less with indignation than with fear and<br>
bashfulness, at being used so familiarly by still a stranger. But he<br>
soon gave me greater occasion to exclaim, by stooping down and slipping<br>
his hands above my garters; thence he strove to regain the pass, which<br>
he had before found so open, and unguarded; but now he could not unlock<br>
the twist of my thighs; I gently complained, and begged him to let me<br>
alone; told him I was not well. However, he saw there was more form and<br>
ceremony in my resistance, than good earnest; he made his conditions<br>
for desisting from pursuing his point, that I should be put instantly<br>
to bed, whilst he gave certain orders to the landlady, and that he<br>
would return in an hour, when he hoped to find me more reconciled to<br>
his passion for me, than I seemed at present. I neither assented nor<br>
denied, but my air and manner of receiving his proposal, gave him to<br>
see that I did not think myself enough my own mistress to refuse it.<br>
<br>
Accordingly he went out and left me, when a minute or two after, before<br>
I could recover myself into any composure for thinking, the maid came<br>
in with her mistress&rsquo;s service, and a small silver orringer of what she<br>
called a bridal posset, and desired me to eat it as I went to bed,<br>
which consequently I did, and felt immediately a heat, a fire run like<br>
a hue-and-cry through every part of my body; I burnt, I glowed, and<br>
wanted even little of wishing for any man.<br>
<br>
The maid, as soon as I was lain down, took the candle away, and wishing<br>
me a good night, went out of the room, and shut the door after her.<br>
<br>
She had hardly time to get down stairs, before Mr. H.... opened my room<br>
door softly, and came in, now undressed, in his night-gown and cap,<br>
with two lighted wax candles, and bolting the door, gave me, though I<br>
expected him, some sort of alarm. He came a tip-toe to the bed side,<br>
and saying with a gentle whisper: &ldquo;Pray, my dear, do not be startled...<br>
I will be very tender and kind to you.&rdquo; He then hurried off his<br>
clothes, and leaped into bed, having given me openings enough, whilst<br>
he was stripping, to observe his brawny structure, strong made limbs,<br>
and rough shaggy breast.<br>
<br>
The bed shook again when it received this new load. He lay on the<br>
outside, where he kept the candles burning, no doubt for the<br>
satisfaction of every sense, for as soon as he had kissed me, he rolled<br>
down the bed clothes, and seemed transported with the view of all my<br>
person at full length, which he covered with a profusion of kisses,<br>
sparing no part of me. Then, being on his knees between my thighs, he<br>
drew up his shirt, and bared all his hairy thighs, and stiff staring<br>
truncheon, red top, and rooted into a thicket of curls, which covered<br>
his belly to the novel, and gave it the air of a flesh brush; and soon<br>
I feel it joining close to mine, when he had drove the nail up to the<br>
head, and left no partition but the intermediate hair on both sides.<br>
<br>
I had it now, I felt it now, and, beginning to drive, he soon gave<br>
nature such a powerful summons down to her favourite quarters, that she<br>
could no longer refuse repairing thither; all my animals spirits then<br>
rushed mechanically to that center of attraction, and presently, inly<br>
warmed, and stirred as I was beyond bearing, I lost all restraint, and<br>
yielding to the force of the emotion, gave down, as mere woman, those<br>
effusions of pleasure, which, in the strictness of still faithful love,<br>
I could have wished to have kept in.<br>
<br>
Yet oh! what an immense difference did I feel between this impression<br>
of a pleasure merely animal, and struck out of the collision of the<br>
sexes, by a passive bodily effect, from that sweet fury, that rage of<br>
active delight which crowns the enjoyments of a mutual love passion,<br>
where two hearts, tenderly and truly united, club to exalt the joy, and<br>
give it a spirit and soul that bids defiance to that end which mere<br>
momentary desires generally terminate in, when they die of a surfeit of<br>
satisfaction!<br>
<br>
Mr. H..., whom no distinctions of that sort seemed to distract, scarce<br>
gave himself or me breathing time from the last encounter, but, as if<br>
he had tasked himself to prove that the appearances of his vigour were<br>
no signs hung out in vain, in a few minutes he was in a condition for<br>
renewing the onset; to which, preluding with a storm of kisses, he<br>
drove the same course as before, with unbated fervour; and thus, in<br>
repeated engagements, kept me constantly in exercise, till dawn of<br>
morning, in all which time he made me fully sensible of the virtues of<br>
his firm texture of limbs, his square shoulders, broad chest, compact<br>
hard muscles, in short a system of manliness, that might pass for no<br>
bad image of our ancient sturdy barons, whose race is now so thoroughly<br>
refined and frittered away into the more delicate and modern built<br>
frame of our pap-nerved softlings, who are as pale, as pretty, and<br>
almost as masculine as their sisters.<br>
<br>
Mr. H..., content, however, with having the day break upon his triumph,<br>
resigned me up to the refreshment of a rest we both wanted, and we soon<br>
dropped into a profound sleep.<br>
<br>
Though he was some time awake before me, yet he did not offer to<br>
disturb a repose he had given me so much occasion for; but on my first<br>
stirring, which was not till past ten o&rsquo;clock, I was obliged to endure<br>
one more trial of his manhood.<br>
<br>
About eleven, in came Mrs. Jones, with two basins of the richest soup,<br>
which her experience in these matters had moved her to prepare. I pass<br>
over the fulsome compliments, the cant of the decent procuress, with<br>
which she saluted us both; but though my blood rose at the sight of<br>
her, I supprest my emotions, and gave all my concerne to reflections on<br>
what would be the consequence of this new engagement.<br>
<br>
But Mr. H..., who penetrated my uneasiness, did not suffer me to<br>
languish under it, and acquainted me, that having taken a solid sincere<br>
affection to me, he would begin by giving me one leading mark of it, in<br>
removing me out of a house which must, for many reasons, be irksome and<br>
disagreeable to me, into convenient lodgings, where he would take all<br>
imaginable care of me; and desiring not to have any explanations with<br>
my landlady, or be impatient till he returned, he dressed and went out,<br>
having left me a purse with two and twenty guineas in it, being all he<br>
had about him, as he express it, to keep my pocket still further<br>
supplied.<br>
<br>
As soon as he was gone, I felt the usual consequence of the first<br>
launch into vice (for my love attachment to Charles never appeared to<br>
me in that light). I was instantly borne away down the stream without<br>
making back to the shore. My dreadful necessities, my gratitude, and<br>
above all, to say the plain truth, the dissipation and diversion I<br>
began to find in this new acquaintance, from the black corroding<br>
thoughts my heart had been a prey to, ever since the absence of my dear<br>
Charles, concurred to stun all my contrary reflections. If I now<br>
thought of my first, my only charmer, it was still with the tenderness<br>
and regret of the fondest love, embittered with the consciousness that<br>
I was no longer worthy of him. I could have begged my bread with him<br>
all over the world, but wretch that I was! I had neither the virtue or<br>
courage requisite not to outlive my separation from him.<br>
<br>
Yet, had not my heart been thus preengaged, Mr. H... might probably<br>
have been the sole master of it; but the place was full, and the force<br>
of conjectures alone had made him the possessor of my person; the<br>
charms of which had, by the bye, been his sole object and passion, and<br>
were, of course, no foundation for a love either very delicate or very<br>
durable.<br>
<br>
He did not return till six in the evening, to take me away to my new<br>
lodgings; and my moveables being soon packed, and conveyed into a<br>
hackney coach, it cost me but little regret to take my leave of a<br>
landlady whom I thought I had so much reason not to be over pleased<br>
with; and as for her part, she made no other difference to my staying<br>
or going, but what that of the profit created.<br>
<br>
We soon got to the house appointed for me, which was that of a plain<br>
tradesman, who, on the score of interest, was entirely at Mr. H...&rsquo;s<br>
devotion, and who let him the first floor, very genteelly furnished,<br>
for two guineas a week, of which I was instated mistress, with a maid<br>
to attend me.<br>
<br>
He stayed with me that evening, and we had a supper from a neighbouring<br>
tavern, after which, and a gay glass or two, the maid put me to bed.<br>
Mr. H.... soon followed, and notwithstanding the fatigues of the<br>
preceding night, I found no quarter nor remission from him: he piquet<br>
himself, as he told me, on doing the honours of my new apartment.<br>
<br>
The morning being pretty well advanced, we got to breakfast; and the<br>
ice now broke, my heart, no longer engrossed by love, began to take<br>
ease, and to please itself with such trifles Mr. H....&rsquo;s liberal liking<br>
led him to make his court to the usual vanity of our sex. Silks, laces:<br>
ear rings, pearl necklace, gold watch, in sort, all the trinkets and<br>
articles of dress were lavishly heaped upon me; the sence of which, if<br>
it did not create returns of love, forced a kind of grateful fondness,<br>
something like love: a distinction which it would be spoiling the<br>
pleasure of nine tenths of the keepers in the town to make, and is, I<br>
suppose, the very good reason why so few of them ever do make it.<br>
<br>
I was now established the kept mistress in form, well lodged, with a<br>
very sufficient allowance, and lighted up with all the lustre of dress.<br>
<br>
Mr. H.... continued kind and tender to me; yet, with all this, I was<br>
far from happy: for, besides my regrets for my dear youth, which,<br>
though often suspended or diverted, still returned upon me in certain<br>
melancholic moments with redoubled violence, I wanted more society,<br>
more dissipation.<br>
<br>
As to Mr. H.... he was so much my superior in every sense, that I felt<br>
it too much to the disadvantage of the gratitude I owed him. Thus he<br>
gained my esteem, though he could not raise my taste; I was qualified<br>
for no sort of conversation with him, except one sort, and that is a<br>
satisfaction which leaves tiresome intervals, if not filled up by love,<br>
or other amusements.<br>
<br>
Mr. H...., so experienced, so learned in the ways of women, numbers of<br>
whom had passed through his hands, doubtless, soon perceived this<br>
uneasiness, and, without approving, or liking me the better for it, had<br>
the complaisance to indulge me.<br>
<br>
He made suppers at my lodging, where he brought several companions of<br>
his pleasures, with their mistresses; and by this means I got into a<br>
circle of acquaintance, that soon stripped me of all the remains of<br>
bashfulness and modesty which might be yet left of my country<br>
education, and were, to a just taste, perhaps, the greatest of my<br>
charms.<br>
<br>
We visited one another in form, and mimicked, as near as we could, all<br>
the miseries, the follies, and impertinencies of the women in quality,<br>
in the round of which they trifle away their time, without it ever<br>
entering their little heads, that on earth there cannot subsist any<br>
thing more silly, more flat, more insipid and worthless, than,<br>
generally considered, their system of life is: they ought to treat the<br>
men as their tyrants, indeed! were they to condemn them to it.<br>
<br>
But though, amongst the kept mistresses (and I was now acquainted with<br>
a good many, besides some useful matrons, who live by their connexions<br>
with them), I hardly knew one that did not perfectly detest their<br>
keepers, and, of course, made little or no scruple of any infidelity<br>
they could safely accomplish, I had still no notion of wronging mine:<br>
for, besides that no mark of jealousy on his side started me the hint,<br>
or gave me the provocation to play him a trick of that sort, and that<br>
his constant generosity, politeness, and tender attention to please me,<br>
forced a regard to him, that, without affecting my heart, insured him<br>
my fidelity, no object had yet presented that could overcome the<br>
habitual liking I had contracted for him and I was on the eve of<br>
obtaining, from the movements of his own voluntary generosity, a modest<br>
provision for life, when an accident happened which broke all the<br>
measures he had resolved upon in my favour.<br>
<br>
I had now lived near seven months with Mr. H.... when one day returning<br>
to my lodgings, from a visit in the neighbourhood, where I used to stay<br>
longer, I found the street door open, and the maid of the house<br>
standing at it, talking with some of her acquaintance, so that I came<br>
in without knocking and, as I passed by, she told me Mr. H.... was<br>
above. I slept up stairs into my own bed-chamber, with no other thought<br>
than of pulling off my hat etc., and then to wait upon him in the<br>
dining room, into which my bed-chamber had a door, as is common enough.<br>
Whilst I was untying my hat strings, I fancied I heard my maid Hannah&rsquo;s<br>
voice and a sort of tustle, which raised my curiosity; I stole softly<br>
to the door, where a knot in the wood had been slipped out, and<br>
afforded a very commanding peep-hole to the scene then in agitation,<br>
the actors of which had been to earnestly employed to hear my opening<br>
my own door, from the landing place of the stairs, into my bedchamber.<br>
<br>
The first sight that struck me was Mr. H.... pulling and hauling this<br>
coarse country strammel towards a couch that stood in a corner of the<br>
dining-room; to which the girl made only a sort of awkward holdening<br>
resistance, crying out so loud, that I, who listened at the door, could<br>
scarce hear her: &ldquo;Pray Sir, don&rsquo;t.., let me alone... I am not for your<br>
turn... You cannot, sure, demean yourself with such a poor body as I...<br>
Lord! Sir, my mistress may come home... I must not indeed... I will cry<br>
out...&rdquo; All of which did not hinder her from insensibly suffering<br>
herself to be brought to the foot of the couch, upon which a push of no<br>
mighty violence served to give her a very easy fall, and my gentleman<br>
having got up his hands to the strong hold of her Virtue, she, no<br>
doubt, thought it was time to give up the argument, and that all<br>
further defense would be vain: and he, throwing her petticoats over her<br>
face, which was now as red as scarlet, discovered a pair of stout,<br>
plump, substantial thighs, and tolerably white; he mounted them round<br>
his haps, and coming out with his drawn weapon, stuck it in the cloven<br>
sport, where he seemed to find a less difficult entrance than perhaps<br>
he had flattered himself with (for, by the way, this blouse had left<br>
her place in the country, for a bastard), and, indeed, all his motions<br>
shewed he was lodged pretty much at large. After he had done, his Deare<br>
gets up, drops her petticoats down, and smooths her apron and<br>
handkerchief. Mr. H.... looked a little silly, and taking out some<br>
money, gave it her, with an air indifferent enough, bidding her be a<br>
good girl, and say nothing.<br>
<br>
Had I loved this man, it was not in nature for me to have had patience<br>
to see the whole scene through: I should have broke in and played the<br>
jealous princess with a vengeance. But that was not the case: my pride<br>
alone was hurt, my heart not, and I could easier win upon myself to see<br>
how far he would go, till I had no uncertainty upon my conscience.<br>
<br>
The least delicate of all affairs of this sort being now over, I<br>
retired softly into my closet, where I began to consider what I should<br>
do. My first scheme naturally, was to rush in and upbraid them; this,<br>
indeed, flattered my present emotions and vexations, as it would have<br>
given immediate vent to them; but, on second thoughts, not being so<br>
clear as to the consequence to be apprehended from such a step, I began<br>
my discovery still a safer season, when dissembly my discovery till a<br>
safer season, when Mr. H.... should have perfected the settlement he<br>
had made overtures to me of, and which I was not to think such a<br>
violent explanation, as I was indeed not equal to the management of,<br>
could possibly forward, and might destroy. On the other hand, the<br>
provocation seemed too gross, too flagrant not to give me some thoughts<br>
of revenge; the very start of which idea restored me to perfect<br>
composure; and delighted as I was with the confused plan of it in my<br>
head, I was easily mistress enough of myself to support the part of<br>
ignorance I had prescribed to myself; and as all this circle of<br>
reflections was instantly over, I stole a tip-toe to the passage door,<br>
and opening it with a noise, passed for having that moment come home;<br>
and after a short pause, as if to pull off my things, I opened the door<br>
into the dining room, where I fund the dowdy blowing the fire, and my<br>
faithful shepherd walking about the room, and wistling, as cool and<br>
unconcerned as if nothing had happened. I think, however, he had not<br>
much to brag of having out-dissembled me: for I kept up, nobly, the<br>
character of our sex for art, and went up to him with the same open air<br>
of frankness as I had ever received him. He stayed but a little while,<br>
made some excuse for not being able to stay the evening with me, and<br>
went out.<br>
<br>
As for the wench, she was now spoiled, at least for my servant; and<br>
scarce eight and forty hours were gone round, before her insolence, on<br>
what had passed betwen Mr. H.... and her, gave me so fair an occasion<br>
to turn her away, at a minute&rsquo;s warning, that, not to have done it<br>
would have been the wonder; so that he could neither disapprove it nor<br>
find in it the least reason to suspect my original motive. What became<br>
of her afterwards, I know not; but generous as Mr. H.... was, he<br>
undoubtedly made her amends: though, I dare answer, that he kept up no<br>
further commerce with her of that sort; as his stooping to such a<br>
coarse morsel, was only a sudden sally of lust, on seeing a wholesome<br>
looking, buxom country wench, and no more strange than hunger, or even<br>
a whimsical appetite&rsquo;s making a fling meal of neck-beef, for change of<br>
diet.<br>
<br>
Had I considered this escapade of Mr. H.... in no more than that light<br>
and contented myself with turning away the wench, I had thought and<br>
acted right; but, flushed as I was with imaginary wrongs, I should have<br>
held Mr. H... to have been cheaply off, if I had not pushed my revenge<br>
farther, and repaid him, as exactly as could for the soul of me, in the<br>
same coin.<br>
<br>
Nor was this worthy act of justice long delayed: I had it too much at<br>
heart. Mr. H... had, about a fortnight before, taken into his service a<br>
tenant&rsquo;s son, just come out the country, a very handsome young lad,<br>
scarce turned of nineteen, fresh as a rose, well sharped and clear<br>
limbed: in short, a very good excuse for any woman&rsquo;s liking, even<br>
though revenge had been out of the question; any woman, I say, who was<br>
disprejudiced, and that wit and spirit enough to prefer a point of<br>
pleasure to a point of pride.<br>
<br>
Mr. H... had clapped a livery upon him; and his chief employ was, after<br>
being shewn my lodgings, to bring and carry letters or messages between<br>
his master and me; and as the situation of all kept ladies is not the<br>
fittest to inspire respect, even to the meanest of mankind, and,<br>
perhaps, less of it from the most ignorant, I could not help observing<br>
that this lad, who was, I suppose, acquainted with my relation to his<br>
master by his fellow servants, used to eye me in that bashful confused<br>
way, more expressive, more moving and readier caught at by our sex,<br>
than any other declarations whatever: my figure had, it seems, struck<br>
him, and modest and innocent as he was, he did not himself know that<br>
the pleasure he took in looking at me was love, or desire; but his<br>
eyes, naturally wanton, and now inflamed with passion, spoke a great<br>
deal more than he durst have imagined they did. Hitherto, indeed, I had<br>
only taken notice of the comeliness of the youth, but without the least<br>
design: my pride alone would have guarded me from a thought that way,<br>
had not Mr. H....&rsquo;s condescension with my maid, where there was not<br>
half the temptation, in point of person, set me a dangerous example;<br>
but now I began to look on this stripling as every way a delicious<br>
instrument of my designed retaliation upon Mr. H.... of an obligation<br>
for which I should have made a conscience to die in his debt.<br>
<br>
In order then to pave the way for the accomplishment of my scheme, for<br>
two or three times that the young fellow came to me with messages, I<br>
managed so, or without affectation to have him admitted to my bed side,<br>
or brought to me at my toilet, where I was dressing; and by carelessly<br>
shewing or letting him, as if without meaning or design, sometimes my<br>
bosom rather more bare than it should be; sometimes my hair, of which I<br>
had a very fine head, in the natural flow of it while combing;<br>
sometimes a neat leg, that had unfortunately slipt its garter, which I<br>
made no scruple of tying before him, easily gave him the impressions<br>
favourable to my purpose, which I could perceive to sparkle in his<br>
eyes, and glow in his cheeks: then certain slight squeezes by the hand,<br>
as I took letters from him, did his business completely.<br>
<br>
When I saw him thus moved, and fired for my purpose, I inflamed him yet<br>
more, by asking him several leading questions, such as: &ldquo;Had he a<br>
mistress?... was she prettier than me?... could he love such a one as I<br>
was?...&rdquo; and the like; to all which the blushing simpleton answered to<br>
my wish, in a strain of perfect nature, perfect undebauched innocence,<br>
but with all the awkwardness and simplicity of country breeding.<br>
<br>
When I thought I had sufficiently ripened him for the laudable point I<br>
had in view, one day that I expected him at a particular hour, I took<br>
care to have the coast clear for the reception I designed him; and, as<br>
I laid it, he came to the dining room door, tapped at it, and, in my<br>
bidding him come in; he did so, and shut the door after him. I desired<br>
him, then, to bolt it on the inside, pretending it would not otherwise<br>
keep shut.<br>
<br>
I was then lying at length upon that very couch, the scene of Mr.<br>
H....&rsquo;s polite joys, in an undress, which was with all the art of<br>
negligence flowing loose, and in a most tempting disorder: no stays, no<br>
hoop..., no incumbrance whatever. On the other hand, he stood at a<br>
little distance, that gave me a full view of a fine featured, shapely,<br>
healthy country lad, breathing the sweets of fresh blooming youth; his<br>
hair, which was of a perfect shining black, played to his face in<br>
natural side curls, and was set out with a smart tuck-up behind; new<br>
buckskin breechs, that, clipping close, shewed the shape of a plump,<br>
well made thigh; white stockings, garter-laced livery, shoulder knot,<br>
altogether composed a figure of pure flesh and blood, and appeared<br>
under no disgrace from the lowness of a dress, to which a certain<br>
spruce neatness seems peculiarly fitted.<br>
<br>
I bid him come towards me, and give me his letter, at the same time<br>
throwing down, carelessly, a book I had in my hands. He coloured, and<br>
came within reach of delivering me the letter, which he held out,<br>
awkwardly enough, for me to take, with his eyes rivetted on my bosom,<br>
which was, through the designed disorder of my handkerchief,<br>
sufficiently bare, and rather than hid.<br>
<br>
I, smiling in his face, took the letter, and immediately catching hold<br>
of his shirt sleeve, drew him towards me, blushing, and almost<br>
trembling; for surely his extreme bashfulness, and utter inexperience<br>
called for, at least, all the advances to encourage him: his body was<br>
now conveniently inclined toward me, and just softly chucking his<br>
beardless chin, I asked him: &ldquo;If he was afraid of a lady?...&rdquo; and with<br>
that took, and carrying his hands to my breasts, I press it tenderly to<br>
them. They were now finely furnished, and raised in flesh, so that,<br>
panting with desire, they rose and fell, in quick heaves, under his<br>
touch: at this, the boy&rsquo;s eyes began to lighten with all the fires of<br>
inflamed nature, and his cheeks flushed with a deep scarlet:<br>
tongue-tied with joy, rapture, and bashfulness, he could not speak, but<br>
then his looks, his emotion, sufficiently satisfied me that my train<br>
had taken, and that I had no disappointment to fear.<br>
<br>
My lips, which I threw in his way, so that he could not escape kissing<br>
them, fixed, fired, and emboldened him: and now, glancing my eyes<br>
towards that part of his dress which covered the essential object of<br>
enjoyment, I plainly discovered the swell and commotion there; and as I<br>
was now too far advanced to stop in so fair a way, and was indeed no<br>
longer able to contain myself, or wait the slower progress of his<br>
maiden bash-fulness (for such it seemed, and really was), I stole my<br>
hands upon his thighs, down one of which I could both see and feel a<br>
stiff hard body, confined by his breeches, that my fingers could<br>
discover no end to. Curious then, and eager to unfold so alarming a<br>
mystery, playing, as it were, with his buttons, which were bursting<br>
ripe from the active force within, those of his waistband and fore-flap<br>
flew open at a touch, when out IT started; and now, disengaged from the<br>
shirt, I saw, with wonder and surprise, what? not the play thing of a<br>
boy, not the weapon of a man, but a Maypole, of so enormous a standard,<br>
that had proportions been observed, it must have belonged to a young<br>
giant. Yet I could not, without pleasure, behold, and even venture to<br>
feel, such a length, such a breadth of animated ivory! perfectly well<br>

violence of the tilt; and soon, indeed! the highwrought agitation, the<br>
sweet urgency of this to-and-fro friction, raised the titillation on me<br>
to its height; so that finding myself on the point of going, and loath<br>
to leave the tender partner of my joys behind me, I employed all the<br>
forwarding motions and arts my experience suggested to me, to promote<br>
his keeping me company to our journey&rsquo;s end. I not only then tightened<br>
the pleasure-girth round my restless inmate, by a secret spring of<br>
friction and compression that obeys the will in those parts, but stole<br>
my hand softly to that store bag of nature&rsquo;s prime sweets, which is so<br>
pleasingly attached to its conduit pipe, from which we receive them;<br>
there feeling, and most gently indeed, squeezing those tender globular<br>
reservoirs, the magic touch took instant effect, quickened, and brought<br>
on upon the spur the symptoms of that sweet agony, the melting moment<br>
of dissolution, when pleasure dies by pleasure, and the mysterious<br>
engine of it overcomes the titillation it has raised in those parts, by<br>
plying them with the stream of a warm liquid, that in itself the<br>
highest of all titillations, and which they thirstily express and draw<br>
in like the hot natured leach, which, to cool itself, tenaciously<br>
extracts all the moisture within its sphere of execution. Chiming then<br>
to me, with exquisite consent, as I melted away, his oily balsamic<br>
injection, mixing deliciously with the sluices in flow from me,<br>
sheathed and blunted all the stings of pleasure, whilst a voluptuous<br>
languor possest, and still maintained us motionless, and fast locked in<br>
one another&rsquo;s arms. Alas! that these delights should be no<br>
longer-lived; for now the point of pleasure, unedged by enjoyment, and<br>
all the brisk sensations flattened upon us, resigned us up to the cool<br>
cares of insipid life. Disengaging myself then from his embrace, I made<br>
him sensible of the reasons there were for his present leaving me; on<br>
which, though reluctantly, he put on his clothes, with as little<br>
expedition, however, as he could help, wantonly interrupting himself,<br>
between whiles, with kisses, touches and embraces I could not refuse<br>
myself to. Yet he happily returned to his master before he was missed;<br>
but, at taking leave, I forced him (for he had sentiments enough to<br>
refuse it) to receive money enough to buy a silver watch, that great<br>
article of subaltern finery, which he at length accepted of, as a<br>
remembrance he was carefully to preserve of my affections.<br>
<br>
And here, Madam, I ought, perhaps, to make you an apology for this<br>
minute detail of things, that dwelt so strongly upon my memory, after<br>
so deep an impression; but, besides that this intrigue bred one great<br>
revolution in my life, which historical truth requires I should not<br>
sink from you, may I not presume that so exalted a pleasure ought not<br>
to be ungratefully forgotten, or suppressed by me, because I found it<br>
in a character in low life; where, by the by, it is oftener met with,<br>
purer, and more unsophisticated, than among the false, ridiculous<br>
refinements with which the great suffer themselves to be so grossly<br>
cheated by their pride: the great! than whom, there exist few amongst<br>
those they call the vulgar, who are more ignorant of, or who cultivate<br>
less, the art of living than they do; they, I say, who for ever mistake<br>
things the most foreign to the nature of pleasure itself; whose capital<br>
favourite object is enjoyment of beauty, wherever that rare invaluable<br>
gift is found, without distinction of birth, or station.<br>
<br>
As love never had, so now revenge had no longer any share in my<br>
commerce in this handsome youth. The sole pleasures of enjoyment were<br>
now the link I held to him by: for though nature had done such great<br>
maters for him in his outward form, and especially in that superb piece<br>
of furniture she had so liberally enriched him with; though he was thus<br>
qualified to give the senses their richest feast, still there was<br>
something more wanting to create in me, and constitute the passion of<br>
love. Yet Will had very good qualities too: gentle, tractable, and,<br>
above all, grateful; silentious, even to a fault: he spoke, at any<br>
time, very little, but made it up emphatically with action; and, to do<br>
him justice, he never gave me the least reason to complain, either of<br>
any tendency to encroach upon me for the liberties I allowed him, or of<br>
his indiscretion in blabbing them. There is, then, a fatality in love,<br>
or have loved him I must; for he was really a treasure, a bit for the<br>
Bonne Bouche of a duchess; and, to say the truth, my liking for him was<br>
so extreme, that it was distinguishing very nicely to deny that I loved<br>
him.<br>
<br>
My happiness, however, with him did not last long, but found an end<br>
from my own imprudent neglect. After having taken even superfluous<br>
precautions against a discovery, our success in repeated meetings<br>
emboldened me to omit the barely necessary ones. About a month after<br>
our first intercourse, one fatal morning (the season Mr. H.... rarely<br>
or never visited me in) I was in my closet, where my toilet stood, in<br>
nothing but my shift, a bed gown and under petticoat. Will was with me,<br>
and both ever too well disposed to baulk an opportunity. For my part, a<br>
whim, a wanton toy had just taken me, and I had challenged my man to<br>
execute it on the spot, who hesitated not to comply with my humour: I<br>
was set in the arm chair, my shift and petticoat up, my thighs wide<br>
spread and mounted over the arms of the chair, presenting the fairest<br>
mark to Will&rsquo;s drawn weapon, which he stood in act to plunge into me,<br>
when, having neglected to secure the chamber door, and that of the<br>
closet standing a-jar, Mr. H.... stole in upon us, before either of us<br>
was aware, and saw us precisely in these convicting attitudes.<br>
<br>
I gave a great scream, and dropped my petticoat: the thunder-struck lad<br>
stood trembling and pale, waiting his sentence of death. Mr. H....<br>
looked sometimes at one, sometimes at the other, with a mixture of<br>
indignation and scorn; and, without saying a word, spun upon his heel<br>
and went out.<br>
<br>
As confused as I was, I heard him very distinctly turn the key, and<br>
lock the chamber door upon us, so that there was no escape but through<br>
the dining room, where he himself was walking about with distempered<br>
strides, stamping in a great chafe, and doubtless debating what he<br>
would do with us.<br>
<br>
In the mean time, poor William was frightened out of his senses, and,<br>
as much need as I had of spirits myself, I was obliged to employ them<br>
all to keep his a little up. The misfortune I had now brought upon him,<br>
endeared him the more to me, and I could have joyfully suffered any<br>
punishment he had not shared in. I watered, plentifully, with my tears,<br>
the face of the frightened youth, who sat, not having strength to<br>
stand, as cold and as lifeless as a statue.<br>
<br>
Presently Mr. H.... comes in to us again, and made us go before him<br>
into the dining room, trembling and dreading the issue, Mr. H.....sat<br>
down on a chair whilst we stood like criminals under examination; and,<br>
beginning with me, asked me, with an even firm tone of voice, neither<br>
soft nor severe, but cruelly indifferent, what I could say for myself,<br>
for having abused him in so unworthy a manner, with his own servant<br>
too, and how he had deserved this of me?<br>
<br>
Without adding to the guilt of my infidelity, that of an audacious<br>
defence of it, in the old style of a common kept miss, my answer was<br>
modest, and often interrupted by my tears, in substance as follows:<br>
&ldquo;That I never had a single thought of wronging him&rdquo; (which was true),<br>
&ldquo;till I had seen him taking the last liberties with my servant wench&rdquo;<br>
(here he coloured prodigiously), &ldquo;and that my resentment at that, which<br>
I was over-awed from giving vent to by complaints, or explanations with<br>
him, had driven me to a course that I did not pretend to justify; but<br>
that as to the young man, he was entirely faultless; for that, in the<br>
view of making him the instrument of my revenge, I had down right<br>
seduced him to what he had done; and therefore hoped, whatever he<br>
determined about me, he would distinguish between the guilty and the<br>
innocent; and that; for the rest, I was entirely at his mercy.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
Mr. H.... on hearing what I said, hung his head a little; but instantly<br>
recovering himself, he said to me, as near as I can retain, to the<br>
following purpose:<br>
<br>
&ldquo;Madam, I owe shame to myself, and confess you have fairly turned the<br>
tables upon me. It is not with one of your cast of breeding and<br>
sentiments, that I allow you so much reason on your side, as great<br>
difference of the provocations: be it sufficient that I should enter<br>
into a discussion of the very to have changed my resolution, in<br>
consideration of what you reproach me with; and I own, too, that your<br>
clearing that rascal there, is fair and honest in you. Renew with you I<br>
cannot: the affront is too gross. I give you a week&rsquo;s warning to get<br>
out of these lodgings; whatever I have given you, remains to you; and<br>
as I never intend to see you more, the landlord will pay you fifty<br>
pieces on my account, with which, and every debt paid, I hope you will<br>
own I do not leave you in a worse condition than what I took you up in,<br>
or that you deserve of me. Blame yourself only that it is no better.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
Then, without giving me time to reply, he addressed himself to the<br>
young fellow:<br>
<br>
&ldquo;For you, spark, I shall, for your father&rsquo;s sake, take care of you: the<br>
town is no place for such an easy fool as thou art; and to-morrow you<br>
shall set out, under the charge of one of my men, well recommended, in<br>
my name, to your father, not to let you return and be spoil&rsquo;d here.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
At these words he went out, after my vainly attempting to stop him, by<br>
throwing myself at his feet. He shook me off, though he seemed greatly<br>
moved too, and took Will away with him, who, I dare swear, thought<br>
himself very cheaply off.<br>
<br>
I was now once more a-drift, and left upon my own hands, by a gentleman<br>
whom I certainly did not deserve. And all the letters, arts, friends,<br>
entreaties that I employed within the week of grace in my lodging,<br>
could never win on him so much as to see me again. He had irrevocably<br>
pronounced my doom, and submission to it was my only part. Soon after<br>
he married a lady of birth and fortune, to whom, I have heard he proved<br>
an irreproachable husband.<br>
<br>
As for poor Will, he was immediately sent down to the country to his<br>
father, who was an easy farmer, where he was not four months before an<br>
inn-keepers&rsquo; buxom young widow, with a very good stock, both in money<br>
and trade, fancied, and perhaps pre-acquainted with his secret<br>
excellencies, married him: and I am sure there was, at least, one good<br>
foundation for their living happily together.<br>
<br>
Though I should have been charmed to see him before he went, such<br>
measures were taken, by Mr. H....&rsquo;s orders, that it was impossible;<br>
otherwise I should certainly have endeavoured to detain him in town,<br>
and would have spared neither offers nor expense to have procured<br>
myself the satisfaction of keeping him with me. He had such powerful<br>
holds upon my inclinations as were not easily to be shaken off, or<br>
replaced; as to my heart, it was quite out of the question: glad,<br>
however, I was from my soul, that nothing worse, and as things turned<br>
out, nothing better could have happened to him.<br>
<br>
As to Mr. H..., though views of conveniency made me, at first, exert<br>
myself to regain his affection, I was giddy and thoughtless enough to<br>
be much easier reconciled to my failure than I ought to have been; but<br>
as I never had loved him, and his leaving me gave me a sort of liberty<br>
that I had often longed for, I was soon comforted; and flattering<br>
myself, that the stock of youth and beauty I was going to trade with,<br>
could hardly fail of procuring me a maintenance, I saw myself under the<br>
necessity of trying my fortune with them, rather, with pleasure and<br>
gaiety, than with the least idea of despondency.<br>
<br>
In the mean time, several of my acquaintances among the sisterhood, who<br>
had soon got wind of my misfortune, flocked to insult me with their<br>
malicious consolations. Most of them had long envied me the affluence<br>
and splendour I had been maintained in; and though there was scarce one<br>
of them that did not at least deserve to be in my case, and would<br>
probably, sooner or later, come to it, it was equally easy to remark,<br>
even in their affected pity, their secret pleasure at seeing me thus<br>
discarded, and their secret grief that it was no worse with me.<br>
Unaccountable malice of the human heart! and which is not confined to<br>
the class of life they were of.<br>
<br>
But as the time approached for me to come to some resolution how to<br>
dispose of myself, and I was considering, round where to shift my<br>
quarters to, Mrs. Cole, a middle aged discreet sort of woman, who had<br>
been brought into my acquaintance by one of the misses that visited me,<br>
upon learning my situation, came to offer her cordial advice and<br>
service to me; and as I had always taken to her more than to any of my<br>
female acquaintances, I listened the easier to her proposals. And, as<br>
it happened, I could not have put myself into worse, or into better<br>
hands in all London: into worse, because keeping a house of<br>
conveniency, there were no lengths in lewdness she would not advise me<br>
to go, in compliance with her customers; no schemes, or pleasure, or<br>
even unbounded debauchery, she did not take even a delight in<br>
promoting: into a better, because nobody having had more experience of<br>
the wicked part of the town than she had, was fitter to advise and<br>
guard one against the worst dangers of our profession; and what was<br>
rare to be met with in those of her&rsquo;s, she contented herself with a<br>
moderate living profit upon her industry and good offices, and had<br>
nothing of their greedy rapacious turn. She was really too a<br>
gentlewoman born and bred, but through a train of accidents reduced to<br>
this course, which she pursued, partly through necessity, partly<br>
through choice, as never woman delighted more in encouraging a brisk<br>
circulation of the trade, for the sake of the trade itself, or better<br>
understood all the mysteries and refinements of it, than she did; so<br>
that she was consummately at the top of her profession, and dealt only<br>
with customers of distinction: to answer the demands of whom she kept a<br>
competent number of her daughters in constant recruit (so she called<br>
those whom their youth and personal charms recommended to her adoption<br>
and management: several of whom, by her means, and through her tuition<br>
and instructions, succeeded very well in the world).<br>
<br>
This useful gentlewoman upon whose protection I now threw myself,<br>
having her reasons of state, respecting Mr. H...., for not appearing<br>
too much in the thing herself, sent a friend of her&rsquo;s, on the day<br>
appointed for my removal, to conduct me to my new lodgings at a<br>
brush-maker&rsquo;s in E&mdash;&mdash; street, Covent Garden, the very next door to her<br>
own house, where she had no conveniences to lodge me herself: lodgings<br>
that, by having been for several successions tenanted by ladies of<br>
pleasures, the landlord of them was familiarized to their ways; and<br>
provided the rent was paid, every thing else was as easy and commodious<br>
as one could desire.<br>
<br>
The fifty guineas promised me by Mr. H...., at his parting with me,<br>
having been duly paid me, all my clothes and moveables chested up,<br>
which were at least of two hundred pounds value, I had them conveyed<br>
into a coach, where I soon followed them, after taking a civil leave of<br>
the landlord and his family, with whom I had never lived in a degree of<br>
familiarity enough to regret the removal; but still, the very<br>
circumstance of its being a removal, drew tears from me. I left, too, a<br>
letter of thanks for Mr. H...., from whom I concluded myself, as I<br>
really was, irretrievably separated.<br>
<br>
My maid I had discharged the day before, not only because I had her of<br>
Mr. H...., but that I suspected her of having some how or other been<br>
the occasion of his discovering me, in revenge, perhaps, for my not<br>
having trusted her with him.<br>
<br>
We soon got to my lodgings, which, though not so handsomely furnished,<br>
nor so showy as those I left, were to the full as convenient, and at<br>
half price, though on the first floor. My trunks were safely landed,<br>
and stowed in my apartments, where my neighbour, and now gouvernante,<br>
Mrs. Cole, was ready with my landlord to receive me, to whom she took<br>
care to set me out in the most favourable light, that of one from whom<br>
there was the clearest reason to expect the regular payment of his<br>
rent: all the cardinal virtues attributed to me, would not have had<br>
half the weight of that recommendation alone.<br>
<br>
I was now settled in lodgings of my own, abandoned to my own conduct,<br>
and turned loose upon the town, to sink or swim, as I could manage with<br>
the current of it; and what were the consequences, together with the<br>
number of adventures which befell me in the exercise of my new<br>
profession, will compose the mater of another letter: for surely it is<br>
high time to put a period! to this.<br>
<br>
I am,<br>
<br>
MADAM,<br>
<br>
Yours, etc., etc., etc.<br>
<br>
THE END OF THE FIRST LETTER<br>
<br>
<br>
<br>
LETTER THE SECOND<br>
<br>
Madam,<br>
<br>
If I have delayed the sequel of my history, it has been purely to allow<br>
myself a little breathing time not without some hopes, that, instead of<br>
pressing me to a continuation, you would have acquitted me of the task<br>
of pursuing a confession, in the course of which my self-esteem has so<br>
many wounds to sustain.<br>
<br>
I imagined, indeed, that you would have been cloyed and tired with<br>
uniformity of adventures and expressions, inseparable from a subject of<br>
this sort, whose bottom, or groundwork being, in the nature of things<br>
eternally one and the same, whatever variety of forms and modes the<br>
situations are susceptible of, there is no escaping a repetition of<br>
near the same images, the same figures, the same expressions, with this<br>
further inconvenience added to the disgust it creates, that the words<br>
Joys, Ardours, Transports, Extasies and the rest of those pathetic<br>
terms so congenial to, so received in the Practice of Pleasure, flatten<br>
and lose much of their due spirit and energy by the frequency they<br>
indispensably recur with, in a narrative of which that Practice<br>
professedly composes the whole basis. I must therefore trust to the<br>
candour of your judgment, for your allowing for the disadvantage I am<br>
necessarily under in that respect; and to your imagination and<br>
sensibility, the pleasing taks of repairing it, by their supplements,<br>
where my descriptions flag or fail: the one will readily place the<br>
pictures I present before your eyes; the other give life to the colours<br>
where they are dull, or worn with too frequent handling.<br>
<br>
What you say besides, by way of encouragement concerning the extreme<br>
difficulty of continuing so long in one strain, in a mean tempered with<br>
taste, between the revoltingness of gross, rank and vulgar expressions,<br>
and the ridicule of mincing metaphors and affected circumlocutions, is<br>
so sensible, as well as good-natured, that you greatly justify me to<br>
myself for my compliance with a curiosity that is to be satisfied so<br>
extremely at my expense.<br>
<br>
Resuming now where I broke off in my last, I am in my way to remark to<br>
you, that it was late in the evening before I arrived at my lodgings,<br>
and Mrs. Cole, after helping me to range and secure my things, spent<br>
the whole evening with me in my apartment, where we supped together, in<br>
giving me the best advice and instruction with regard to the new stage<br>
of my profession I was now to enter upon; and passing thus from a<br>
private devotee to pleasure into a public one, to become a more general<br>
good, with all the advantages requisite to put my person out to use,<br>
either for interest or pleasure, or both. &ldquo;But then,&rdquo; she observed, &ldquo;as<br>
I was a kind of new face upon the town, that is, was an established<br>
rule, and part of trade, for me to pass for a maid and dispose of<br>
myself as such on the first good occasion, without prejudice, however,<br>
to such diversions as I might have a mind to in the interim; for that<br>
nobody could be a greater enemy than she was to the losing of time.<br>
That she would, in the mean time, do her best to find out a proper<br>
person, and would undertake to manage this nice point for me, if I<br>
would accept of her aid and advice to such good purpose, that, in the<br>
loss of a fictitious maidenhead, I should reap all the advantages of a<br>
native one.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
As too great a delicacy of sentiments did not extremely belong to my<br>
character at that time, I confess, against myself, that I perhaps too<br>
readily closed with a proposal which my candor and ingenuity gave me<br>
some repugnance to: but not enough to contradict the intention of one<br>
to whom I had now thoroughly abandoned the direction of all my steps.<br>
For Mrs. Cole had, I do not know how unless by one of those<br>
unaccountable invincible sympathies that, nevertheless, from the<br>
strongest links, especially of female friendship, won and got entire<br>
possession of me. On her side, she pretended that a strict resemblance,<br>
she fancied she saw in me, to an only daughter whom she had lost at my<br>
age, was the first motive of her taking to me so affectionately as she<br>
did. It might be so: there exist a slender motives of attachment, that,<br>
gathering force from habit and liking, have proved often more solid and<br>
durable than those founded on much stronger reasons; but this I know,<br>
that though I had no other acquaintance with her, than seeing her at my<br>
lodgings, when I lived with Mr. H..., where she had made errands to<br>
sell me some millinery ware, she had by degrees insinuated herself so<br>
far into my confidence, that I threw myself blindly into her hands, and<br>
came, at length, to regard, love, and obey her implicitly; and, to do<br>
her justice, I never experienced at her hands other than a sincerity of<br>
tenderness, and care for my interest, hardly heard of in those of her<br>
profession. We parted that night, after having settled a perfect<br>
unreserved agreement; and the next morning Mrs. Cole came, and took me<br>
with her to her house for the first time.<br>
<br>
Here, at the first sight of things, I found every thing breathe an air<br>
of decency, modesty and order.<br>
<br>
In the outer parlour, or rather shop, sat three young women, rather<br>
demurely employed on millinery work, which was the cover of a traffic<br>
in more precious commodities; but three beautifuller creatures could<br>
hardly be seen. Two of them were extremely fair, the eldest not above<br>
nineteen; and the third, much about that age, was a piquant brunette,<br>
whose black sparking eyes, and perfect harmony of features and shape,<br>
left her nothing to envy in her fairer companions. Their dress too had<br>
the more design in it, the less it appeared to have, being in a taste<br>
of uniform correct neatness, and elegant simplicity. These were the<br>
girls that composed the small domestic flock, which my governess<br>
trained up with surprising order and management, considering the giddy<br>
wildness of young girls once got upon the loose. But then she never<br>
continued any in her house, whom, after a due noviciate, she found<br>
un-tractable, or unwilling to comply with the rules of it. Thus she had<br>
insensibly formed a little family of love, in which the members found<br>
so sensibly their account, in a rare alliance of pleasure and interest,<br>
and of a necessary outward decency, with unbounded secret liberty, that<br>
Mrs. Cole, who had picked them as much for their temper as their<br>
beauty, governed them with ease to herself and them too.<br>
<br>
To these pupils then of hers, whom she had prepared, she presented me<br>
as a new boarder, and one that was to be immediately admitted to all<br>
the intimacies of the house; upon which these charming girls gave me<br>
all the marks of a welcome reception, and indeed of being perfectly<br>
pleased with my figure, that I could possibly expect from any of my own<br>
sex: but they had been effectually brought to sacrifice all jealousy,<br>
or competition of charms, to a common interest, and considered me a<br>
partner that was bringing no despicable stock of goods into the trade<br>
of the house. They gathered round me, viewed me on all sides; and as my<br>
admission into this joyous troop made a little holiday, the shew of<br>
work was laid aside; and Mrs. Cole giving me up, with special<br>
recommendation, to their caresses and entertainment, went about her<br>
ordinary business of the house.<br>
<br>
The sameness of our sex, age, profession, and views, soon creased as<br>
unreserved a freedom and intimacy as if we had been for years<br>
acquainted. They took and shewed me the house, their respective<br>
apartments, which were furnished with every article of convenience and<br>
luxury; and above all, a spacious drawing-room, where a select<br>
revelling band usually met, in general parties of pleasure; the girls<br>
supping with their sparks, and acting their wanton pranks with<br>
unbounded licentiousness; whilst a defiance of awe, modesty or jealousy<br>
were their standing rules, by which, according to the principles of<br>
their society, whatever pleasure was lost on the side of sentiment, was<br>
abundantly made up to the senses in the poignancy of variety, and the<br>
charms of ease and luxury. The authors and supporters of this secret<br>
institution would, in the height of their humour, style themselves the<br>
restorers of the golden age and its simplicity of pleasures, before<br>
their innocence became so unjustly branded with the names of guilt and<br>
shame.<br>
<br>
As soon then as the evening began, and the shew of a shop was shut, the<br>
academy opened; the mask of mock-modesty was completely taken off, and<br>
all the girls delivered over to their respective calls of pleasure or<br>
interest with their men: and none of that sex was promiscuously<br>
admitted, but only such as Mrs. Cole was previously satisfied with<br>
their character and discretion. In short, this was the safest,<br>
politest, and, at the same time, the most thorough house of<br>
accommodation in town: every thing being conducted so, that decency<br>
made no intrenchment upon the most libertine pleasures; in the practice<br>
of which, too, the choice familiars of the house had found the secret<br>
so rare and difficult, of reconciling even all the refinements of taste<br>
and delicacy, with the most gross and determinate gratifications of<br>
sensuality.<br>
<br>
After having consumed the morning in the dear endearments and<br>
instructions of my new acquaintance, we went to dinner, when Mrs. Cole,<br>
presiding at the head of her club, gave me the first idea of her<br>
management and address, in inspiring these girls with so sensible a<br>
love and respect for her. There was no stiffness, no reserve, no airs<br>
of pique, or little jealousies, but all was unaffectedly gay, cheerful<br>
and easy.<br>
<br>
After dinner, Mrs. Cole, seconded by the young ladies, acquainted me<br>
that there was a chapter to be held that night in form, for the<br>
ceremony of my reception into the sisterhood; and in which, with all<br>
due reserve to my maidenhead, that was to be occasionally cooked up for<br>
the first proper chapman. I was to undergo a ceremonial of initiation<br>
they were sure I should not be displeased with.<br>
<br>
Embarked as I was, and moreover captivated with the charms of my new<br>
companions, I was too much prejudiced in favour of any proposal they<br>
could make, to as much as hesitate an assent; which, therefore, readily<br>
giving in the style of a carte blanche, I received fresh kisses of<br>
compliment from them all, in approval of my docility and good nature.<br>
Now I was &ldquo;a sweet girl... I came into things with a good grace... I<br>
was not affectedly coy... I should be the pride of the house,&rdquo; and the<br>
like.<br>
<br>
This point thus adjusted, the young women left Mrs. Cole to talk and<br>
concert matters with me, when she explained to me, that &ldquo;I should be<br>
introduced that very evening, to four of her best friends, one of whom<br>
she had, according to the custom of the house, favoured with the<br>
preference of engaging me in the first party of pleasure;&rdquo; assuring me,<br>
at the same time, &ldquo;that they were all young gentlemen agreeable in<br>
their persons, and unexceptionable in every respect; that united, and<br>
holding together by the band of common pleasures, they composed the<br>
chief support of her house, and made very liberal presents to the girls<br>
that pleased and humoured them, so that they were, properly speaking,<br>
the founders and patrons of this little seraglio. Not but that she had,<br>
at proper seasons, other customers to deal with, whom she stood less<br>
upon punctilio with, than with these; for instance, it was not on one<br>
of them she could attempt to pass me for a maid; they were not only too<br>
knowing, too much town-bred to bite at such a bait, but they were such<br>
generous benefactors to her, that it would be unpardonable to think of<br>
it.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
Amidst all the flutter and emotion which this promise of pleasure, for<br>
such I conceived it, stirred up in me, I preserved so much of the<br>
woman, as to feign just reluctance enough to make some merit, of<br>
sacrificing it to the influence of my patroness, whom I likewise, still<br>
in character, reminded of it perhaps being right for me to go home and<br>
dress, in favour of my first impressions.<br>
<br>
But Mrs. Cole, in opposition to this, assured me, &ldquo;that the gentlemen I<br>
should be presented to were, by their rank and taste of things,<br>
infinitely superior to the being touched with any glare of dress or<br>
ornaments, such slick women rather confound and overlay than set off<br>
their beauty with; that these veteran voluptuaries knew better than not<br>
to hold them in the highest contempt: they with whom the pure native<br>
charms alone could pass current, and who would at any time leave a<br>
sallow, washy, painted duchess on her own hands, for a ruddy, healthy<br>
firm fleshed country maid; and as for my part, that nature had done<br>
enough for me, to set me above owing the least favour to art;&rdquo;<br>
concluding withal, that for the instant occasion, there was no dress<br>
like an undress.<br>
<br>
I thought my governess too good a judge of these matters, not to be<br>
easily overruled by her: after which she went on preaching very<br>
pathetically the doctrine of passive obedience and non-resistance to<br>
all those arbitrary tastes of pleasure, which are by some styled the<br>
refinements, and by others the depravations of it; between whom it was<br>
not the business of a simple girl, who was to profit by pleasing, to<br>
decide, but to conform to. Whilst I was edifying by these wholesome<br>
lessons, tea was brought in, and the young ladies, returning, joined<br>
company with us.<br>
<br>
After a great deal of mixed chat, frolic and humour, one of them,<br>
observing that there would be a good deal of time on and before the<br>
assembly hour, proposed that each girl should entertain the company<br>
with that critical period of her personal history, in which she first<br>
exchanged the maiden state for womanhood. The proposal was approved,<br>
with only one restriction of Mrs. Cole, that she, on account of her<br>
age, and I, on account of my titular maidenhead, should be excused, at<br>
least till I had undergone the forms of the house. This obtained me a<br>
dispensation, and the promotress of this amusement was desired to<br>
begin.<br>
<br>
Her name was Emily; a girl fair to excess, and whose limbs were, if<br>
possible, too well made, since their plump fulness was rather to the<br>
prejudice of that delicate slimness required by the nicer judges of<br>
beauty; her eyes were blue, and streamed inexpressible sweetness, and<br>
nothing could be prettier than her mouth and lips, which closed over a<br>
range of the evenest and whitest teeth. Thus she began:<br>
<br>
&ldquo;Neither my extraction, nor the most critical adventure of my life, is<br>
sublime enough to impeach me of any vanity in the advancement of the<br>
proposal you have approved of. My father and mother were, and for aught<br>
I know, are still, farmers in the country, not above forty miles from<br>
town: their barbarity to me, in favour of a son, on whom alone they<br>
vouchsafed to bestow their tenderness, had a thousand times determined<br>
me to fly their house, and throw myself on the wide world; but, at<br>
length, an accident forced me on this desperate attempt at the age of<br>
fifteen. I had broken a chinabowl, the pride and idol of both their<br>
hearts; and as an unmerciful beating was the least I had to depend on<br>
at their hands, in the silliness of these tender years, I left the<br>
house, and, at all adventures, took the road to London. How my loss was<br>
resented I do not know, for till this instant I have not heard a<br>
syllable about them. My whole stock was two broad pieces of my<br>
godmother&rsquo;s, a few shillings, silver shoe-buckles and a silver thimble.<br>
Thus equipped, with no more clothes than the ordinary ones I had on my<br>
back, and frightened at every foot or noise I heard behind me, I<br>
hurried on; and I dare sweare, walked a dozen miles before I stopped,<br>
through mere weariness and fatigue. At length I sat down on a style,<br>
wept bitterly, and yet was still rather under increased impressions of<br>
fear on the account of my escape; which made me dread, worse than<br>
death, the going back to my unnatural parents. Refreshed by this little<br>
repose, and relieved by my tears, I was proceeding onward, when I was<br>
overtaken by a sturdy country lad, who was going to London to see what<br>
he could do for himself there, and, like me, had given his friends the<br>
slip. He could not be above seventeen, was ruddy, well featured enough,<br>
with uncombed flaxen hair, a little flapped hat, kersey frock, yarn<br>
stockings, in short, a perfect plough boy. I saw him come whistling<br>
behind me, with a bundle tied to the end of a stick, his travelling<br>
equipage. We walked by one another for some time without speaking; at<br>
length we joined company, and agreed to keep together till we got to<br>
our journey&rsquo;s end; what his designs or ideas were, I know not: the<br>
innocence of mine I can solemnly protest.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;As night drew on, it became us to look out for some inn or shelter; to<br>
which perplexity another was added, and that was, what we should say<br>
for ourselves, if we were questioned. After some puzzle, the young<br>
fellow started a proposal, which I thought the finest that could be;<br>
and what was that? why, that we should pass for husband and wife: I<br>
never dreamed of consequences. We came presently, after having agreed<br>
on this notable experience, to one of those hedge accommodations for<br>
foot passengers, at the door of which stood an old crazy beldam, who<br>
seeing us trudge by, invited us to lodge there. Glad of any cover, we<br>
went in, and my fellow traveller, taking all upon him, called for what<br>
the house afforded, and we supped together as man and wife; which,<br>
considering our figures and ages, could not have passed on any one but<br>
such as any thing could pass on. But when bed-time came on, we had<br>
neither of us the courage to contradict our first account of ourselves;<br>
and what was extremely pleasant, the young lad seemed as perplexed as I<br>
was how to evade lying together, which was so natural for the state we<br>
had pretended to. Whilst we were in this quandary, the landlady takes<br>
the candles, and lights us to our apartment, through a long yard, at<br>
the end of which it stood, separate from the body of the house. Thus we<br>
suffered ourselves to be conducted, without saying a word in opposition<br>
to it; and there, in a wretched room, with a bed answerable, we were<br>
left to pass the night together, as a thing quite of course. For my<br>
part, I was so incredibly innocent, as not even to think much more harm<br>
of going into bed with the young man, than with one of our dairy<br>
wenches; nor had he, perhaps, any other notions than those of<br>
innocence, till such a fair occasion put them into his head.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;Before either of us undressed, however, he put out the candle; and the<br>
bitterness of the weather made it a kind of necessity for me to go into<br>
bed: slipping then my clothes off, I crept under the bedclothes, where<br>
I found the young stripling already nestled, and the touch of his warm<br>
flesh rather pleased than alarmed me. I was indeed too much disturbed<br>
with the novelty of my condition to be able to sleep; but then I had<br>
not the least thought of harm. But oh! how powerful are the instincts<br>
of nature! how little is there wanting to set them in action! The young<br>
man, sliding his arm under my body, drew me gently towards him, as if<br>
to keep himself and me warmer; and the heat I felt from joining our<br>
breasts, kindled another that I had hitherto never felt, and was, even<br>
then, a stranger to the nature of. Emboldened, I suppose, by my<br>
easiness, he ventured to kiss me, and I insensibly returned it; without<br>
knowing the consequence of returning it: for, on this encouragement, he<br>
slipped his hand all down from my breast to that part of me where the<br>
sense of feeling is so exquisitely critical, as I then experienced by<br>
its instant taking fire upon the touch, and glowing with a strange<br>
tickling heat: there he pleased himself and me, by feeling, till<br>
growing a little too bold with me, he hurt me, and made me complain.<br>
Then he took my hand, which he guided, not unwillingly on my side,<br>
between the twist of his closed thighs, which were extremely warm;<br>
there he lodged and pressed it, till raising it by degrees, he made me<br>
feel the proud distinction of his sex from mine. I was frightened at<br>
the novelty, and drew back my hand; yet, pressed and spurred on by<br>
sensations of a strange pleasure, I could not help asking him what that<br>
was for? He told me he would shew me if I would let him; and without<br>
waiting for my answer, which he prevented by stopping my mouth with<br>
kisses I was far from disrelishing, he got upon me, and inserting one<br>
of his thighs between mine, opened them so as to make way for himself,<br>
and fixed me to his purpose; whilst I was so much out of my usual<br>
sense, so subdued by the present power of a new one, that, between far<br>
and desire, I lay utter passive, till the piercing pain rouzed and made<br>
me cry out. But it was too late: he was too firm fixed in the saddle<br>
for me to compass flinging him, with all the struggles I could use,<br>
some of which only served to further his point, and at length an<br>
irresistible thrust murdered at once my maidenhead, and almost me. I<br>
now lay a bleeding witness of the necessity imposed on our sex, to<br>
gather the first honey off the thorns.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;But the pleasure rising as the pain subsided, I was soon reconciled to<br>
fresh trials, and before morning, nothing on earth could be dearer to<br>
me than this rifler of my virgin sweets: he was every thing to me now.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;How we agreed to join fortunes: how we came up to town together, where<br>
we lived some time, till necessity-parted us, and drove me into this<br>
course of life, to which I had been long ago bettered and torn to<br>
pieces before I came to this age, as much through my easiness, as<br>
through inclination, had it not been for my finding refuge in this<br>
house: these are all circumstances which pass the mark I proposed, so<br>
that here my narrative ends.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
In the order of our sitting, it was Harriet&rsquo;s turn to go on. Amongst<br>
all the beauties of our sex, that I had before, or have since seen, few<br>
indeed were the forms that could dispute excellence with her&rsquo;s; it was<br>
not delicate, but delicacy itself incarnate, such was the symmetry of<br>
her small but exactly fashioned limbs. Her complexion, fair as it was,<br>
appeared yet more fair, from the effect of two black eyes, the<br>
brilliancy of which gave her face more vivacity than belonged to the<br>
colour of it, which was only defended from paleness, by a sweetly<br>
pleasing blush in her cheeks, that grew fainter and fainter, till at<br>
length it died away insensibly into the overbearing white. Then her<br>
miniature features joined to finish the extreme sweetness of it, which<br>
was not belied by that of a temper turned to indolence, languor, and<br>
the pleasures of love. Pressed to subscribe her contingent, she smiled,<br>
blushed a little, and thus complied with our desires:<br>
<br>
&ldquo;My father was neither better nor worse than a miller near the city of<br>
York; and both he and my mother dying whilst I was an infant, I fell<br>
under the care of a widow and childless aunt, housekeeper to my lord<br>
N..., at his seat in the county of..., where she brought me up with all<br>
imaginable tenderness. I was not seventeen, as I am not now eighteen,<br>
before I had, on account of my person purely (for fortune I had<br>
notoriously none), several advantageous proposals; but whether nature<br>
was slow in making me sensible in her favourite passion, or that I had<br>
not seen any of the other sex who had stirred up the least emotion or<br>
curiosity to be better acquainted with it, I had, till that age,<br>
preserved a perfect innocence, even of thought: whilst my fears of I<br>
did not now well know what, made me no more desirous of marrying than<br>
of dying. My aunt, good woman, favoured my timorousness, which she<br>
looked on as childish affection, that her own experience might<br>
probably assure her would wear off in time, and gave my suitors proper<br>
answers for me.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;The family had not been down at that seat for years, so that it was<br>
neglected, and committed entirely to my aunt, and two old domestics to<br>
take care of it. Thus I had the full range of a spacious lonely house<br>
and gardens, situated at about half a mile distance from any other<br>
habitation, except, perhaps, a straggling cottage or so.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;Here, in tranquillity and innocence, I grew up without any memorable<br>
accident, till one fatal day I had, as I had often done before, left my<br>
aunt asleep, and secure for some hours, after dinner; and resorting to<br>
a kind of ancient summer house, at some distance from the house, I<br>
carried my work with me, and sat over a rivulet, which its door and<br>
window faced upon. Here I fell into a gentle breathing slumber, which<br>
stole upon my senses, as they fainted under the excessive heat of the<br>
season at that hour; a cane couch, with my work basked for a pillow,<br>
were all the conveniences of my short repose; for I was soon awaked and<br>
alarmed by a flounce, and noise of splashing in the water. I got up to<br>
see what was the matter; and what indeed should it be but the son of a<br>
neighbouring gentleman, as I afterwards found (for I had never seen him<br>
before), who had strayed that way with his gun, and heated by his<br>
sport, and the sultriness of the day, had been tempted by the freshness<br>
of the clear stream; so that presently stripping, he jumped into it on<br>
the other side, which bordered on a wood, some trees whereof, inclined<br>
down to the water, formed a pleasing shady recess, commodious to<br>
undress and leave his clothes under.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;My first emotions at the sight of this youth, naked in the water,<br>
were, with all imaginable respect to truth, those of surprise and fear;<br>
and, in course, I should immediately have run out, had not my modesty,<br>
fatally for itself, interposed the objection of the door and window<br>
being so situated, that it was scarce possible to get out, and make my<br>
way along the bank to the house, without his seeing me: which I could<br>
not bear the thought of, so much ashamed and confounded was I at having<br>
seen him. Condemned then to stay till his departure should release me,<br>
I was greatly embarrassed how to dispose of myself: I kept some time<br>
betwixt terror and modesty, even from looking through the window, which<br>
being an old fashioned casement, without any light behind me, could<br>
hardly betray any one&rsquo;s being there to him from within; then the door<br>
was so secure, that without violence, or my own consent, there was no<br>
opening it from without.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;But now, by my own experience, I found it too true, that objects which<br>
affright us, when we cannot get from them, draw our eyes as forcibly as<br>
those that please us. I could not long withstand that nameless impulse,<br>
which, without any desire of this novel sight, compelled me towards it;<br>
emboldened too by my certainty of being at once unseen and safe, I<br>
ventured by degrees to cast my eyes on an object so terrible and<br>
alarming to my virgin modesty as a naked man.<br>
<br>
&ldquo;But as I snatched a look, the first gleam that struck me, was in<br>
general the dewy lustre of the whitest skin imaginable, which the sun<br>
playing upon made the reflection of it perfectly beamy. His face, in<br>
the confusion I was in, I could not well distinguish the lineamints of,<br>
any farther than that there was a great deal of youth and freshness in<br>
it. The frolic and various play of all his fine polished limbs, as they<br>
appeared above the surface, in the course of his swimming or wantoning<br>
with the water, amused and insensibly delighted me; sometimes he lay<br>
motionless, on his back, waterborne, and dragging after him a fine head<br>
of hair, that, floating, swept the stream in a bush of black curls.<br>
Then the overflowing water would make a separation between his breast<br>
and glossy white belly; at the bottom of which I could not escape<br>
observing so remarkable a distinction, as a black mossy tuft, out of<br>
which appeared to emerge a round, softish, limber, white something,<br>
that played every way, with ever the least motion or whirling eddy. I<br>
cannot say but that part chiefly, by a kind of natural instinct,<br>
attracted, detained, captivated my attention: it was out of the power<br>
of all my modesty to command my eye away from it; and seeing nothing so<br>
very dreadful in its appearance, I insensibly looked away all my fears:<br>
but as fast as they gave way, new desires and strange wishes took<br>
place, and I melted as I gazed. The fire of nature, that had so long<br>
lain dormant or concealed, began to break out, and made me feel my sex<br>
for the first time. He had now changed his posture, and swam prone on<br>
his belly, striking out with his legs and arms; finer modeled than<br>
which could not have been cast, whilst his floating locks played over a<br>
