balsamic injection, as softened and unedged all those irritating stings<br>
of a new species of titillation, which I had been so intolerably<br>
maddened with, and restored the ferment of my senses to some degree of<br>
composure.<br>
<br>
I had now achieved this rare adventure ultimately much more to my<br>
satisfaction than I had bespoken the nature of it to turn out; nor was<br>
it much lessened, you may think, by spark&rsquo;s lavish praises of my<br>
constancy and complaisance, which he gave weight to by a present that<br>
greatly surpassed my utmost expectation, besides his gratification to<br>
Mrs. Cole.<br>
<br>
I was not, however, at any time re-enticed to renew with him, or resort<br>
again to the violent expedient of lashing nature into more haste than<br>
good speed: which, by the way, I conceive acts somewhat in the manner<br>
of a dose of Spanish flies; with more pain perhaps, but less danger;<br>
and might be necessary to him, but was nothing less so than to me,<br>
whose appetite wanted the bridle more than the spur.<br>
<br>
Mrs. Cole, to whom this adventurous exploit had more and more endeared<br>
me, looked on me now as a girl after her own heart, afraid of nothing,<br>
and, on a good account, hardly enough to fight all the weapons of<br>
pleasure through. Attentive then, in consequence of these favourable<br>
conceptions, to promote either my profit or pleasure, she had special<br>
regard for the first, in a new gallant of a very singular turn, that<br>
she procured for and introduced to me.<br>
<br>
This was a grave staid, solemn, elderly gentleman, whose peculiar<br>
humour was a delight in combing fine tresses of hair; and as I was<br>
perfectly headed to his taste, he used to come constantly at my toilet<br>
hours, when I let down my hair as loose as nature, and abandoned it to<br>
him to do what he pleased with it; and accordingly he would keep me an<br>
hour or more in play with it, drawing the comb through it, winding the<br>
curls round his fingers, even kissing it as he smoothed it; and all<br>
this led to no other use of my person, or any other liberties whatever,<br>
any more than if a distinction of sexes had not existed.<br>
<br>
Another peculiarity of taste he had, which was to present me with a<br>
dozen pairs of the whitest kid gloves at a time: these he would divert<br>
himself with drawing on me, and then biting off their finger ends; all<br>
which fooleries of a silly appetite, the old gentleman paid more<br>
liberally for, than most others did for more essential favours. This<br>
lasted till a violent cough, seizing and laying him up, delivered me<br>
from this most innocent and insipid trifler, for I never heard more of<br>
him after his first retreat.<br>
<br>
You may be sure a by-job of this sort interfered with no other pursuit,<br>
or plan of life; which I led, in truth, with a modesty and reserve that<br>
was less the work of virtue than of exhausted novelty, a glut of<br>
pleasure, and easy circumstances, that made me indifferent to any<br>
engagements in which pleasure and profit were not eminently united; and<br>
such I could, with the less impatience, wait for at the hands of time<br>
and fortune, as I was satisfied I could never mend my pennyworths,<br>
having evidently been served at the top of the market, and even been<br>
pampered with dainties: besides that, in the sacrifice of a few<br>
momentary impulses, I found a secret satisfaction in respecting myself,<br>
as well as preserving the life and freshness of my complexion. Louisa<br>
and Emily did not carry indeed their reserve so high as I did; but<br>
still they were far from cheap or abandoned, though two of their<br>
adventures seemed to contradict this general character, which, for<br>
their singularity, I shall give you in course, beginning first with<br>
Emily&rsquo;s:<br>
<br>
Louisa and she went one night to a ball, the first in the habit of a<br>
shepherdess, Emily in that of a shepherd: I saw them in their dresses<br>
before they went, and nothing in nature could represent a prettier boy<br>
than this last did, being so fair and well limbed. They had kept<br>
together for some time, when Louisa, meeting an old acquaintance of<br>
hers, very cordially gives her companion the slip, and leaves her under<br>
the protection of her boy&rsquo;s habit, which was not much, and of her<br>
discretion, which was, it seems, still less. Emily, finding herself<br>
deserted, sauntered thoughtless about a while, and, as much for<br>
coolness and air as any thing else, at length pulled off her mask and<br>
went to the sideboard; where, eyed and marked out by a gentleman in a<br>
very handsome domino, she was accosted by, and fell into chat with him.<br>
The domino, after a little discourse, in which Emily doubtless<br>
distinguished her good nature and easiness more than her wit, began to<br>
make violent love to her, and drawing her insensibly to some benches at<br>
the lower end of the masquerade room, got her to sit by him, where he<br>
squeezed her hands, pinched her cheeks, praised and played with her<br>
fine hair, admired her complexion, and all in a style of courtship<br>
dashed with a certain oddity, that not comprehending the mystery of,<br>
poor Emily attributed to his falling in with the humour of her<br>
disguise; and being naturally not the cruellest of her profession,<br>
began to incline to a parley on those essentials. But here was the<br>
stress of the joke: he took her really for what she appeared to be, a<br>
smock-faced boy; and she, forgetting her dress, and of course ranging<br>
quite wide of his ideas, took all those address to be paid to herself<br>
as a woman, which she precisely owed to his not thinking her one.<br>
However, this double error was pushed to such a height on both sides,<br>
that Emily, who saw nothing in him but a gentleman of distinction by<br>
those points of dress to which his disguise did not extend, warmed too<br>
by the wine he had plyed her with, and the caresses he had lavished<br>
upon her, suffered herself to be persuaded to go to a bagnio with him;<br>
and thus, losing sight of Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s cautions, with a blind<br>
confidence, put herself into his hands, to be carried wherever he<br>
pleased. For his part, equally blinded by his wishes, whilst here<br>
gregious simplicity favoured his deception more than the most exquisite<br>
art could have done, he supposed, no doubt, that he had lighted on some<br>
soft simpleton, fit for his; purpose, or some kept minion broken to his<br>
hand, who understood him perfectly well, and entered into his designs.<br>
But, be that as it would, he led her to a coach, went into it with her,<br>
and brought her to a very handsome apartment, with a bed in it; but<br>
whether it was a bagnio or not, she could not tell, having spoken to<br>
nobody but himself. But when they were alone together, and her<br>
inamorato began to proceed to those extremities which instantly<br>
discover the sex, she remarked, that no description could paint up to<br>
the life, the mixture of pique, confusion and disappointment, that<br>
appeared in his countenance, joined to the mournful exclamation: &ldquo;By<br>
heavens, a woman!&rdquo; This at once opened her eyes, which had been shut in<br>
downright stupidity. However, as if he had meant to retrieve that<br>
escape, he still continued to toy with and fondle her, but with so<br>
staring an alteration from extreme warmth into a chill and forced<br>
civility, that even Emily herself could not but take notice of it, and<br>
now began to wish she had paid more regard to Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s premonitions<br>
against ever engaging with a stranger. And now an excess of timidity<br>
succeeded to an excess of confidence, and she thought herself so much<br>
at his mercy and discretion, that she stood passive throughout the<br>
whole progress of his prelude: for now, whether the impressions of so<br>
great a beauty had even made him forgive her sex, or whether her<br>
appearance or figure in that dress still humoured his first illusion,<br>
he recovered by degrees a good part of his first warmth, and keeping<br>
Emily with her breeches still unbuttoned, stript them down to her<br>
knees, and gently impelling her to lean down, with her face against the<br>
bed-side, placed her so, that the double way, between the double rising<br>
behind, presented the choice fair to him, and he was so fairly set on a<br>
mis-direction, as to give the girl no small alarms for fear of losing a<br>
maidenhead she had not dreamt of. However, her complaints, and a<br>
resistance, gentle, but firm, checked and brought him to himself again;<br>
so that turning his steed&rsquo;s head, he drove him at length in the right<br>
road, in which his imagination having probably made the most of those<br>
resemblances that flattered his taste, he got, with much ado, to his<br>
journey&rsquo;s end: after which, he led her out himself, and walking with<br>
her two or three streets length, got her a chair, when making her a<br>
present not any thing inferior to what she could have expected, he left<br>
her, well recommended to the chairmen, who, on her directions, brought<br>
her home.<br>
<br>
This she related to Mrs. Cole and me the same morning, not without the<br>
visible remains of the fear and confusion she had been in, still<br>
stamped on her countenance. Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s remark was, that her<br>
indiscretion proceeding from a constitutional facility, there were<br>
little hopes of any thing curing her of it, but repeated severe<br>
experience. Mine was, that I could not conceive how it was possible for<br>
mankind to run into a taste, not only universally odious, but absurd,<br>
and impossible to gratify; since, according to the notions and<br>
experience I had of things, it was not in nature to force such immense<br>
disproportions. Mrs. Cole only smiled at my ignorance, and said nothing<br>
towards my undeception, which was not affected but by ocular<br>
demonstration, some months after, which a most singular accident<br>
furnished me, and which I will here set down, that I may not return<br>
again to so disagreeable a subject.<br>
<br>
I had, on a visit intended to Harriet, who had taken lodgings at<br>
Hampton-court, hired a chariot to go out thither, Mrs. Cole having,<br>
promised to accompany me; but some indispensable business intervening,<br>
to detain her, I was obliged to set out alone; and scarce had I got a<br>
third of my way, before the axle-tree broke down, and I was well off to<br>
get out, safe and unhurt, into a public-house, of a tolerable handsome<br>
appearance, on the road. Here the people told me that the stage would<br>
come by in a couple of hours at farthest, upon; which, determining to<br>
wait for it, sooner than lose the jaunt I had got so far forward on, I<br>
was carried into a very clean decent room, up one pair of stairs, which<br>
I took possession of for the time I had to stay, in right of calling<br>
for sufficient to do the house justice.<br>
<br>
Here, whilst I was amusing myself with looking out of the window, a<br>
single horse-chaise stopt at the door, out of which lightly leaped two<br>
young&rsquo; gentlemen, for so they seemed, who came in only as it were to<br>
bait and refresh a little, for they gave their horse to be held in<br>
readiness against they came out. And presently I heard the door of the<br>
next room, where they were let in, and called about them briskly; and<br>
as soon as they were served, I could just hear that they shut and<br>
fastened the door on the inside.<br>
<br>
A spirit of curiosity, far from sudden, since I do not know when I was<br>
without it, prompted me, without any particular suspicion, or other<br>
drift or view, to see what they were, and examine their persons and<br>
behaviour. The partition of our rooms was one of those moveable ones<br>
that, when taken down, served occasionally to lay them into one, for<br>
the conveniency of as larger company; and now, my nicest search could<br>
not shew me the shadow of a peep-hole, a circumstance which probably<br>
had not escaped the review of the parties on the other side, whom much<br>
it stood upon not to be deceived in it; but at length I observed a<br>
paper patch of the same colour as the wainscot, which I took to conceal<br>
some flaw; but then it was so high, that I was obliged to stand upon a<br>
chair to reach it, which I did as soft as possible, and, with a point<br>
of a bodkin, soon pierced it, and opened myself espial room sufficient.<br>
And now, applying my eye close, I commanded the room perfectly, and<br>
could see my two young sparks romping and pulling one another about,<br>
entirely, to my imagination, in frolic and innocent play.<br>
<br>
The eldest might be, on my nearest guess, towards nineteen, a tall<br>
comely young man, in a white fustian frock, with a green velvet cape,<br>
and cut bob-wig.<br>
<br>
The youngest could not be above seventeen, fair, ruddy, completely well<br>
made, and to say the truth, a sweet pretty stripling: he was too, I<br>
fancy, a country lad, by his dress, which was a green plush frock, and<br>
breeches of the same, white waistcoat and stockings, a jockey cap, with<br>
his yellowish hair, long and loose, in natural curls.<br>
<br>
But after a look of circumspection, which I saw the eldest cast every<br>
way round the room, probably in too much hurry and heat not to overlook<br>
the very small opening I was posted at, especially at the height it<br>
was, whilst my eye close to it kept the light from shining through and<br>
betraying it, he said something to his companion that presently changed<br>
the face of things.<br>
<br>
For now the elder began to embrace, to press and kiss the younger, to<br>
put his hands into his bosom, and give him such manifest signs of an<br>
amorous intention, as made me conclude the other to be a girl in<br>
disguise: a mistake that nature kept me in countenance for, for she had<br>
certainly made one, when she gave him the male stamp.<br>
<br>
In the rashness then of their age, and bent as they were to accomplish<br>
their project of preposterous pleasure, at the risk of the very worst<br>
of consequences, where a discovery was nothing less than improbable,<br>
they now proceeded to such lengths as soon satisfied me what they were.<br>
<br>
For presently the eldest unbuttoned the other&rsquo;s breeches, and removing<br>
the linen barrier, brought out to view a white shaft, middle sized, and<br>
scarce fledged, when after handling and playing with it a little, with<br>
other dalliance, all received by the boy without other opposition than<br>
certain wayward coyness, ten times-more alluring than repulsive, he got<br>
him so turned round, with his face from him, to a chair that stood hard<br>
by; when knowing, I suppose, his office, the Ganymede now obsequiously<br>
leaned his head against the back of it, and projecting his body, made a<br>
fair mark, still covered with his shirt. As he thus stood in a side<br>
view to me, but fronting his companion, who, presently unmasking his<br>
battery, produced an engine that certainly deserved to be put to a<br>
better use, and very fit to confirm me in my disbelief of the<br>
possibility of things; being pushed to odious extremities, which I had<br>
built on the disproportion of parts; but this disbelief I was now cured<br>
of, as by my consent all young men should likewise be, that their<br>
innocence may not be betrayed into such snares, for want of knowing the<br>
extent of their danger: for nothing is more certain than that ignorance<br>
of advice is by no means a guard against it.<br>
<br>
Slipping, then, aside the young lad&rsquo;s shirt, and tucking it up under<br>
his clothes behind, he shewed to the open air those globular fleshy<br>
eminences that compose the Mount Peasants of Rome, and which now, with<br>
all the narrow vale that intersects them, stood displayed and exposed<br>
to his attack; nor could I without a shudder behold the dispositions he<br>
made for it. First, then, moistening well with spittle his instrument,<br>
obviously to make it glib, he pointed, he introduced it, as I could<br>
plainly discern, not only from its direction and my losing sight of it,<br>
but by the writhing, twisting and soft murmured complaints of the young<br>
sufferer; but at length, the first straits of entrance being pretty<br>
well go through, every thing seemed to move and go pretty currently on,<br>
as on a carpet road, without much rub or resistance; and now, passing<br>
one hand round his minions&rsquo; hips, he got hold of his red-topped ivory<br>
toy, that stood perfectly stiff, and shewed, that if he was like his<br>
mother behind, he was like his father before; this he diverted himself<br>
with, whilst, with the other he wantoned with his hair, and leaning<br>
forward over his back, drew his face, from which the boy shook the<br>
loose curls that fell over it, in the posture he stood him in, and<br>
brought him towards his, so as to receive a long breathed kiss; after<br>
which, renewing his driving, and thus continuing to harass his rear,<br>
the height of the fist came on with its usual symptoms, and dismissed<br>
the action.<br>
<br>
The criminal scene they acted, I had the patience to see to an end,<br>
purely that I might gather more facts and certainty against them in my<br>
design to do their deserts instant justice; and accordingly, when they<br>
had re-adjusted themselves; and were preparing to go out, burning as I<br>
was with rage and indignation, I jumped down from the chair, in order<br>
to raise the house upon them, but with such an unlucky impetuosity,<br>
that some nail or ruggedness in the floor caught my foot, and flung me<br>
on my face with such violence, that I fell senseless on the ground, and<br>
lay there some time before any one came to my relief: so that they,<br>
alarmed, I suppose, by the noise of my fall, had more than the<br>
necessary time to make a safe retreat. This they effected, as I learnt,<br>
with a precipitation nobody could account for, until, when come to<br>
myself, and composed enough to speak, I acquainted those of the house<br>
with the whole transaction I had been evidence to.<br>
<br>
When I came home again, and told Mrs. Cole this adventure, she very<br>
sensibly observed to me, that &ldquo;there was no doubt of the due vengeance<br>
one time or other overtaking these miscreants, however they might<br>
escape for the present; and that, had I been the temporal instrument of<br>
it, I should have been put to a great deal more trouble and confusion<br>
than I imagined; that, as to the thing itself, the less said of it was<br>
the better; but that though she might be suspected of partiality, from<br>
its being the common cause of womankind, out of whose mouths this<br>
practice tended to take something more than bread, yet she protested<br>
against any mixture of passion, with a declaration extorted from her by<br>
pure regard to truth; which was, that whatever effect this infamous<br>
passion had in other ages and other countries, it seemed a peculiar<br>
blessing on our air and climate, that there was a plaguespot visibly<br>
imprinted on all that are tainted with it, in this nation at least, for<br>
that among numbers of that stamp whom she had known, or at least were<br>
universally under the scandalous suspicion of it, she would not name an<br>
exception hardly to one of them, whose character was not, in all other<br>
respects, the most worthless and despicable that could be; stript of<br>
all the manly virtues of their own sex, and filled up with only the<br>
worst vices and follies of ours; that, in fine, they were scarce less<br>
execrable than ridiculous in their monstrous inconsistence, of loathing<br>
and contemning women, and at the same time apeing all their manners,<br>
airs, lisps, scuttle, and, in general, all their little modes of<br>
affectation, which become them at least better, than they do these<br>
unsexed, male misses.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
But here, washing my hands of them, I re-plunge into the stream of my<br>
history, which I may very properly ingraft a terrible sally of<br>
Louisa&rsquo;s, since I had some share in it myself, and have besides engaged<br>
myself to relate it, in point of countenance to poor Emily. It will<br>
add, too, one more example to thousands, in confirmation of the maxim,<br>
that women get once out of compass, there are no lengths of<br>
licentiousness, that they are not capable of running.<br>
<br>
One morning then, that both Mrs. Cole and Emily were gone out for the<br>
day, and only Louisa and I (not to mention the house-maid) were left in<br>
charge of the house, whilst we were loitering away the time, in looking<br>
through the shop windows, the son of a poor woman, who earned very hard<br>
bread indeed by mending of stockings, in a stall in the neighbourhood,<br>
offered us some nosegays, ranged round a small basket; by selling of<br>
which the poor boy eked out his mother&rsquo;s maintenance of them both: nor<br>
was he fit for any other way of livelihood, since he was not only a<br>
perfect changeling, or idiot, but stammered so that there was no<br>
understanding even those sounds his half-dozen animals ideas, at most,<br>
prompted him to utter.<br>
<br>
The boys and servants in the neighbourhood had given him the nick-name<br>
of good-natured Dick, from the soft simpleton&rsquo;s doing every thing he<br>
was bid at the first word, and from his naturally having no turn to<br>
mischief; then, by the way, he was perfectly well made, stout,<br>
clean-limbed, tall of his age, as strong as a horse, and, withal,<br>
pretty featured; so that he was not, absolutely, such a figure to be<br>
snuffled at neither, if your nicety could, in favour of such<br>
essentials, have dispensed with a face unwashed, hair tangled for want<br>
of combing, and so ragged a plight, that he might have disputed points<br>
of shew with any heathen philosopher of them all.<br>
<br>
This boy we had often seen, and bought his flowers, out of pure<br>
compassion, and nothing more; but just at this time as he stood<br>
presenting us his basket, a sudden whim, a start of wayward fancy,<br>
seized Louisa; and, without consulting me, she calls him in, and<br>
beginning to examine his nosegays, culls out two, one for herself,<br>
another for me, and pulling out half a crown, very currently gives it<br>
him to change, as if she had really expected he could have changed it:<br>
but the boy, scratching his head, made his signs explain his inability<br>
in place of words, which he could not, with all his struggles,<br>
articulate.<br>
<br>
Louisa, at this, says: &ldquo;Well, my lad, come up stairs with me, and I<br>
will give you your due,&rdquo; winking at the same time to me, and beckoning<br>
me to accompany her, which I did, securing first the street-door, that<br>
by this means, together with the shop, became wholly the care of the<br>
faithful house-maid.<br>
<br>
As we went up, Louisa whispered me &ldquo;that she had conceived a strange<br>
longing to be satisfied, whether the general rule held good with regard<br>
to this changeling, and how far nature had made him amends, in her best<br>
bodily gifts, for her denial of the sublimer intellectual ones; begin,<br>
at the same time, my assistance in procuring her this satisfaction.&rdquo; A<br>
want of complaisance was never my vice, and I was so far from opposing<br>
this extravagant frolic, that now, bit with the same maggot, and my<br>
curiosity conspiring with hers, I entered plump into it, on my own<br>
account.<br>
<br>
Consequently, soon as we came into Louisa&rsquo;s bed-chamber, whilst she was<br>
amusing him with picking out his nosegays, I undertook the lead, and<br>
began the attack. As it was not then very material to keep much<br>
measures with a mere natural, I made presently free with him, though at<br>
my first motion of meddling, his surprise and confusion made him<br>
receive my advances but awkwardly: nay, insomuch that he bashfully<br>
shied, and shied back a little; till encouraging him with my eyes,<br>
plucking him playfully by the hair, sleeking his cheeks, and forwarding<br>
my point by a number of little wantonnesses, I soon turned him<br>
familiar, and gave nature her sweetest alarm: so that aroused, and<br>
beginning to feel himself, we could, amidst all the innocent laugh and<br>
grin I had provoked him into, perceive the fire lighting in his eyes,<br>
and, diffusing over his cheeks, blend its glow with that of his<br>
blushes. The emotion in short of animal pleasure glared distinctly in<br>
the simpleton&rsquo;s countenance; yet struck with the novelty of the scene,<br>
he did not know which way to look or move; but tame, passive,<br>
simpering, with his mouth half open, in stupid rapture, stood and<br>
tractably suffered me to do what I pleased with him. His basket was<br>
dropt out of his hands, which Louisa took care of.<br>
<br>
I had now, through more than one rent, discovered and felt his thighs,<br>
the skin of which seemed the smoother and fairer for the coarseness,<br>
and even the dirt of his dress, as the teeth of negroes seem the whiter<br>
for the surrounded black; and poor indeed of habit, poor of<br>
understanding, he was, however, abundantly rich in personal treasures,<br>
such as flesh, firm, plump, and replete with the juices of youth, and<br>
robust well-knit limbs. My fingers too had now got within reach of the<br>
true, the genuine sensitive plant, which, instead of shrinking from the<br>
touch, joys to meet it, and swells and vegetates under it: mine<br>
pleasingly informed me that matters were so ripe for the discovery we<br>
meditated, that they were too mighty for the confinement they were<br>
ready to break. A waistband that I unskewered, and a rag of a shirt<br>
that I removed, and which could not have covered a quarter of it,<br>
revealed the whole of the idiot&rsquo;s standard of distinction, erect, in<br>
full pride and display: but such a one! it was positively of so<br>
tremendous a size, that prepared as we were to see something<br>
extraordinary, it still, out of measure, surpassed our expectation, and<br>
astonished even me, who had not been used to trade in trifles. In fine,<br>
it might have answered very well the making a skew of; its enormous<br>
head seemed, in hue and size, not unlike a common sheep&rsquo;s heart; then<br>
you might have trolled dice securely along the broad back of the body<br>
of it; the length of it too was prodigious; then the rich appendage of<br>
the treasure-bag beneath, large in proportion, gathered and crisped up<br>
round in shallow furrows, helped to fill the eye, and complete the<br>
proof of his being a natural, not quite in vain; since it was full<br>
manifest that he inherited, and largely too, the prerogative of majesty<br>
which distinguishes that otherwise most unfortunate condition, and gave<br>
rise to the vulgar saying &ldquo;That a fool&rsquo;s bauble is a lady&rsquo;s<br>
playfellow.&rdquo; Not wholly without reason: for, generally speaking, it is<br>
in love as it is in war, where the longest weapon carries it. Nature,<br>
in short, had done so much for him in those parts, that she perhaps<br>
held herself acquitted in doing so little for his head.<br>
<br>
For my part, who had sincerely no intention to push the joke further<br>
than simply satisfying my curiosity with the sight of it alone, I was<br>
content, in spite of the temptation that stared me in the face, with<br>
having raised a May-pole for another to hang a garland on: for, by this<br>
time, easily reading Louisa&rsquo;s desires in her wishful eyes, I acted the<br>
commodious part, and made her, who sought no better sport, significant<br>
terms of encouragement to go through stitch with her adventure;<br>
intimating too that I would stay and see fair play: in which, indeed, I<br>
had in view to humour a new born curiosity, to observe what appearances<br>
active nature would put on in a natural, in the course of this her<br>
darling operation.<br>
<br>
Louisa, whose appetite was up, and who, like the industrious bee, was,<br>
it seems, not above gathering the sweet of so rare a flower, though she<br>
found it planted on a dunghill, was but too readily disposed to take<br>
the benefit of my cession. Urged then strongly by her own desires, and<br>
emboldened by me, she presently determined to risk a trial of parts<br>
with the idiot, who was by this time nobly inflamed for her purpose, by<br>
all the irritation we had used to put the principles of pleasure<br>
effectually into motion, and to wind up the springs of its organ to<br>
their supreme pitch; and it stood accordingly stiff and straining,<br>
ready to burst with the blood and spirits that swelled it... to a bulk!<br>
No! I shall never forget it.<br>
<br>
Louisa then, taking and holding the fine handle that so invitingly<br>
offered itself, led the ductile youth, by that mastertool of his, as<br>
she stept backward towards the bed; which he joyfully gave way to,<br>
under the incitations of instinct, and palpably delivered up to the<br>
goad of desire.<br>
<br>
Stopped then by the bed, she took the fall she loved, and leaned to the<br>
most, gently backward upon it, still holding fast what she held, and<br>
taking care to give her clothes a convenient toss up, so that her<br>
thighs duly disclosed, and elevated, laid open all the outward prospect<br>
of the treasury of love: the rose-lipt overture presenting the cockpit<br>
so fair, that it was not in nature even for a natural to miss it. Nor<br>
did he: for Louisa, fully bent on grappling with it, and impatient of<br>
dalliance or delay, directed faithfully the point of the<br>
battering-piece, and bounded up with a rage of so voracious appetite,<br>
to meet and favour the thrust of insertion, that the fierce activity on<br>
both sides effected it with such pain of distention, that Louisa cried<br>
out violently, that she was hurt beyond bearing, that she was killed.<br>
But it was too late: the storm was up, and force was on her to give way<br>
to it; for now the man-machine, strongly worked upon by the sensual<br>
passion, felt so manfully his advantages and superiority, felt withal<br>
the sting of pleasure so intolerable, that maddening with it, his joys<br>
began to assume a character of furiousness, which made me tremble for<br>
the too tender Louisa. He seemed, at this juncture, greater than<br>
himself; his countenance, before so void of meaning, or expression, now<br>
grew big with the importance of the act he was upon. In short, it was<br>
not now that he was to be played the fool with. But, what is pleasant<br>
enough, I myself was awed into a sort of respect for him, by the comely<br>
terrors his motions dressed him in: his eyes shooting sparks of fire;<br>
his face glowing with ardours that gave another life to it; his teeth<br>
churning; his whole frame agitated with a raging ungovernable<br>
impetuosity: all sensibly betraying the formidable fierceness with<br>
which the genial instinct acted upon him. Butting then and goring all<br>
before him, and mad and wild like an ower-driven steer, he ploughs up<br>
the tender furrow all insensible to Louisa&rsquo;s complaints; nothing can<br>
stop, nothing can keep out a fury like his: with which, having once got<br>
its head in, its blind rage soon made way for the rest, piercing,<br>
rending, and breaking open all obstruction. The torn, split, wounded<br>
girl cries, struggles, invokes me to her rescue, and endeavours to get<br>
from under the young savage, or shake him off, but alas! in vain: her<br>
breath, might as soon have strength to have quelled his rough assault,<br>
or put him out of his course. And indeed, all her efforts and struggles<br>
were managed with such disorder, that they served rather to entangle,<br>
and fold her the faster in the twine of his boisterous arms; so that<br>
she was tied to the stake, and obliged to fight the match out, if she<br>
died for it. For his part, instinct-ridden as he was, the expressions<br>
of his animal passion, partaking something of ferocity, were rather<br>
worrying than kisses, intermixed with ravenous love-bites on her cheeks<br>
and necks, the prints of which did not wear out for some days after.<br>
<br>
Poor Louisa, however, bore up at length better than could have been<br>
expected: and though she suffered, and greatly too, yet, ever true to<br>
the good old cause, she suffered with pleasure and enjoyed her pain.<br>
And soon now, by dint of an enraged enforcement, the brute-machine,<br>
driven like a whirlwind, made all smoke again, and wedging its way up,<br>
to the utmost extremity, left her, in point of penetration, nothing to<br>
fear or to desire: and now,<br>
<br>
&ldquo;Gorged with the dearest morsel of the earth,&rdquo;<br>
(Shakespeare.)<br>
<br>
<br>
Louisa lay, pleased to the heart, pleased to her utmost capacity of<br>
being so, with every fibre in those parts, stretched almost to<br>
breaking, on a rack of joy, whilst the instrument of all this<br>
over-fullness searched her senses with its sweet excess, till the<br>
pleasure gained upon her so, its point stung her so home, that catching<br>
at length the rage from her furious driver and sharing the riot of his<br>
wild rapture, she went wholly out of her mind into that favourite part<br>
of her body, the whole intenseness of which was so fervously filled,<br>
and employed: there alone she existed, all lost in those delirious<br>
transports, those extasies of the senses, which her winking eyes, the<br>
brightened vermilion of her lips and cheeks, and sighs of pleasure<br>
deeply fetched, so pathetically expressed. In short, she was now as<br>
mere a machine as much wrought on, and had her motions as little at her<br>
own command, as the natural himself, who, thus broke in upon her, made<br>
her feel with a vengeance his tempestuous mettle he battered with;<br>
their active loins quivered again with the violence of their conflict,<br>
till the surge of pleasure, foaming and raging to a height, drew down<br>
the pearly shower that was, to allay this hurricane. The purely<br>
sensitive idiot then first shed those tears of joy that attend its last<br>
moments, not without an agony of delight, and even almost a roar of<br>
rapture, as the gush escaped him; so sensibly too for Louisa, that she<br>
kept him faithful company, going off, in consent, with the old<br>
symptoms: a delicious delirium, a tremendous convulsive shudder, and<br>
the critical dying: Oh! And now, on his getting off she lay<br>
pleasure-drenched, and regorging its essential sweets; but quite spent,<br>
and gasping for breath, without other sensation of life than in those<br>
exquisite vibrations that trembled still on the strings of delight;<br>
which had been too intensively touched, and which nature had so<br>
ravishingly stirred with, for the senses to be quickly at peace from.<br>
<br>
As for the changeling, whose curious engine had been thus successfully<br>
played off, his shift of countenance and gesture had even something<br>
droll, or rather tragi-comic in it: there was now an air of sad<br>
repining foolishness, superadded to his natural one of no meaning and<br>
idiotism, as he stood with his label of manhood, now lank, unstiffened,<br>
becalmed, and flapping against his thighs, down which it reached half<br>
way, terrible even in its fall, whilst under the dejection of spirit<br>
and flesh, which naturally followed his eyes, by turns, cast down<br>
towards his struck standard, or piteously lifted to Louisa, seemed to<br>
require at her hands what he had so sensibly parted from to her, and<br>
now ruefully missed. But the vigour of nature, soon returning,<br>
dissipated the blast of faintness which the common law of enjoyment had<br>
subjected him to; and now his basket re-became his main concern, which<br>
I looked for, and brought him, whilst Louisa restored his dress to its<br>
usual condition, and afterwards pleased him perhaps more by taking all<br>
his flowers off his hands, and paying him, at his rate, for them, than<br>
if she had embarrassed him by a present, that he would have been<br>
puzzled to account for, and might have put others on tracing the<br>
motives of.<br>
<br>
Whether she ever returned to the attack I know not, and, to say truth,<br>
I believe not. She had had her freak out, and had pretty plentifully<br>
drowned her curiosity in a glut of pleasure, which, as it happened, had<br>
no other consequence than that the lad, who retained only a confused<br>
memory of the transaction, would, when he saw her, forget her in favour<br>
of the next woman, tempted, on the report of his parts, to take him in.<br>
Louisa herself did not long outstay this adventure at Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s (to<br>
whom, by the bye, we took care not to boast of our exploit, till all<br>
fear of consequences were clearly over): for an occasion presenting<br>
itself of proving her passion for a young fellow, at the expense of her<br>
discretion, proceeding all in character, she packed up her toilet, at<br>
half a day&rsquo;s warning, and went with him abroad, since which I entirely<br>
lost sight of her, and it never fell in my way to hear what became of<br>
her.<br>
<br>
But a few days after she had left us, two very occasion, not to wrong<br>
our training at Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s, especially favourites, and free of her<br>
academy, easily obtained her consent for Emily&rsquo;s and my acceptance of a<br>
party of pleasure, at a little but agreeable house, belonging to one of<br>
them situated not far up the river Thames, on the Surrey side.<br>
<br>
Every thing being settled, and it being a fine summer day, but rather<br>
of the warmest, we set out after dinner, and got to our rendezvous<br>
about four in the afternoon; where, landing at the foot of a neat,<br>
joyous pavilion, Emily and I were handed into it by our esquires, and<br>
there drank tea with a cheerfulness and gaiety, that the beauty of the<br>
prospect, the serenity of the weather, and the tender politeness of our<br>
sprightly gallants, naturally led us into.<br>
<br>
After tea, and taking a turn in the garden, my particular, who was the<br>
master of the house, and had in no sense schemed this party of pleasure<br>
for a dry one, proposed to us, with that frankness which his<br>
familiarity at Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s entitled him to, as the weather was<br>
excessively hot, to bathe together, under a commodious shelter that he<br>
had prepared expressly for that purpose, in a creek of the river, with<br>
which a side-door of the pavilion immediately communicated, and where<br>
we might be sure of having our diversion out, safe from interruption,<br>
and with the utmost privacy.<br>
<br>
Emily, who never refused anything, and I, who ever delighted in<br>
bathing, and had no exception to the person who proposed it, or to<br>
those pleasure it was easy to guess it implied, took care, on this<br>
occasion, not to wrong our training at Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s, and agreed to it<br>
with as good a grace as we could. Upon which, without loss of time, we<br>
returned instantly to the pavilion, one door of which opened into a<br>
tent, pitched before it, that with its marquise, formed a pleasing<br>
defense again the sun, or the weather, and was besides as private as we<br>
could wish. The lining of it, embossed cloth, represented a wild forest<br>
foliage, from the top, down to the sides, which, in the same stuff,<br>
were figured with fluted pilasters, with their spaces between filled<br>
with flower vases, the whole having a pay effect croon the eye,<br>
wherever you turned it.<br>
<br>
Then it reached sufficiently into the water, yet contained convenient<br>
benches round it, on the dry ground, either to keep our clothes, or...,<br>
or..., in short for more uses than resting upon. There was a side-table<br>
too, loaded with sweetmeats, jellies, and other eatables, and bottles<br>
of wine and cordials, by way of occasional relief from any rawness, or<br>
chill of the water, or from any faintness from whatever cause; and in<br>
fact, my gallant, who understood _ch&egrave;re enti&ecirc;re_ perfectly, and who,<br>
for taste (even if you would not approve this specimen of it) might<br>
have been comptroller of pleasures to a Roman emperor, had left no<br>
requisite towards convenience or luxury unprovided.<br>
<br>
As soon as we had looked round this inviting spot, and every<br>
preliminary of privacy was duly settled, strip was the word: when the<br>
young gentlemen soon dispatched the undressing each his partner and<br>
reduced us to the naked confession of all those secrets of person which<br>
dress generally hides, and which the discovery of was, naturally<br>
speaking, not to our disadvantage. Our hands, indeed, mechanically<br>
carried towards the most interesting part of us, screened, at first,<br>
all from the tufted cliff downwards, till we took them away at their<br>
desire, and employed them in doing them the same office, of helping off<br>
with their clothes; in the process of which, there passed all the<br>
little wantonnesses and frolics that you may easily imagine.<br>
<br>
As for my spark, he was presently undressed, all to his shirt, the<br>
fore-lappet of which as he leaned languishingly on me, he smilingly<br>
pointed to me to observe, as it bellied out, or rose and fell,<br>
according to the unruly starts of the motion behind it; but it was soon<br>
fixed, for now taking off his shirt, and naked as a Cupid, he shewed it<br>
me at so upright a stand, as prepared me indeed for his application to<br>
me for instant ease; but, though the sight of its fine size was fit<br>
enough to fire me, the cooling air, as I stood in this state of nature,<br>
joined to the desire I had of bathing-first, enabled me to put him off,<br>
and tranquillize him, with the remark, that a little suspense would<br>
only set a keener edge on the pleasure. Leading them the way, and<br>
shewing our friends an example of continency, which they were giving<br>
signs of losing respect to, we went hand in hand into the stream, till<br>
it took us up to our necks, where the no more than grateful coolness of<br>
the water gave my senses a delicious refreshment from the sultriness of<br>
the season, and made more alive, more happy in myself, and, in course,<br>
more alert, and open to voluptuous impressions.<br>
<br>
Here I laved and wantoned with the water, or sportively played with my<br>
companion, leaving Emily to deal with hers at discretion. Mine, at<br>
length, not content with making me take the plunge over head and ears,<br>
kept splashing me, and provoking me with all the little playful tricks<br>
he could devise, and which I strove not to remain in his debt for. We<br>
gave, in short, a loose to mirth; and now, nothing would serve him but<br>
giving his hand the regale of going over every part of me, neck,<br>
breast, belly, thighs, and all the _et c&aelig;tera_, so dear to the<br>
imagination, under the pretext of washing and rubbing them; as we both<br>
stood in the water, no higher now than the pit of our stomachs, and<br>
which did not hinder him from feeling, and toying with that leak that<br>
distinguishes our sex, and it so wonderfully water-tight: for his<br>
fingers, in vain dilating and opening it, only let more flame than<br>
water into it, be it said without a figure. At the same time he made me<br>
feel his own engine, which was so well wound up, as to stand even the<br>
working in water, and he accordingly threw one arm round my neck, and<br>
was endeavouring to get the better of that harsher construction bred by<br>
the surrounding fluid; and had in effect one his way so far as to make<br>
me sensible of the pleasing stretch of those nether lips, from the<br>
in-driving machine; when, independent of my not liking that awkward<br>
mode of enjoyment, I could not help interrupting him, in order to<br>
become joint spectators of a plan of joy, in hot operation between<br>
Emily and her partner; who impatient of the fooleries and dalliance of<br>
the bath, had led his nymph to one of the benches on the green bank,<br>
where he was very cordially proceeding to teach her the difference<br>
betwixt jest and earnest.<br>
<br>
There, setting her on his knee, and gliding one hand over the surface<br>
of that smooth polished snow-white skin of hers, which now doubly shone<br>
with a dew-bright lustre, and presented to the touch something like<br>
what one would imagine of animated ivory, especially in those<br>
ruby-nippled globes, which the touch is so fond of and delights to make<br>
love to, with the other he was lusciously exploring the sweet secret of<br>
nature, in order to make room for a stately piece of machinery, that<br>
stood up-reared, between her thighs, as she continued sitting on his<br>
lap, and pressed hard for instant intromission, which the tender Emily,<br>
in a fit of humour deliciously protracted, affected to decline, and<br>
elude the very pleasure she sighed for, but in a style of waywardness,<br>
so prettily put on, and managed, as to render it ten times more<br>
poignant; then her eyes, all amidst the softest dying languishment,<br>
expressed, ait once a mock denial and extreme desire, whilst her<br>
sweetness was zested with a coyness so pleasingly provoking, her moods<br>
of keeping him off were so attractive, that they redoubled the<br>
impetuous rage with, which, he covered her with kisses: and kisses<br>
that, whilst she seemed to shy from or scuffle for, the cunning wanton<br>
contrived such sly returns, of, as were, doubtless the sweeter for the<br>
gust she gave them, of being stolen ravished.<br>
<br>
Thus Emily, who knew no art but that which nature itself, in favour of<br>
her principal end, pleasure, had inspired her with, the art of<br>
yielding, coy&rsquo;d it indeed, but coy&rsquo;d it to the purpose; for with all<br>
her straining, her wrestling, and striving to break from the clasp of<br>
his arms, she was so far wiser yet than to mean it, that in her<br>
struggles, it was visible she aimed at nothing more than multiplying<br>
points of touch with him, and drawing yet closer the folds that held<br>
them every where entwined, like two tendrils of a vine intercurling:<br>
together: so that the same effect, as when Louisa strove in good<br>
earnest to disengage from the idiot, was now produced by different<br>
motives.<br>
<br>
Mean while, their emersion out of the cold water had caused a general<br>
glow, a tender suffusion of heightened carnation over their bodies;<br>
both equally white and smooth-skinned; so that as their limbs were thus<br>
amorously interwoven, in sweet confusion, it was scarce possible to<br>
distinguish who they respectively belonged to, but for the brawnier,<br>
bolder muscles of the stronger sex.<br>
<br>
In a little time, however, the champion was fairly in with her, and had<br>
tied at all points the true lover&rsquo;s knot; when now, adieu all the<br>
little refinements of a finessed reluctance; adieu the friendly feint!<br>
She was presently driven forcibly out of the power of using any art;<br>
and indeed, what art must not give way, when nature, corresponding with<br>
her assailant, invaded in the heart of her capital and carried by<br>
storm, lay at the mercy of the proud conqueror, who had made his entry<br>
triumphantly and completely? Soon, however, to become a tributary: for<br>
the engagement growing hotter and hotter, at close quarters, she<br>
presently brought him to the pass of paying down the dear debt to<br>
nature; which she had no sooner collected in, but, like a duellist who<br>
has laid his antagonist at his feet, when he has himself received a<br>
mortal wound, Emily had scarce time to plume herself upon her victory,<br>
but, shot with the same discharge, she, in a loud expiring sigh, in the<br>
closure of her eyes, the stretch-out of her limbs, and a remission of<br>
her whole frame, gave manifest signs that all was as it should be.<br>
<br>
For my part, who had not with the calmest patience stood in the water<br>
all this time, to view this warm action, I leaned tenderly on my<br>
gallant, and at the close of it, seemed to ask him with my eyes, what<br>
he thought of it; but he, more eager to satisfy me by his actions than<br>
by words or looks, as we shoaled the water towards the shore, showed me<br>
the staff of love so intensely set up, that had not even charity,<br>
beginning at home in this case, urged me to our mutual relief, it would<br>
have been cruel indeed to have suffered the youth to burst with<br>
straining, when the remedy was so obvious and so near at hand.<br>
<br>
Accordingly we took a bench, whilst Emily and her spark, who belonged<br>
it seems to the sea, stood at the side-board, drinking to our good<br>
voyage: for, as the last observed, we were well under weigh, with a<br>
fair wind up channel, and full-freighted; nor indeed were we long<br>
before we finished our trip to Cythera, and unloaded in the old haven;<br>
but, as the circumstances did not admit of much variation, I shall<br>
spare you the description.<br>
<br>
At the same time, allow me to place you here an excuse I am conscious<br>
of owing you, for having, perhaps, too much affected the figurative<br>
style; though surely, it can pass nowhere more allowable than in a<br>
subject which is so properly the province of poetry, nay, is poetry<br>
itself, pregnant with every flower of imagination and loving metaphors,<br>
even were not the natural expressions, for respects of fashion and<br>
sound, necessarily forbidden.<br>
<br>
Resuming now my history, you may please to know, that what with a<br>
competent number of repetitions, all in the same strain (and, by the<br>
bye, we have a certain natural sense that those repetitions are very<br>
much to the taste), what with a circle of pleasures delicately varied,<br>
there was not a moment lost to joy all the time we staid there, till<br>
late in the night we were re-escorted home by our esquires, who<br>
delivered us safe to Mrs. Cole, with generous thanks for our company.<br>
<br>
This too was Emily&rsquo;s last adventure in our way: for scarce a week<br>
after, she was, by an accident too trivial to detail to you the<br>
particulars, found out by her parents, who were in good circumstances,<br>
and who had been punished for their partiality to their son, in the<br>
loss of him, occasioned by a circumstance of their over indulgence to<br>
his appetite; upon which the so long engrossed stream of fondness,<br>
running violently in favour of this lost and inhumanly abandoned child<br>
whom if they had not neglected enquiry about, they might long before<br>
have recovered, they were now so over-joyed at the retrieval of her,<br>
that, I presume, it made them much less strict in examining the bottom<br>
of things: for they seemed very glad to take for granted, in the lump,<br>
every thing that the grave and decent Mrs. Cole was pleased to pass<br>
upon them; and soon afterwards sent her, from the country, handsome<br>
acknowledgment.<br>
<br>
But it was not so easy to replace to our community the loss of so sweet<br>
a member of it: for, not to mention her beauty, she was one of those<br>
mild, pliant characters, that if one does not entirely esteem, one can<br>
scarce help loving, which is not such a bad compensation neither. Owing<br>
all her weaknesses to good nature, and an indolent facility that kept<br>
her too much at the mercy of first impressions, she had just sense<br>
enough to know that she wanted leading strings, and thought herself so<br>
much obliged to any who would take the pains to think for her, and<br>
guide her, that with a very little management, she was capable of being<br>
made a most agreeable, nay a most virtuous wife: for vice, it is<br>
probable, had never been her choice, or her fate, if it had not been<br>
for occasion, or example, or had she not depended less upon herself<br>
than upon her circumstances. This presumption her conduct afterwards<br>
verified: for presently meeting with a match, that was ready cut and<br>
dry for her, with a neighbour&rsquo;s son of her own rank, and a young man of<br>
sense and order, who took as the widow of one lost at sea (for so it<br>
seems one of her gallants, whose name she had made free with, really<br>
was), she naturally struck into all the duties of her domestic life,<br>
with as much simplicity of affection, with as much constancy and<br>
regularity, as if she had never swerved from a state of undebauched<br>
innocence from her youth.<br>
<br>
These desertions had, however, now so far thinned Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s cluck<br>
that she was left with only me, like a hen with one chicken; but though<br>
she was earnestly entreated and encouraged to recruit her crops, her<br>
growing infirmities, and, above all, the tortures, of a stubborn hip<br>
gout, which she found would yield to no remedy, determined her to break<br>
up her business, and retire with a decent pittance into the country,<br>
where I promised myself, nothing so sure, as my going down to live with<br>
her, as soon as I had seen a little more of life, and improved my small<br>
matters into a competency that would create in me an independence on<br>
the world: for I was now, thanks to Mrs. Cole, wise enough to keep that<br>
essential in view.<br>
<br>
Thus was I then to lose my faithful preceptress, as did the<br>
philosophers of the town the white crow of her profession. For besides<br>
that she never ransacked her customers, whose tastes too she ever<br>
studiously consulted, she never racked her pupils with unconscionable<br>
extortions, nor ever put their hard earnings, as she called them, under<br>
the contribution of poundage. She was a severe enemy to the seduction<br>
for innocence, and confined her acquisitions solely to those<br>
unfortunate young women, who, having lost it, were but the juster<br>
objects of compassion: among these, indeed, she picked out such as<br>
suited her views and taking them under her protection, rescued them<br>
from the danger of the public sinks of ruin and misery, to place, or<br>
for them, well or ill, in the manner you have seen. Having then settled<br>
her affairs, she set out on her journey, after taking the most tender<br>
leave of me, and at the end of some excellent instructions,<br>
recommending me to myself, with an anxiety perfectly maternal. In<br>
short, she affected me so much, that I was not presently reconciled to<br>
myself for suffering her at any rate to go without me; but fate had, it<br>
seems, otherwise disposed of me.<br>
<br>
I had, on my separation from Mrs. Cole, taken a pleasant convenient<br>
house at Marylebone, but easy to rent and manage from its smallness,<br>
which I furnished neatly and modestly. There, with a reserve of eight<br>
hundred pounds, the fruit of my deference to Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s counsels,<br>
exclusive of clothes, some jewels, and some plate, I saw myself in<br>
purse for a long time, to wait without impatience for what the chapter<br>
of accidents might produce in my favour.<br>
<br>
Here, under the new character of a young gentlewoman whose husband was<br>
gone to sea, I had marked me out such lines of life and conduct, as<br>
leaving me a competent liberty to pursue my views either out of<br>
pleasure or fortune, bounded me nevertheless strictly within the rules<br>
of decency and discretion: a disposition, in which you cannot escape<br>
observing a true pupil of Mrs. Cole.<br>
<br>
I was scarce, however, well warm in my new abode, when going out one<br>
morning pretty early to enjoy the freshness of it, in the pleasing<br>
outlet of the fields, accompanied only by a maid, whom I had newly<br>
hired, as we were carelessly walking among the trees, we were alarmed<br>
with the noise of a violent coughing: turning our heads towards which,<br>
we distinguished a plain well dressed elderly gentleman, who, attacked<br>
with a sudden fit, was so much overcome, as to be forced to give way to<br>
it and sit down at the foot of a tree, where he seemed suffocating with<br>
the severity of it, being perfectly black in the face; not less moved<br>
than frightened with which, I flew on the instant to his relief, and<br>
using the rote of practice I had observed on the like occasion, I<br>
loosened his cravat and clapped him on the back; but whether to any<br>
purpose, or whether the cough had had its course, I know not, but the<br>
fit immediately went off; and now recovered to his speech and legs, he<br>
returned me thanks with as much emphasis as if I had saved his life.<br>
This naturally engaging a conversation, he acquainted me where he<br>
lived, which was at a considerable distance from where I met him, and<br>
where he had strayed insensibly on the same intention of a morning<br>
walk.<br>
<br>
He was, as I afterwards learned in the course of the intimacy which<br>
this little accident gave birth to, an old bachelor, turned of sixty,<br>
but of a fresh vigorous complexion, insomuch that he scarce marked five<br>
and forty, having never racked his constitution by permitting his<br>
desires to over-tax his ability.<br>
<br>
As to his birth and conditions, his parents, honest and failed<br>
mechanics, had, by the best traces he could get of them, left him an<br>
infant orphan on the parish; so that it was from a charity-school,<br>
that, by honesty and industry, he made his way into a merchant&rsquo;s<br>
counting house, from whence, being sent to a house in Cadiz, he there,<br>
by his talents and activity, acquired not only a fortune, but an<br>
immense one, with which he returned to his native country; where he<br>
could not, however, fish out so much as one single relation out of the<br>
obscurity he was born in. Taking then a taste for refinement, and<br>
pleased to enjoy life, like a mistress in the dark, he flowed his days<br>
in all the ease of opulence, without the least parade of it; and,<br>
rather studying the concealment than the shew of a fortune, looked down<br>
on a world he perfectly knew himself, to his wish, unknown and unmarked<br>
by.<br>
<br>
But, as I propose to devote a letter entirely to the pleasure of<br>
retracing to you all the particulars of my acquaintance with this ever,<br>
to me, memorable friend, I shall, in this, transiently touch on no more<br>
than may serve, as mortar, to cement, or form the connection of my<br>
history, and to obviate your surprise that one of my blood and relish<br>
of life, should count a gallant of three score such a catch.<br>
<br>
Referring then to a more explicit narrative, to explain by what<br>
progressions our acquaintance, certainly innocent at first, insensibly<br>
changed nature, and run into unplatonic length, as might well be<br>
expected from one of my condition of life, and above all, from that<br>
principle of electricity that scarce ever fails of producing fire when<br>
the sexes meet. I shall only here acquaint you, that as age had not<br>
subdued his tenderness for our sex, neither had it robbed him of the<br>
power of pleasing, since whatever he wanted in the bewitching charms of<br>
youth, he atoned for, or supplemented with the advantages of<br>
experience, the sweetness of his manners, and above all, his flattering<br>
address in touching the heart, by an application to the understanding.<br>
From him it was I first learned, to any purpose, and not without<br>
infinite pleasure, that I had such a portion of me worth bestowing some<br>
regard on; from him I received my first essential encouragement, and<br>
instructions how to put it in that train of cultivation, which I have<br>
since pushed to the little degree of improvement you see it at; he it<br>
was, who first taught me to be sensible that the pleasures of the mind<br>
were superior to those of the body; at the same time, that they were so<br>
far from obnoxious to, or, incompatible with each other, that, besides<br>
the sweetness in the variety and transition, the one served to exalt<br>
and perfect the taste of the other, to a degree that the senses alone<br>
can never arrive at.<br>
<br>
Himself a rational pleasurist; as being much too wise to be ashamed of<br>
the pleasures of humanity, loved me indeed, but loved me with dignity;<br>
in a mean equally removed from the sourness, of forwardness, by which<br>
age is unpleasingly characterized, and from that childish silly dotage<br>
that so often disgraces it, and which he himself used to turn into<br>
ridicule, and compare to an old goat affecting the frisk of a young<br>
kid.<br>
<br>
In short, every thing that is generally unamiable in his season of<br>
life, was, in him, repaired by so many advantages, that he existed a<br>
proof, manifest at least to me, that it is not out of the power of age<br>
to please, if it lays out to please, and if, making just allowance,<br>
those in that class do not forget, that if must cost them more pains<br>
and attention, than what youth, the natural spring-time of joy, stands<br>
in need of: as fruits out of season require proportionally more skill<br>
and cultivation, to force them.<br>
<br>
With this gentleman, who took me home soon after our acquaintance<br>
commenced, I lived near eight months in which time, my constant<br>
complaisance and docility, my attention to deserve his confidence and<br>
love, and a conduct, in general, devoid of the least art and founded on<br>
my sincere regard and esteem for him, won and attached him so firmly to<br>
me, that, after having generously trusted me with a genteel,<br>
independent settlement, proceeding to heap marks of affection on me, he<br>
appointed me, by an authentic will, his sole heiress and executrix: a<br>
disposition which he did not outlive two months, being taken from me by<br>
a violent cold that he contracted, as he unadvisedly ran to the window,<br>
on an alarm of fire at some streets distant, and stood there<br>
naked-breasted, and exposed to the fatal impressions of a damp night<br>
air.<br>
<br>
After acquitting myself of the duty towards my deceased benefactor, and<br>
paying him a tribute of un-feigned sorrow, which a little time changed<br>
into a most tender, graceful memory of him, which I shall ever retain,<br>
I grew somewhat comforted by the prospect that now opened to me, if not<br>
of happiness, at least of affluence and independence.<br>
<br>
I saw myself then in the full bloom and pride of youth (for I was not<br>
yet nineteen), actually at the head of so large a fortune, as it would<br>
have been even the height of impudence in me to have raised my wishes,<br>
much more my hopes to; and that this unexpected elevation did not turn<br>
my head, I owed to the pains my benefactor had taken to form and<br>
prepare me for it, as I owed his opinion of my management of the vast<br>
possessions he left me, to what he had observed of the prudential<br>
economy I had learned under Mrs. Cole, the reserve of which he saw I<br>
had made, was a proof and encouragement to him.<br>
<br>
But, alas! how easily in the enjoyment of the greatest sweets in life,<br>
in present possession, poisoned by the regret of an absent one! But my<br>
regret was a mighty and just one, since it had my only truly beloved<br>
Charles for its object.<br>
<br>
Given him up I had, indeed, completely, having never once heard from<br>
him since our separation; which, as I found afterwards, had been my<br>
misfortune, and not his neglect, for he wrote me several letters which<br>
had all miscarried; but forgotten him I never had. And amidst all my<br>
personal infidelities, not one had made a pin&rsquo;s point impression on a<br>
heart impenetrable to the true love passion, but for him.<br>
<br>
As soon, however, as I was mistress of this unexpected fortune, I felt<br>
more than ever how dear he was to me, from its insufficiency to make me<br>
happy, whilst he was not to share it with me. My earliest care,<br>
consequently, was to endeavour at getting some account of him; but all<br>
my researches produced me no more light, than that his father had been<br>
dead for some time, not so well as even with the world; and that<br>
Charles had reached his port of destination in the South Seas, where,<br>
finding the estate he was sent to recover, dwindled to a trifle, by the<br>
loss of two ships in which the bulk of his uncle&rsquo;s fortune lay, he was<br>
come away with the small remainder, and might, perhaps, according to<br>
the best advice, in a few months return to England, from whence he had,<br>
at the time of this my inquiry, been absent two years and seven months.<br>
A little eternity in love!<br>
<br>
You cannot conceive with what joy I embraced the hopes thus given me of<br>
seeing the delight of my heart again. But, as the term of months was<br>
assigned it, in order to divert and amuse my impatience for his return,<br>
after settling my affairs with much ease and security, I set out on a<br>
journey for Lancashire, with an equipage suitable to my fortune, and<br>
with a design purely to revisit my place of nativity, for which I could<br>
not help retaining a great tenderness; and might naturally not be sorry<br>
to shew myself there, to the advantage I was now in pass to do, after<br>
the report Esther Davis had spread of my being spirited away to the<br>
plantations; for on no other supposition could she account for the<br>
suppression of myself to her, since her leaving me so abruptly at the<br>
inn. Another favourite intention I had, to look out for my relations,<br>
though I had none but distant ones, and prove a benefactress to them.<br>
Then Mrs. Cole&rsquo;s place of retirement lying in my way, was not amongst<br>
the least of the pleasures I had proposed to myself in this expedition.<br>
<br>
I had taken nobody with me but a discreet decent woman, to figure it as<br>
my companion, besides my servants; and was scarce got into an inn,<br>
about twenty miles from London, where I was to sup and pass the night,<br>
when such a storm of wind and rain come on, as made me congratulate<br>
myself on having got under shelter before it began.<br>
<br>
This had continued a good half an hour, when bethinking me of some<br>
directions to be given to the coachman, I sent for him, not caring that<br>
his shoes should soil the very clean parlour, in which the cloth was<br>
laid, I stept into the hall kitchen, where he was, and where, whilst I<br>
was talking to him, I slantingly observed two horsemen driven in by the<br>
weather, and both wringing wet; one of whom was asking if they could<br>
not be assisted with a change, while their clothes were dried. But,<br>
heavens! who can express what I felt at the sound of a voice, ever<br>
present to my heart, and that it now rebounded at! or when pointing my<br>
eyes towards the person it came from, they confirmed its information,<br>
in spite of so long an absence, and of a dress one would have studied<br>
for a disguise: a horseman&rsquo;s great coat, with a stamp-up cape, and his<br>
hat flapped... but what could escape the alertness of a sense truly<br>
guided by love? A transport then like mine was above all consideration,<br>
or schemes of surprise; and I, that instant, with the rapidity of the<br>
emotions that I felt the spur of, shot into his arms, crying out, as I<br>
threw mine round his neck: &ldquo;My life!... my soul!... my Charles!..&rdquo; and<br>
without further power of speech, swooned away, under the pressing<br>
agitation of joy and surprise.<br>
<br>
Recovered out of my entrancement, I found myself in my charmer&rsquo;s arms,<br>
but in the parlour, surrounded by a crowd which this event had gathered<br>
round us, and which immediately, on a signal from the discreet<br>
landlady, who currently took him for my husband, cleared the room, and<br>
desirably left us alone to the raptures of this reunion; my joy at<br>
which had like to have proved, at the expense of my life, its power<br>
superior to that of grief at our fatal separation.<br>
<br>
The first object then, that my eyes opened on, was their supreme idol,<br>
and my supreme wish, Charles, on one knee, holding me fast by the hand<br>
and gazing on me with a transport of fondness. Observing my recovery,<br>
he attempted to speak, and give vent to his patience of hearing my<br>
voice again, to satisfy him once more that it was I; but the mightiness<br>
and suddenness of the surprise continuing to stun him, choked his<br>
utterance: he could only stammer out a few broken, half-formed,<br>
filtering accents, which my ears greedily drinking in, spelt, and put<br>
together, so as to make out their sense: &ldquo;After so long!... so cruel an<br>
absence!... my dearest Fanny!... can it?... can it be you?...&rdquo; stifling<br>
me at the time with kisses, that, stopping my opening mouth, at once<br>
prevented the answer that he panted for, and increased the delicious<br>
disorder in which all my senses were rapturously lost. However, amidst<br>
this crowd of ideas, and all blissful ones, there obtruded only one<br>
cruel doubt that poisoned nearly all the transcendant happiness: and<br>
what was it, but my dread of its being too excessive to be real? I<br>
trembled now with my fear of its being no more than a dream, and of<br>
waking out of it into the horrors of finding it one. Under this fond<br>
apprehension, imagining I could not make too much of the present<br>
prodigious joy, before it would vanish and leave me in the desert<br>
again, nor verify its reality too strongly, I clung to him, I clasped<br>
him, as if to hinder him from escaping me again: &ldquo;Where have you<br>
been?... how could you... could you leave me?... Say you are still<br>
mine... that you still love me... and thus! thus!&rdquo; (kissing him as if I<br>
would consolidated lips with him) &ldquo;I forgive you... forgive my hard<br>
fortune in favour of this restoration.&rdquo;<br>
<br>
All these interjections breaking from me, in that wildness of<br>
expression that justly passes for eloquence in love, drew from him all<br>
the returns my fond heart could wish or require. Our caresses, our<br>
questions, our answers, for some time observed no order; all crossing,<br>
or interrupting one another in sweet confusion, whilst we exchanged<br>
hearts at our eyes, and renewed the ratifications of a love unabated by<br>
time or absence: not a breath, not a motion, not a gesture on either<br>
side, but what was strongly impressed with it. Our hands, locked in<br>
each other, repeated the most passionate squeezes, so that their fiery<br>
thrill went to the heart again.<br>
<br>
Thus absorbed, and concentered in this unutterable delight, I had not<br>
attended to the sweet author of it being thoroughly wet, and in danger<br>
of catching cold; when, in good time, the landlady, whom the appearance<br>
of my equipage (which, bye the bye Charles knew nothing of) had gained<br>
me an interest in, for me and mine interrupted us by bringing in a<br>
decent shift of linen and clothes; which now, somewhat recovered into a<br>
calmer composure by the coming in of a third person, I pressed him to<br>
take the benefit of, with a tender concern and anxiety that made me<br>
tremble for his health.<br>
<br>
The landlady leaving us again, he proceeded to shift; in the act of<br>
which, though he proceeded with all that modesty which became these<br>
first solemner instants of our re-meeting, after so long an absence, I<br>
could not refrain certain snatches of my eyes, lured by the dazzling<br>
discoveries of his naked skin, that escaped him as he changed his<br>
linen, and which I could not observe the unfaded life and complexion of<br>
without emotions of tenderness and joy, that had himself too purely for<br>
their object, to partake of a loose or mis-timed desire.<br>
<br>
He was soon dressed in these temporary clothes, which neither fitted<br>
him, nor became the light my passion placed him in, to me at least;<br>
yet, as they were on him, they looked extremely well, in virtue of that<br>
magic charm which love put into every thing that he touched, or had<br>
relation to him: and where, indeed, was that dress that a figure like<br>
his would not give grace to? For now, as I eyed him more in detail, I<br>
could not but observe the even favourable alteration which the time of<br>
his absence had produced in his person.<br>
<br>
There were still the requisite lineaments, still the same vivid<br>
vermillion and bloom reigning in his face; but now the roses were more<br>
fully blown; the tan of his travels, and a beard somewhat more<br>
distinguishable, had, at the expense of no more delicacy than what he<br>
could well spare, given it an air of becoming manliness and maturity,<br>
that symmetrized nobly with that air of distinction and empire with<br>
which nature had stamped it, in a rare mixture with the sweetness of<br>
it; still nothing had he lost of that smooth plumpness of flesh, which,<br>
glowing with freshness, blooms florid to the eye, and delicious to the<br>
touch; then his shoulders were grown more square, his shape more<br>
formed, more portly, but still free and airy. In short, his figure<br>
showed riper, greater, and perfecter to the experienced eye, than in<br>
his tender youth; and now he was not much more than two and twenty.<br>
<br>
In this interval, however, I picked out of the broken, often pleasingly<br>
interrupted account of himself, that he was, at that instant, actually<br>
on his road to London, in not a very paramount plight or condition,<br>
having been wrecked on the Irish coast for which he had prematurely<br>
embarked, and lost the little all he had brought with him from the<br>
South Seas: so that he had not till after great shifts and hardships,<br>
in the company of his fellow-traveller, the captain, got so far on his<br>
journey; that so it was (having heard of his father&rsquo;s death and<br>
circumstances,) he had now the world to begin again, on a new account:<br>
a situation, which he assured me, in a vein of sincerity, that flowing<br>
from his heart, penetrated mine, gave him to farther pain, than that he<br>
had not his power to make me as happy as he could wish. My fortune, you<br>
will please to observe, I had not entered upon any overture of,<br>
reserving, to feast myself with the surprise of it to him, in calmer<br>
instants. And, as to my dress, it could give him no idea of the truth,<br>
not only as it was mourning, but likewise in a style of plainness and<br>
simplicity that I had ever kept to with studied art. He pressed me<br>
indeed tenderly to satisfy his ardent curiosity, both with regard to my<br>
past and present state of life, since his being torn away from me: but<br>
I found means to elude his questions, by answers that shewing his<br>
satisfaction at no great distance, won upon him to waive his<br>
impatience, in favour of the thorough confidence he had in my not<br>
delaying it, but for respect I should in good time acquaint him with.<br>
<br>
Charles, however, thus returned to my longing arms, tender, faithful,<br>
and in health, was already a blessing too mighty for my conception: but<br>
Charles in distress!... Charles reduced, and broken down to his naked<br>
personal merit, was such a circumstance, in favour of the sentiments I<br>
had for him, as exceeded my utmost desire; and accordingly I seemed so<br>
visibly charmed, so out of time and measure pleased at his mention of<br>
his ruined fortune, that he could account for it no way, but that the<br>
joy of seeing him again had swallowed up every other sense of concern.<br>
<br>
In the mean time, my woman had taken, all possible care of Charles&rsquo;s<br>
travelling companion; and as supper was coming in, he was introduced to<br>
me, when I received him as became my regard for all of Charles&rsquo;s<br>
acquaintance or friends.<br>
<br>
We four then supped together, in the style of joy, congratulation, and<br>
pleasing disorder that you may guess. For my part, though all these<br>
agitations had left me not the least stomach, but for that uncloying<br>
feast, the sight of my adored youth, I endeavoured to force it, by way<br>
of example for him, who I conjectured must want such a recruit after<br>
riding; and, indeed, he; ate like a traveller, but gazed at, and<br>
addressed me all the time like a lover.<br>
<br>
After the cloth was taken away, and the hour of repose came on, Charles<br>
and I were, without further ceremony, in quality of man and wife, shown<br>
up together to a very handsome apartment, and, all in course, the bed,<br>
they said, the best in the inn.<br>
<br>
And here, Decency, forgive me! if once more I violate thy laws and<br>
keeping the curtains undrawn, sacrifice thee for the last time to that<br>
confidence, without reserve, with which I engaged to recount to you the<br>
most striking circumstances of my youthful disorders.<br>
<br>
As soon, then, as we were in the room together, left to ourselves, the<br>
sight of the bed starving the remembrance of our first joys, and the<br>
thought of my being instantly to share it with the dear possessor of my<br>
virgin heart, moved me so strongly, that it was well I leaned upon him,<br>
or I must have fainted again under the overpowering sweet alarm.<br>
Charles saw into my confusion, and forgot his own, that was scarce<br>
less, to apply himself to the removal of mine.<br>
<br>
But now the true refining passion had regained throughout possession of<br>
me, with all its train of symptoms: a sweet sensibility, a tender<br>
timidity, love-sick yearnings tempered with diffidence and modesty, all<br>
held me in a subjection of soul, incomparably dearer to me than the<br>
liberty of heart which I had been long, too long! the mistress of, in<br>
the course of those grosser gallantries, the consciousness of which now<br>
made me sigh with a virtuous confusion and regret. No real virgin, in<br>
short, in view of the nuptial bed, could give more bashful blushes to<br>
unblemished innocence, than I did to a sense of guilt; and indeed I<br>
loved Charles too truly not to feel severely that I did not deserve<br>
him.<br>
<br>
As I kept hesitating and disconcerted under this soft distraction,<br>
Charles, with a fond impatience, took the pains to undress me; and all<br>
I can remember amidst the nutter and discomposure of my senses, was,<br>
some flattering exclamation of joy and admiration, more specially at<br>
the feel of my breasts, now set at liberty from my stays, and which<br>
panting and rising in tumultous throbs, swelled upon his dear touch,<br>
and gave it the welcome pleasure of finding them well formed, and<br>
un-failed in firmness.<br>
<br>
I was soon laid in bed, and scarce languished an instant for the<br>
darling partner of it, before he was undressed and got between the<br>
sheets, with his arms clasped round me, giving and taking, with gust<br>
inexpressible, a kiss of welcome, that my heart rising to my lips<br>
stamped with its warmest impression, concurring to my bliss, with that<br>
delicate and voluptuous emotion which Charles alone had the secret to<br>
excite, and which constitutes the very life, the essence of pleasure.<br>
<br>
Mean while, two candles lighted on a side-table near us, and a joyous<br>
wood fire, threw a light into the bed, that took from one sense, of<br>
great importance to our joys, all pretext for complaining of its being<br>
shut out of its share of them; and, indeed, the sight of my idolized<br>
youth was alone, from the ardour with which I had wished for it,<br>
without other circumstance, a pleasure to die of.<br>
<br>
But as action was now a necessity to desires so much on edge as ours,<br>
